46: 
47: PART I.
48: 
49: CHAPTER I.
50: 
51: 
52: In the days when the spinning-wheels hummed busily in the
53: farmhouses—and even great ladies, clothed in silk and thread-lace, had
54: their toy spinning-wheels of polished oak—there might be seen in
55: districts far away among the lanes, or deep in the bosom of the hills,
56: certain pallid undersized men, who, by the side of the brawny
57: country-folk, looked like the remnants of a disinherited race. The
58: shepherd’s dog barked fiercely when one of these alien-looking men
59: appeared on the upland, dark against the early winter sunset; for what
60: dog likes a figure bent under a heavy bag?—and these pale men rarely
61: stirred abroad without that mysterious burden. The shepherd himself,
62: though he had good reason to believe that the bag held nothing but
63: flaxen thread, or else the long rolls of strong linen spun from that
64: thread, was not quite sure that this trade of weaving, indispensable
65: though it was, could be carried on entirely without the help of the
66: Evil One. In that far-off time superstition clung easily round every
67: person or thing that was at all unwonted, or even intermittent and
68: occasional merely, like the visits of the pedlar or the knife-grinder.
69: No one knew where wandering men had their homes or their origin; and
70: how was a man to be explained unless you at least knew somebody who
71: knew his father and mother? To the peasants of old times, the world
72: outside their own direct experience was a region of vagueness and
73: mystery: to their untravelled thought a state of wandering was a
74: conception as dim as the winter life of the swallows that came back
75: with the spring; and even a settler, if he came from distant parts,
76: hardly ever ceased to be viewed with a remnant of distrust, which would
77: have prevented any surprise if a long course of inoffensive conduct on
78: his part had ended in the commission of a crime; especially if he had
79: any reputation for knowledge, or showed any skill in handicraft. All
80: cleverness, whether in the rapid use of that difficult instrument the
81: tongue, or in some other art unfamiliar to villagers, was in itself
82: suspicious: honest folk, born and bred in a visible manner, were mostly
83: not overwise or clever—at least, not beyond such a matter as knowing
84: the signs of the weather; and the process by which rapidity and
85: dexterity of any kind were acquired was so wholly hidden, that they
86: partook of the nature of conjuring. In this way it came to pass that
87: those scattered linen-weavers—emigrants from the town into the
88: country—were to the last regarded as aliens by their rustic neighbours,
89: and usually contracted the eccentric habits which belong to a state of
90: loneliness.
91: 
92: In the early years of this century, such a linen-weaver, named Silas
93: Marner, worked at his vocation in a stone cottage that stood among the
94: nutty hedgerows near the village of Raveloe, and not far from the edge
95: of a deserted stone-pit. The questionable sound of Silas’s loom, so
96: unlike the natural cheerful trotting of the winnowing-machine, or the
97: simpler rhythm of the flail, had a half-fearful fascination for the
98: Raveloe boys, who would often leave off their nutting or birds’-nesting
99: to peep in at the window of the stone cottage, counterbalancing a
100: certain awe at the mysterious action of the loom, by a pleasant sense
101: of scornful superiority, drawn from the mockery of its alternating
102: noises, along with the bent, tread-mill attitude of the weaver. But
103: sometimes it happened that Marner, pausing to adjust an irregularity in
104: his thread, became aware of the small scoundrels, and, though chary of
105: his time, he liked their intrusion so ill that he would descend from
106: his loom, and, opening the door, would fix on them a gaze that was
107: always enough to make them take to their legs in terror. For how was it
108: possible to believe that those large brown protuberant eyes in Silas
109: Marner’s pale face really saw nothing very distinctly that was not
110: close to them, and not rather that their dreadful stare could dart
111: cramp, or rickets, or a wry mouth at any boy who happened to be in the
112: rear? They had, perhaps, heard their fathers and mothers hint that
113: Silas Marner could cure folks’ rheumatism if he had a mind, and add,
114: still more darkly, that if you could only speak the devil fair enough,
115: he might save you the cost of the doctor. Such strange lingering echoes
116: of the old demon-worship might perhaps even now be caught by the
117: diligent listener among the grey-haired peasantry; for the rude mind
118: with difficulty associates the ideas of power and benignity. A shadowy
119: conception of power that by much persuasion can be induced to refrain
120: from inflicting harm, is the shape most easily taken by the sense of
121: the Invisible in the minds of men who have always been pressed close by
122: primitive wants, and to whom a life of hard toil has never been
123: illuminated by any enthusiastic religious faith. To them pain and
124: mishap present a far wider range of possibilities than gladness and
125: enjoyment: their imagination is almost barren of the images that feed
126: desire and hope, but is all overgrown by recollections that are a
127: perpetual pasture to fear. “Is there anything you can fancy that you
128: would like to eat?” I once said to an old labouring man, who was in his
129: last illness, and who had refused all the food his wife had offered
130: him. “No,” he answered, “I’ve never been used to nothing but common
131: victual, and I can’t eat that.” Experience had bred no fancies in him
132: that could raise the phantasm of appetite.
133: 
134: And Raveloe was a village where many of the old echoes lingered,
135: undrowned by new voices. Not that it was one of those barren parishes
136: lying on the outskirts of civilization—inhabited by meagre sheep and
137: thinly-scattered shepherds: on the contrary, it lay in the rich central
138: plain of what we are pleased to call Merry England, and held farms
139: which, speaking from a spiritual point of view, paid highly-desirable
140: tithes. But it was nestled in a snug well-wooded hollow, quite an
141: hour’s journey on horseback from any turnpike, where it was never
142: reached by the vibrations of the coach-horn, or of public opinion. It
143: was an important-looking village, with a fine old church and large
144: churchyard in the heart of it, and two or three large brick-and-stone
145: homesteads, with well-walled orchards and ornamental weathercocks,
146: standing close upon the road, and lifting more imposing fronts than the
147: rectory, which peeped from among the trees on the other side of the
148: churchyard:—a village which showed at once the summits of its social
149: life, and told the practised eye that there was no great park and
150: manor-house in the vicinity, but that there were several chiefs in
151: Raveloe who could farm badly quite at their ease, drawing enough money
152: from their bad farming, in those war times, to live in a rollicking
153: fashion, and keep a jolly Christmas, Whitsun, and Easter tide.
154: 
155: It was fifteen years since Silas Marner had first come to Raveloe; he
156: was then simply a pallid young man, with prominent short-sighted brown
157: eyes, whose appearance would have had nothing strange for people of
158: average culture and experience, but for the villagers near whom he had
159: come to settle it had mysterious peculiarities which corresponded with
160: the exceptional nature of his occupation, and his advent from an
161: unknown region called “North’ard”. So had his way of life:—he invited
162: no comer to step across his door-sill, and he never strolled into the
163: village to drink a pint at the Rainbow, or to gossip at the
164: wheelwright’s: he sought no man or woman, save for the purposes of his
165: calling, or in order to supply himself with necessaries; and it was
166: soon clear to the Raveloe lasses that he would never urge one of them
167: to accept him against her will—quite as if he had heard them declare
168: that they would never marry a dead man come to life again. This view of
169: Marner’s personality was not without another ground than his pale face
170: and unexampled eyes; for Jem Rodney, the mole-catcher, averred that one
171: evening as he was returning homeward, he saw Silas Marner leaning
172: against a stile with a heavy bag on his back, instead of resting the
173: bag on the stile as a man in his senses would have done; and that, on
174: coming up to him, he saw that Marner’s eyes were set like a dead man’s,
175: and he spoke to him, and shook him, and his limbs were stiff, and his
176: hands clutched the bag as if they’d been made of iron; but just as he
177: had made up his mind that the weaver was dead, he came all right again,
178: like, as you might say, in the winking of an eye, and said
179: “Good-night”, and walked off. All this Jem swore he had seen, more by
180: token that it was the very day he had been mole-catching on Squire
181: Cass’s land, down by the old saw-pit. Some said Marner must have been
182: in a “fit”, a word which seemed to explain things otherwise incredible;
183: but the argumentative Mr. Macey, clerk of the parish, shook his head,
184: and asked if anybody was ever known to go off in a fit and not fall
185: down. A fit was a stroke, wasn’t it? and it was in the nature of a
186: stroke to partly take away the use of a man’s limbs and throw him on
187: the parish, if he’d got no children to look to. No, no; it was no
188: stroke that would let a man stand on his legs, like a horse between the
189: shafts, and then walk off as soon as you can say “Gee!” But there might
190: be such a thing as a man’s soul being loose from his body, and going
191: out and in, like a bird out of its nest and back; and that was how
192: folks got over-wise, for they went to school in this shell-less state
193: to those who could teach them more than their neighbours could learn
194: with their five senses and the parson. And where did Master Marner get
195: his knowledge of herbs from—and charms too, if he liked to give them
196: away? Jem Rodney’s story was no more than what might have been expected
197: by anybody who had seen how Marner had cured Sally Oates, and made her
198: sleep like a baby, when her heart had been beating enough to burst her
199: body, for two months and more, while she had been under the doctor’s
200: care. He might cure more folks if he would; but he was worth speaking
201: fair, if it was only to keep him from doing you a mischief.
202: 
203: It was partly to this vague fear that Marner was indebted for
204: protecting him from the persecution that his singularities might have
205: drawn upon him, but still more to the fact that, the old linen-weaver
206: in the neighbouring parish of Tarley being dead, his handicraft made
207: him a highly welcome settler to the richer housewives of the district,
208: and even to the more provident cottagers, who had their little stock of
209: yarn at the year’s end. Their sense of his usefulness would have
210: counteracted any repugnance or suspicion which was not confirmed by a
211: deficiency in the quality or the tale of the cloth he wove for them.
212: And the years had rolled on without producing any change in the
213: impressions of the neighbours concerning Marner, except the change from
214: novelty to habit. At the end of fifteen years the Raveloe men said just
215: the same things about Silas Marner as at the beginning: they did not
216: say them quite so often, but they believed them much more strongly when
217: they did say them. There was only one important addition which the
218: years had brought: it was, that Master Marner had laid by a fine sight
219: of money somewhere, and that he could buy up “bigger men” than himself.
220: 
221: But while opinion concerning him had remained nearly stationary, and
222: his daily habits had presented scarcely any visible change, Marner’s
223: inward life had been a history and a metamorphosis, as that of every
224: fervid nature must be when it has fled, or been condemned, to solitude.
225: His life, before he came to Raveloe, had been filled with the movement,
226: the mental activity, and the close fellowship, which, in that day as in
227: this, marked the life of an artisan early incorporated in a narrow
228: religious sect, where the poorest layman has the chance of
229: distinguishing himself by gifts of speech, and has, at the very least,
230: the weight of a silent voter in the government of his community. Marner
231: was highly thought of in that little hidden world, known to itself as
232: the church assembling in Lantern Yard; he was believed to be a young
233: man of exemplary life and ardent faith; and a peculiar interest had
234: been centred in him ever since he had fallen, at a prayer-meeting, into
235: a mysterious rigidity and suspension of consciousness, which, lasting
236: for an hour or more, had been mistaken for death. To have sought a
237: medical explanation for this phenomenon would have been held by Silas
238: himself, as well as by his minister and fellow-members, a wilful
239: self-exclusion from the spiritual significance that might lie therein.
240: Silas was evidently a brother selected for a peculiar discipline; and
241: though the effort to interpret this discipline was discouraged by the
242: absence, on his part, of any spiritual vision during his outward
243: trance, yet it was believed by himself and others that its effect was
244: seen in an accession of light and fervour. A less truthful man than he
245: might have been tempted into the subsequent creation of a vision in the
246: form of resurgent memory; a less sane man might have believed in such a
247: creation; but Silas was both sane and honest, though, as with many
248: honest and fervent men, culture had not defined any channels for his
249: sense of mystery, and so it spread itself over the proper pathway of
250: inquiry and knowledge. He had inherited from his mother some
251: acquaintance with medicinal herbs and their preparation—a little store
252: of wisdom which she had imparted to him as a solemn bequest—but of late
253: years he had had doubts about the lawfulness of applying this
254: knowledge, believing that herbs could have no efficacy without prayer,
255: and that prayer might suffice without herbs; so that the inherited
256: delight he had in wandering in the fields in search of foxglove and
257: dandelion and coltsfoot, began to wear to him the character of a
258: temptation.
259: 
260: Among the members of his church there was one young man, a little older
261: than himself, with whom he had long lived in such close friendship that
262: it was the custom of their Lantern Yard brethren to call them David and
263: Jonathan. The real name of the friend was William Dane, and he, too,
264: was regarded as a shining instance of youthful piety, though somewhat
265: given to over-severity towards weaker brethren, and to be so dazzled by
266: his own light as to hold himself wiser than his teachers. But whatever
267: blemishes others might discern in William, to his friend’s mind he was
268: faultless; for Marner had one of those impressible self-doubting
269: natures which, at an inexperienced age, admire imperativeness and lean
270: on contradiction. The expression of trusting simplicity in Marner’s
271: face, heightened by that absence of special observation, that
272: defenceless, deer-like gaze which belongs to large prominent eyes, was
273: strongly contrasted by the self-complacent suppression of inward
274: triumph that lurked in the narrow slanting eyes and compressed lips of
275: William Dane. One of the most frequent topics of conversation between
276: the two friends was Assurance of salvation: Silas confessed that he
277: could never arrive at anything higher than hope mingled with fear, and
278: listened with longing wonder when William declared that he had
279: possessed unshaken assurance ever since, in the period of his
280: conversion, he had dreamed that he saw the words “calling and election
281: sure” standing by themselves on a white page in the open Bible. Such
282: colloquies have occupied many a pair of pale-faced weavers, whose
283: unnurtured souls have been like young winged things, fluttering
284: forsaken in the twilight.
285: 
286: It had seemed to the unsuspecting Silas that the friendship had
287: suffered no chill even from his formation of another attachment of a
288: closer kind. For some months he had been engaged to a young
289: servant-woman, waiting only for a little increase to their mutual
290: savings in order to their marriage; and it was a great delight to him
291: that Sarah did not object to William’s occasional presence in their
292: Sunday interviews. It was at this point in their history that Silas’s
293: cataleptic fit occurred during the prayer-meeting; and amidst the
294: various queries and expressions of interest addressed to him by his
295: fellow-members, William’s suggestion alone jarred with the general
296: sympathy towards a brother thus singled out for special dealings. He
297: observed that, to him, this trance looked more like a visitation of
298: Satan than a proof of divine favour, and exhorted his friend to see
299: that he hid no accursed thing within his soul. Silas, feeling bound to
300: accept rebuke and admonition as a brotherly office, felt no resentment,
301: but only pain, at his friend’s doubts concerning him; and to this was
302: soon added some anxiety at the perception that Sarah’s manner towards
303: him began to exhibit a strange fluctuation between an effort at an
304: increased manifestation of regard and involuntary signs of shrinking
305: and dislike. He asked her if she wished to break off their engagement;
306: but she denied this: their engagement was known to the church, and had
307: been recognized in the prayer-meetings; it could not be broken off
308: without strict investigation, and Sarah could render no reason that
309: would be sanctioned by the feeling of the community. At this time the
310: senior deacon was taken dangerously ill, and, being a childless
311: widower, he was tended night and day by some of the younger brethren or
312: sisters. Silas frequently took his turn in the night-watching with
313: William, the one relieving the other at two in the morning. The old
314: man, contrary to expectation, seemed to be on the way to recovery, when
315: one night Silas, sitting up by his bedside, observed that his usual
316: audible breathing had ceased. The candle was burning low, and he had to
317: lift it to see the patient’s face distinctly. Examination convinced him
318: that the deacon was dead—had been dead some time, for the limbs were
319: rigid. Silas asked himself if he had been asleep, and looked at the
320: clock: it was already four in the morning. How was it that William had
321: not come? In much anxiety he went to seek for help, and soon there were
322: several friends assembled in the house, the minister among them, while
323: Silas went away to his work, wishing he could have met William to know
324: the reason of his non-appearance. But at six o’clock, as he was
325: thinking of going to seek his friend, William came, and with him the
326: minister. They came to summon him to Lantern Yard, to meet the church
327: members there; and to his inquiry concerning the cause of the summons
328: the only reply was, “You will hear.” Nothing further was said until
329: Silas was seated in the vestry, in front of the minister, with the eyes
330: of those who to him represented God’s people fixed solemnly upon him.
331: Then the minister, taking out a pocket-knife, showed it to Silas, and
332: asked him if he knew where he had left that knife? Silas said, he did
333: not know that he had left it anywhere out of his own pocket—but he was
334: trembling at this strange interrogation. He was then exhorted not to
335: hide his sin, but to confess and repent. The knife had been found in
336: the bureau by the departed deacon’s bedside—found in the place where
337: the little bag of church money had lain, which the minister himself had
338: seen the day before. Some hand had removed that bag; and whose hand
339: could it be, if not that of the man to whom the knife belonged? For
340: some time Silas was mute with astonishment: then he said, “God will
341: clear me: I know nothing about the knife being there, or the money
342: being gone. Search me and my dwelling; you will find nothing but three
343: pound five of my own savings, which William Dane knows I have had these
344: six months.” At this William groaned, but the minister said, “The proof
345: is heavy against you, brother Marner. The money was taken in the night
346: last past, and no man was with our departed brother but you, for
347: William Dane declares to us that he was hindered by sudden sickness
348: from going to take his place as usual, and you yourself said that he
349: had not come; and, moreover, you neglected the dead body.”
350: 
351: “I must have slept,” said Silas. Then, after a pause, he added, “Or I
352: must have had another visitation like that which you have all seen me
353: under, so that the thief must have come and gone while I was not in the
354: body, but out of the body. But, I say again, search me and my dwelling,
355: for I have been nowhere else.”
356: 
357: The search was made, and it ended—in William Dane’s finding the
358: well-known bag, empty, tucked behind the chest of drawers in Silas’s
359: chamber! On this William exhorted his friend to confess, and not to
360: hide his sin any longer. Silas turned a look of keen reproach on him,
361: and said, “William, for nine years that we have gone in and out
362: together, have you ever known me tell a lie? But God will clear me.”
363: 
364: “Brother,” said William, “how do I know what you may have done in the
365: secret chambers of your heart, to give Satan an advantage over you?”
366: 
367: Silas was still looking at his friend. Suddenly a deep flush came over
368: his face, and he was about to speak impetuously, when he seemed checked
369: again by some inward shock, that sent the flush back and made him
370: tremble. But at last he spoke feebly, looking at William.
371: 
372: “I remember now—the knife wasn’t in my pocket.”
373: 
374: William said, “I know nothing of what you mean.” The other persons
375: present, however, began to inquire where Silas meant to say that the
376: knife was, but he would give no further explanation: he only said, “I
377: am sore stricken; I can say nothing. God will clear me.”
378: 
379: On their return to the vestry there was further deliberation. Any
380: resort to legal measures for ascertaining the culprit was contrary to
381: the principles of the church in Lantern Yard, according to which
382: prosecution was forbidden to Christians, even had the case held less
383: scandal to the community. But the members were bound to take other
384: measures for finding out the truth, and they resolved on praying and
385: drawing lots. This resolution can be a ground of surprise only to those
386: who are unacquainted with that obscure religious life which has gone on
387: in the alleys of our towns. Silas knelt with his brethren, relying on
388: his own innocence being certified by immediate divine interference, but
389: feeling that there was sorrow and mourning behind for him even
390: then—that his trust in man had been cruelly bruised. _The lots declared
391: that Silas Marner was guilty._ He was solemnly suspended from
392: church-membership, and called upon to render up the stolen money: only
393: on confession, as the sign of repentance, could he be received once
394: more within the folds of the church. Marner listened in silence. At
395: last, when everyone rose to depart, he went towards William Dane and
396: said, in a voice shaken by agitation—
397: 
398: “The last time I remember using my knife, was when I took it out to cut
399: a strap for you. I don’t remember putting it in my pocket again. _You_
400: stole the money, and you have woven a plot to lay the sin at my door.
401: But you may prosper, for all that: there is no just God that governs
402: the earth righteously, but a God of lies, that bears witness against
403: the innocent.”
404: 
405: There was a general shudder at this blasphemy.
406: 
407: William said meekly, “I leave our brethren to judge whether this is the
408: voice of Satan or not. I can do nothing but pray for you, Silas.”
409: 
410: Poor Marner went out with that despair in his soul—that shaken trust in
411: God and man, which is little short of madness to a loving nature. In
412: the bitterness of his wounded spirit, he said to himself, “_She_ will
413: cast me off too.” And he reflected that, if she did not believe the
414: testimony against him, her whole faith must be upset as his was. To
415: people accustomed to reason about the forms in which their religious
416: feeling has incorporated itself, it is difficult to enter into that
417: simple, untaught state of mind in which the form and the feeling have
418: never been severed by an act of reflection. We are apt to think it
419: inevitable that a man in Marner’s position should have begun to
420: question the validity of an appeal to the divine judgment by drawing
421: lots; but to him this would have been an effort of independent thought
422: such as he had never known; and he must have made the effort at a
423: moment when all his energies were turned into the anguish of
424: disappointed faith. If there is an angel who records the sorrows of men
425: as well as their sins, he knows how many and deep are the sorrows that
426: spring from false ideas for which no man is culpable.
427: 
428: Marner went home, and for a whole day sat alone, stunned by despair,
429: without any impulse to go to Sarah and attempt to win her belief in his
430: innocence. The second day he took refuge from benumbing unbelief, by
431: getting into his loom and working away as usual; and before many hours
432: were past, the minister and one of the deacons came to him with the
433: message from Sarah, that she held her engagement to him at an end.
434: Silas received the message mutely, and then turned away from the
435: messengers to work at his loom again. In little more than a month from
436: that time, Sarah was married to William Dane; and not long afterwards
437: it was known to the brethren in Lantern Yard that Silas Marner had
438: departed from the town.
439: 
440: 
441: 
442: CHAPTER II.
443: 
444: Even people whose lives have been made various by learning, sometimes
445: find it hard to keep a fast hold on their habitual views of life, on
446: their faith in the Invisible, nay, on the sense that their past joys
447: and sorrows are a real experience, when they are suddenly transported
448: to a new land, where the beings around them know nothing of their
449: history, and share none of their ideas—where their mother earth shows
450: another lap, and human life has other forms than those on which their
451: souls have been nourished. Minds that have been unhinged from their old
452: faith and love, have perhaps sought this Lethean influence of exile, in
453: which the past becomes dreamy because its symbols have all vanished,
454: and the present too is dreamy because it is linked with no memories.
455: But even _their_ experience may hardly enable them thoroughly to
456: imagine what was the effect on a simple weaver like Silas Marner, when
457: he left his own country and people and came to settle in Raveloe.
458: Nothing could be more unlike his native town, set within sight of the
459: widespread hillsides, than this low, wooded region, where he felt
460: hidden even from the heavens by the screening trees and hedgerows.
461: There was nothing here, when he rose in the deep morning quiet and
462: looked out on the dewy brambles and rank tufted grass, that seemed to
463: have any relation with that life centring in Lantern Yard, which had
464: once been to him the altar-place of high dispensations. The whitewashed
465: walls; the little pews where well-known figures entered with a subdued
466: rustling, and where first one well-known voice and then another,
467: pitched in a peculiar key of petition, uttered phrases at once occult
468: and familiar, like the amulet worn on the heart; the pulpit where the
469: minister delivered unquestioned doctrine, and swayed to and fro, and
470: handled the book in a long accustomed manner; the very pauses between
471: the couplets of the hymn, as it was given out, and the recurrent swell
472: of voices in song: these things had been the channel of divine
473: influences to Marner—they were the fostering home of his religious
474: emotions—they were Christianity and God’s kingdom upon earth. A weaver
475: who finds hard words in his hymn-book knows nothing of abstractions; as
476: the little child knows nothing of parental love, but only knows one
477: face and one lap towards which it stretches its arms for refuge and
478: nurture.
479: 
480: And what could be more unlike that Lantern Yard world than the world in
481: Raveloe?—orchards looking lazy with neglected plenty; the large church
482: in the wide churchyard, which men gazed at lounging at their own doors
483: in service-time; the purple-faced farmers jogging along the lanes or
484: turning in at the Rainbow; homesteads, where men supped heavily and
485: slept in the light of the evening hearth, and where women seemed to be
486: laying up a stock of linen for the life to come. There were no lips in
487: Raveloe from which a word could fall that would stir Silas Marner’s
488: benumbed faith to a sense of pain. In the early ages of the world, we
489: know, it was believed that each territory was inhabited and ruled by
490: its own divinities, so that a man could cross the bordering heights and
491: be out of the reach of his native gods, whose presence was confined to
492: the streams and the groves and the hills among which he had lived from
493: his birth. And poor Silas was vaguely conscious of something not unlike
494: the feeling of primitive men, when they fled thus, in fear or in
495: sullenness, from the face of an unpropitious deity. It seemed to him
496: that the Power he had vainly trusted in among the streets and at the
497: prayer-meetings, was very far away from this land in which he had taken
498: refuge, where men lived in careless abundance, knowing and needing
499: nothing of that trust, which, for him, had been turned to bitterness.
500: The little light he possessed spread its beams so narrowly, that
501: frustrated belief was a curtain broad enough to create for him the
502: blackness of night.
503: 
504: His first movement after the shock had been to work in his loom; and he
505: went on with this unremittingly, never asking himself why, now he was
506: come to Raveloe, he worked far on into the night to finish the tale of
507: Mrs. Osgood’s table-linen sooner than she expected—without
508: contemplating beforehand the money she would put into his hand for the
509: work. He seemed to weave, like the spider, from pure impulse, without
510: reflection. Every man’s work, pursued steadily, tends in this way to
511: become an end in itself, and so to bridge over the loveless chasms of
512: his life. Silas’s hand satisfied itself with throwing the shuttle, and
513: his eye with seeing the little squares in the cloth complete themselves
514: under his effort. Then there were the calls of hunger; and Silas, in
515: his solitude, had to provide his own breakfast, dinner, and supper, to
516: fetch his own water from the well, and put his own kettle on the fire;
517: and all these immediate promptings helped, along with the weaving, to
518: reduce his life to the unquestioning activity of a spinning insect. He
519: hated the thought of the past; there was nothing that called out his
520: love and fellowship toward the strangers he had come amongst; and the
521: future was all dark, for there was no Unseen Love that cared for him.
522: Thought was arrested by utter bewilderment, now its old narrow pathway
523: was closed, and affection seemed to have died under the bruise that had
524: fallen on its keenest nerves.
525: 
526: But at last Mrs. Osgood’s table-linen was finished, and Silas was paid
527: in gold. His earnings in his native town, where he worked for a
528: wholesale dealer, had been after a lower rate; he had been paid weekly,
529: and of his weekly earnings a large proportion had gone to objects of
530: piety and charity. Now, for the first time in his life, he had five
531: bright guineas put into his hand; no man expected a share of them, and
532: he loved no man that he should offer him a share. But what were the
533: guineas to him who saw no vista beyond countless days of weaving? It
534: was needless for him to ask that, for it was pleasant to him to feel
535: them in his palm, and look at their bright faces, which were all his
536: own: it was another element of life, like the weaving and the
537: satisfaction of hunger, subsisting quite aloof from the life of belief
538: and love from which he had been cut off. The weaver’s hand had known
539: the touch of hard-won money even before the palm had grown to its full
540: breadth; for twenty years, mysterious money had stood to him as the
541: symbol of earthly good, and the immediate object of toil. He had seemed
542: to love it little in the years when every penny had its purpose for
543: him; for he loved the _purpose_ then. But now, when all purpose was
544: gone, that habit of looking towards the money and grasping it with a
545: sense of fulfilled effort made a loam that was deep enough for the
546: seeds of desire; and as Silas walked homeward across the fields in the
547: twilight, he drew out the money and thought it was brighter in the
548: gathering gloom.
549: 
550: About this time an incident happened which seemed to open a possibility
551: of some fellowship with his neighbours. One day, taking a pair of shoes
552: to be mended, he saw the cobbler’s wife seated by the fire, suffering
553: from the terrible symptoms of heart-disease and dropsy, which he had
554: witnessed as the precursors of his mother’s death. He felt a rush of
555: pity at the mingled sight and remembrance, and, recalling the relief
556: his mother had found from a simple preparation of foxglove, he promised
557: Sally Oates to bring her something that would ease her, since the
558: doctor did her no good. In this office of charity, Silas felt, for the
559: first time since he had come to Raveloe, a sense of unity between his
560: past and present life, which might have been the beginning of his
561: rescue from the insect-like existence into which his nature had shrunk.
562: But Sally Oates’s disease had raised her into a personage of much
563: interest and importance among the neighbours, and the fact of her
564: having found relief from drinking Silas Marner’s “stuff” became a
565: matter of general discourse. When Doctor Kimble gave physic, it was
566: natural that it should have an effect; but when a weaver, who came from
567: nobody knew where, worked wonders with a bottle of brown waters, the
568: occult character of the process was evident. Such a sort of thing had
569: not been known since the Wise Woman at Tarley died; and she had charms
570: as well as “stuff”: everybody went to her when their children had fits.
571: Silas Marner must be a person of the same sort, for how did he know
572: what would bring back Sally Oates’s breath, if he didn’t know a fine
573: sight more than that? The Wise Woman had words that she muttered to
574: herself, so that you couldn’t hear what they were, and if she tied a
575: bit of red thread round the child’s toe the while, it would keep off
576: the water in the head. There were women in Raveloe, at that present
577: time, who had worn one of the Wise Woman’s little bags round their
578: necks, and, in consequence, had never had an idiot child, as Ann
579: Coulter had. Silas Marner could very likely do as much, and more; and
580: now it was all clear how he should have come from unknown parts, and be
581: so “comical-looking”. But Sally Oates must mind and not tell the
582: doctor, for he would be sure to set his face against Marner: he was
583: always angry about the Wise Woman, and used to threaten those who went
584: to her that they should have none of his help any more.
585: 
586: Silas now found himself and his cottage suddenly beset by mothers who
587: wanted him to charm away the whooping-cough, or bring back the milk,
588: and by men who wanted stuff against the rheumatics or the knots in the
589: hands; and, to secure themselves against a refusal, the applicants
590: brought silver in their palms. Silas might have driven a profitable
591: trade in charms as well as in his small list of drugs; but money on
592: this condition was no temptation to him: he had never known an impulse
593: towards falsity, and he drove one after another away with growing
594: irritation, for the news of him as a wise man had spread even to
595: Tarley, and it was long before people ceased to take long walks for the
596: sake of asking his aid. But the hope in his wisdom was at length
597: changed into dread, for no one believed him when he said he knew no
598: charms and could work no cures, and every man and woman who had an
599: accident or a new attack after applying to him, set the misfortune down
600: to Master Marner’s ill-will and irritated glances. Thus it came to pass
601: that his movement of pity towards Sally Oates, which had given him a
602: transient sense of brotherhood, heightened the repulsion between him
603: and his neighbours, and made his isolation more complete.
604: 
605: Gradually the guineas, the crowns, and the half-crowns grew to a heap,
606: and Marner drew less and less for his own wants, trying to solve the
607: problem of keeping himself strong enough to work sixteen hours a-day on
608: as small an outlay as possible. Have not men, shut up in solitary
609: imprisonment, found an interest in marking the moments by straight
610: strokes of a certain length on the wall, until the growth of the sum of
611: straight strokes, arranged in triangles, has become a mastering
612: purpose? Do we not wile away moments of inanity or fatigued waiting by
613: repeating some trivial movement or sound, until the repetition has bred
614: a want, which is incipient habit? That will help us to understand how
615: the love of accumulating money grows an absorbing passion in men whose
616: imaginations, even in the very beginning of their hoard, showed them no
617: purpose beyond it. Marner wanted the heaps of ten to grow into a
618: square, and then into a larger square; and every added guinea, while it
619: was itself a satisfaction, bred a new desire. In this strange world,
620: made a hopeless riddle to him, he might, if he had had a less intense
621: nature, have sat weaving, weaving—looking towards the end of his
622: pattern, or towards the end of his web, till he forgot the riddle, and
623: everything else but his immediate sensations; but the money had come to
624: mark off his weaving into periods, and the money not only grew, but it
625: remained with him. He began to think it was conscious of him, as his
626: loom was, and he would on no account have exchanged those coins, which
627: had become his familiars, for other coins with unknown faces. He
628: handled them, he counted them, till their form and colour were like the
629: satisfaction of a thirst to him; but it was only in the night, when his
630: work was done, that he drew them out to enjoy their companionship. He
631: had taken up some bricks in his floor underneath his loom, and here he
632: had made a hole in which he set the iron pot that contained his guineas
633: and silver coins, covering the bricks with sand whenever he replaced
634: them. Not that the idea of being robbed presented itself often or
635: strongly to his mind: hoarding was common in country districts in those
636: days; there were old labourers in the parish of Raveloe who were known
637: to have their savings by them, probably inside their flock-beds; but
638: their rustic neighbours, though not all of them as honest as their
639: ancestors in the days of King Alfred, had not imaginations bold enough
640: to lay a plan of burglary. How could they have spent the money in their
641: own village without betraying themselves? They would be obliged to “run
642: away”—a course as dark and dubious as a balloon journey.
643: 
644: So, year after year, Silas Marner had lived in this solitude, his
645: guineas rising in the iron pot, and his life narrowing and hardening
646: itself more and more into a mere pulsation of desire and satisfaction
647: that had no relation to any other being. His life had reduced itself to
648: the functions of weaving and hoarding, without any contemplation of an
649: end towards which the functions tended. The same sort of process has
650: perhaps been undergone by wiser men, when they have been cut off from
651: faith and love—only, instead of a loom and a heap of guineas, they have
652: had some erudite research, some ingenious project, or some well-knit
653: theory. Strangely Marner’s face and figure shrank and bent themselves
654: into a constant mechanical relation to the objects of his life, so that
655: he produced the same sort of impression as a handle or a crooked tube,
656: which has no meaning standing apart. The prominent eyes that used to
657: look trusting and dreamy, now looked as if they had been made to see
658: only one kind of thing that was very small, like tiny grain, for which
659: they hunted everywhere: and he was so withered and yellow, that, though
660: he was not yet forty, the children always called him “Old Master
661: Marner”.
662: 
663: Yet even in this stage of withering a little incident happened, which
664: showed that the sap of affection was not all gone. It was one of his
665: daily tasks to fetch his water from a well a couple of fields off, and
666: for this purpose, ever since he came to Raveloe, he had had a brown
667: earthenware pot, which he held as his most precious utensil among the
668: very few conveniences he had granted himself. It had been his companion
669: for twelve years, always standing on the same spot, always lending its
670: handle to him in the early morning, so that its form had an expression
671: for him of willing helpfulness, and the impress of its handle on his
672: palm gave a satisfaction mingled with that of having the fresh clear
673: water. One day as he was returning from the well, he stumbled against
674: the step of the stile, and his brown pot, falling with force against
675: the stones that overarched the ditch below him, was broken in three
676: pieces. Silas picked up the pieces and carried them home with grief in
677: his heart. The brown pot could never be of use to him any more, but he
678: stuck the bits together and propped the ruin in its old place for a
679: memorial.
680: 
681: This is the history of Silas Marner, until the fifteenth year after he
682: came to Raveloe. The livelong day he sat in his loom, his ear filled
683: with its monotony, his eyes bent close down on the slow growth of
684: sameness in the brownish web, his muscles moving with such even
685: repetition that their pause seemed almost as much a constraint as the
686: holding of his breath. But at night came his revelry: at night he
687: closed his shutters, and made fast his doors, and drew forth his gold.
688: Long ago the heap of coins had become too large for the iron pot to
689: hold them, and he had made for them two thick leather bags, which
690: wasted no room in their resting-place, but lent themselves flexibly to
691: every corner. How the guineas shone as they came pouring out of the
692: dark leather mouths! The silver bore no large proportion in amount to
693: the gold, because the long pieces of linen which formed his chief work
694: were always partly paid for in gold, and out of the silver he supplied
695: his own bodily wants, choosing always the shillings and sixpences to
696: spend in this way. He loved the guineas best, but he would not change
697: the silver—the crowns and half-crowns that were his own earnings,
698: begotten by his labour; he loved them all. He spread them out in heaps
699: and bathed his hands in them; then he counted them and set them up in
700: regular piles, and felt their rounded outline between his thumb and
701: fingers, and thought fondly of the guineas that were only half-earned
702: by the work in his loom, as if they had been unborn children—thought of
703: the guineas that were coming slowly through the coming years, through
704: all his life, which spread far away before him, the end quite hidden by
705: countless days of weaving. No wonder his thoughts were still with his
706: loom and his money when he made his journeys through the fields and the
707: lanes to fetch and carry home his work, so that his steps never
708: wandered to the hedge-banks and the lane-side in search of the once
709: familiar herbs: these too belonged to the past, from which his life had
710: shrunk away, like a rivulet that has sunk far down from the grassy
711: fringe of its old breadth into a little shivering thread, that cuts a
712: groove for itself in the barren sand.
713: 
714: But about the Christmas of that fifteenth year, a second great change
715: came over Marner’s life, and his history became blent in a singular
716: manner with the life of his neighbours.
717: 
718: 
719: 
720: CHAPTER III.
721: 
722: The greatest man in Raveloe was Squire Cass, who lived in the large red
723: house with the handsome flight of stone steps in front and the high
724: stables behind it, nearly opposite the church. He was only one among
725: several landed parishioners, but he alone was honoured with the title
726: of Squire; for though Mr. Osgood’s family was also understood to be of
727: timeless origin—the Raveloe imagination having never ventured back to
728: that fearful blank when there were no Osgoods—still, he merely owned
729: the farm he occupied; whereas Squire Cass had a tenant or two, who
730: complained of the game to him quite as if he had been a lord.
731: 
732: It was still that glorious war-time which was felt to be a peculiar
733: favour of Providence towards the landed interest, and the fall of
734: prices had not yet come to carry the race of small squires and yeomen
735: down that road to ruin for which extravagant habits and bad husbandry
736: were plentifully anointing their wheels. I am speaking now in relation
737: to Raveloe and the parishes that resembled it; for our old-fashioned
738: country life had many different aspects, as all life must have when it
739: is spread over a various surface, and breathed on variously by
740: multitudinous currents, from the winds of heaven to the thoughts of
741: men, which are for ever moving and crossing each other with
742: incalculable results. Raveloe lay low among the bushy trees and the
743: rutted lanes, aloof from the currents of industrial energy and Puritan
744: earnestness: the rich ate and drank freely, accepting gout and apoplexy
745: as things that ran mysteriously in respectable families, and the poor
746: thought that the rich were entirely in the right of it to lead a jolly
747: life; besides, their feasting caused a multiplication of orts, which
748: were the heirlooms of the poor. Betty Jay scented the boiling of Squire
749: Cass’s hams, but her longing was arrested by the unctuous liquor in
750: which they were boiled; and when the seasons brought round the great
751: merry-makings, they were regarded on all hands as a fine thing for the
752: poor. For the Raveloe feasts were like the rounds of beef and the
753: barrels of ale—they were on a large scale, and lasted a good while,
754: especially in the winter-time. After ladies had packed up their best
755: gowns and top-knots in bandboxes, and had incurred the risk of fording
756: streams on pillions with the precious burden in rainy or snowy weather,
757: when there was no knowing how high the water would rise, it was not to
758: be supposed that they looked forward to a brief pleasure. On this
759: ground it was always contrived in the dark seasons, when there was
760: little work to be done, and the hours were long, that several
761: neighbours should keep open house in succession. So soon as Squire
762: Cass’s standing dishes diminished in plenty and freshness, his guests
763: had nothing to do but to walk a little higher up the village to Mr.
764: Osgood’s, at the Orchards, and they found hams and chines uncut,
765: pork-pies with the scent of the fire in them, spun butter in all its
766: freshness—everything, in fact, that appetites at leisure could desire,
767: in perhaps greater perfection, though not in greater abundance, than at
768: Squire Cass’s.
769: 
770: For the Squire’s wife had died long ago, and the Red House was without
771: that presence of the wife and mother which is the fountain of wholesome
772: love and fear in parlour and kitchen; and this helped to account not
773: only for there being more profusion than finished excellence in the
774: holiday provisions, but also for the frequency with which the proud
775: Squire condescended to preside in the parlour of the Rainbow rather
776: than under the shadow of his own dark wainscot; perhaps, also, for the
777: fact that his sons had turned out rather ill. Raveloe was not a place
778: where moral censure was severe, but it was thought a weakness in the
779: Squire that he had kept all his sons at home in idleness; and though
780: some licence was to be allowed to young men whose fathers could afford
781: it, people shook their heads at the courses of the second son, Dunstan,
782: commonly called Dunsey Cass, whose taste for swopping and betting might
783: turn out to be a sowing of something worse than wild oats. To be sure,
784: the neighbours said, it was no matter what became of Dunsey—a spiteful
785: jeering fellow, who seemed to enjoy his drink the more when other
786: people went dry—always provided that his doings did not bring trouble
787: on a family like Squire Cass’s, with a monument in the church, and
788: tankards older than King George. But it would be a thousand pities if
789: Mr. Godfrey, the eldest, a fine open-faced good-natured young man who
790: was to come into the land some day, should take to going along the same
791: road with his brother, as he had seemed to do of late. If he went on in
792: that way, he would lose Miss Nancy Lammeter; for it was well known that
793: she had looked very shyly on him ever since last Whitsuntide
794: twelvemonth, when there was so much talk about his being away from home
795: days and days together. There was something wrong, more than
796: common—that was quite clear; for Mr. Godfrey didn’t look half so
797: fresh-coloured and open as he used to do. At one time everybody was
798: saying, What a handsome couple he and Miss Nancy Lammeter would make!
799: and if she could come to be mistress at the Red House, there would be a
800: fine change, for the Lammeters had been brought up in that way, that
801: they never suffered a pinch of salt to be wasted, and yet everybody in
802: their household had of the best, according to his place. Such a
803: daughter-in-law would be a saving to the old Squire, if she never
804: brought a penny to her fortune; for it was to be feared that,
805: notwithstanding his incomings, there were more holes in his pocket than
806: the one where he put his own hand in. But if Mr. Godfrey didn’t turn
807: over a new leaf, he might say “Good-bye” to Miss Nancy Lammeter.
808: 
809: It was the once hopeful Godfrey who was standing, with his hands in his
810: side-pockets and his back to the fire, in the dark wainscoted parlour,
811: one late November afternoon in that fifteenth year of Silas Marner’s
812: life at Raveloe. The fading grey light fell dimly on the walls
813: decorated with guns, whips, and foxes’ brushes, on coats and hats flung
814: on the chairs, on tankards sending forth a scent of flat ale, and on a
815: half-choked fire, with pipes propped up in the chimney-corners: signs
816: of a domestic life destitute of any hallowing charm, with which the
817: look of gloomy vexation on Godfrey’s blond face was in sad accordance.
818: He seemed to be waiting and listening for some one’s approach, and
819: presently the sound of a heavy step, with an accompanying whistle, was
820: heard across the large empty entrance-hall.
821: 
822: The door opened, and a thick-set, heavy-looking young man entered, with
823: the flushed face and the gratuitously elated bearing which mark the
824: first stage of intoxication. It was Dunsey, and at the sight of him
825: Godfrey’s face parted with some of its gloom to take on the more active
826: expression of hatred. The handsome brown spaniel that lay on the hearth
827: retreated under the chair in the chimney-corner.
828: 
829: “Well, Master Godfrey, what do you want with me?” said Dunsey, in a
830: mocking tone. “You’re my elders and betters, you know; I was obliged to
831: come when you sent for me.”
832: 
833: “Why, this is what I want—and just shake yourself sober and listen,
834: will you?” said Godfrey, savagely. He had himself been drinking more
835: than was good for him, trying to turn his gloom into uncalculating
836: anger. “I want to tell you, I must hand over that rent of Fowler’s to
837: the Squire, or else tell him I gave it you; for he’s threatening to
838: distrain for it, and it’ll all be out soon, whether I tell him or not.
839: He said, just now, before he went out, he should send word to Cox to
840: distrain, if Fowler didn’t come and pay up his arrears this week. The
841: Squire’s short o’ cash, and in no humour to stand any nonsense; and you
842: know what he threatened, if ever he found you making away with his
843: money again. So, see and get the money, and pretty quickly, will you?”
844: 
845: “Oh!” said Dunsey, sneeringly, coming nearer to his brother and looking
846: in his face. “Suppose, now, you get the money yourself, and save me the
847: trouble, eh? Since you was so kind as to hand it over to me, you’ll not
848: refuse me the kindness to pay it back for me: it was your brotherly
849: love made you do it, you know.”
850: 
851: Godfrey bit his lips and clenched his fist. “Don’t come near me with
852: that look, else I’ll knock you down.”
853: 
854: “Oh no, you won’t,” said Dunsey, turning away on his heel, however.
855: “Because I’m such a good-natured brother, you know. I might get you
856: turned out of house and home, and cut off with a shilling any day. I
857: might tell the Squire how his handsome son was married to that nice
858: young woman, Molly Farren, and was very unhappy because he couldn’t
859: live with his drunken wife, and I should slip into your place as
860: comfortable as could be. But you see, I don’t do it—I’m so easy and
861: good-natured. You’ll take any trouble for me. You’ll get the hundred
862: pounds for me—I know you will.”
863: 
864: “How can I get the money?” said Godfrey, quivering. “I haven’t a
865: shilling to bless myself with. And it’s a lie that you’d slip into my
866: place: you’d get yourself turned out too, that’s all. For if you begin
867: telling tales, I’ll follow. Bob’s my father’s favourite—you know that
868: very well. He’d only think himself well rid of you.”
869: 
870: “Never mind,” said Dunsey, nodding his head sideways as he looked out
871: of the window. “It ’ud be very pleasant to me to go in your
872: company—you’re such a handsome brother, and we’ve always been so fond
873: of quarrelling with one another, I shouldn’t know what to do without
874: you. But you’d like better for us both to stay at home together; I know
875: you would. So you’ll manage to get that little sum o’ money, and I’ll
876: bid you good-bye, though I’m sorry to part.”
877: 
878: Dunstan was moving off, but Godfrey rushed after him and seized him by
879: the arm, saying, with an oath—
880: 
881: “I tell you, I have no money: I can get no money.”
882: 
883: “Borrow of old Kimble.”
884: 
885: “I tell you, he won’t lend me any more, and I shan’t ask him.”
886: 
887: “Well, then, sell Wildfire.”
888: 
889: “Yes, that’s easy talking. I must have the money directly.”
890: 
891: “Well, you’ve only got to ride him to the hunt to-morrow. There’ll be
892: Bryce and Keating there, for sure. You’ll get more bids than one.”
893: 
894: “I daresay, and get back home at eight o’clock, splashed up to the
895: chin. I’m going to Mrs. Osgood’s birthday dance.”
896: 
897: “Oho!” said Dunsey, turning his head on one side, and trying to speak
898: in a small mincing treble. “And there’s sweet Miss Nancy coming; and we
899: shall dance with her, and promise never to be naughty again, and be
900: taken into favour, and—”
901: 
902: “Hold your tongue about Miss Nancy, you fool,” said Godfrey, turning
903: red, “else I’ll throttle you.”
904: 
905: “What for?” said Dunsey, still in an artificial tone, but taking a whip
906: from the table and beating the butt-end of it on his palm. “You’ve a
907: very good chance. I’d advise you to creep up her sleeve again: it ’ud
908: be saving time, if Molly should happen to take a drop too much laudanum
909: some day, and make a widower of you. Miss Nancy wouldn’t mind being a
910: second, if she didn’t know it. And you’ve got a good-natured brother,
911: who’ll keep your secret well, because you’ll be so very obliging to
912: him.”
913: 
914: “I’ll tell you what it is,” said Godfrey, quivering, and pale again,
915: “my patience is pretty near at an end. If you’d a little more sharpness
916: in you, you might know that you may urge a man a bit too far, and make
917: one leap as easy as another. I don’t know but what it is so now: I may
918: as well tell the Squire everything myself—I should get you off my back,
919: if I got nothing else. And, after all, he’ll know some time. She’s been
920: threatening to come herself and tell him. So, don’t flatter yourself
921: that your secrecy’s worth any price you choose to ask. You drain me of
922: money till I have got nothing to pacify _her_ with, and she’ll do as
923: she threatens some day. It’s all one. I’ll tell my father everything
924: myself, and you may go to the devil.”
925: 
926: Dunsey perceived that he had overshot his mark, and that there was a
927: point at which even the hesitating Godfrey might be driven into
928: decision. But he said, with an air of unconcern—
929: 
930: “As you please; but I’ll have a draught of ale first.” And ringing the
931: bell, he threw himself across two chairs, and began to rap the
932: window-seat with the handle of his whip.
933: 
934: Godfrey stood, still with his back to the fire, uneasily moving his
935: fingers among the contents of his side-pockets, and looking at the
936: floor. That big muscular frame of his held plenty of animal courage,
937: but helped him to no decision when the dangers to be braved were such
938: as could neither be knocked down nor throttled. His natural
939: irresolution and moral cowardice were exaggerated by a position in
940: which dreaded consequences seemed to press equally on all sides, and
941: his irritation had no sooner provoked him to defy Dunstan and
942: anticipate all possible betrayals, than the miseries he must bring on
943: himself by such a step seemed more unendurable to him than the present
944: evil. The results of confession were not contingent, they were certain;
945: whereas betrayal was not certain. From the near vision of that
946: certainty he fell back on suspense and vacillation with a sense of
947: repose. The disinherited son of a small squire, equally disinclined to
948: dig and to beg, was almost as helpless as an uprooted tree, which, by
949: the favour of earth and sky, has grown to a handsome bulk on the spot
950: where it first shot upward. Perhaps it would have been possible to
951: think of digging with some cheerfulness if Nancy Lammeter were to be
952: won on those terms; but, since he must irrevocably lose _her_ as well
953: as the inheritance, and must break every tie but the one that degraded
954: him and left him without motive for trying to recover his better self,
955: he could imagine no future for himself on the other side of confession
956: but that of “’listing for a soldier”—the most desperate step, short of
957: suicide, in the eyes of respectable families. No! he would rather trust
958: to casualties than to his own resolve—rather go on sitting at the
959: feast, and sipping the wine he loved, though with the sword hanging
960: over him and terror in his heart, than rush away into the cold darkness
961: where there was no pleasure left. The utmost concession to Dunstan
962: about the horse began to seem easy, compared with the fulfilment of his
963: own threat. But his pride would not let him recommence the conversation
964: otherwise than by continuing the quarrel. Dunstan was waiting for this,
965: and took his ale in shorter draughts than usual.
966: 
967: “It’s just like you,” Godfrey burst out, in a bitter tone, “to talk
968: about my selling Wildfire in that cool way—the last thing I’ve got to
969: call my own, and the best bit of horse-flesh I ever had in my life. And
970: if you’d got a spark of pride in you, you’d be ashamed to see the
971: stables emptied, and everybody sneering about it. But it’s my belief
972: you’d sell yourself, if it was only for the pleasure of making somebody
973: feel he’d got a bad bargain.”
974: 
975: “Aye, aye,” said Dunstan, very placably, “you do me justice, I see. You
976: know I’m a jewel for ’ticing people into bargains. For which reason I
977: advise you to let _me_ sell Wildfire. I’d ride him to the hunt
978: to-morrow for you, with pleasure. I shouldn’t look so handsome as you
979: in the saddle, but it’s the horse they’ll bid for, and not the rider.”
980: 
981: “Yes, I daresay—trust my horse to you!”
982: 
983: “As you please,” said Dunstan, rapping the window-seat again with an
984: air of great unconcern. “It’s _you_ have got to pay Fowler’s money;
985: it’s none of my business. You received the money from him when you went
986: to Bramcote, and _you_ told the Squire it wasn’t paid. I’d nothing to
987: do with that; you chose to be so obliging as to give it me, that was
988: all. If you don’t want to pay the money, let it alone; it’s all one to
989: me. But I was willing to accommodate you by undertaking to sell the
990: horse, seeing it’s not convenient to you to go so far to-morrow.”
991: 
992: Godfrey was silent for some moments. He would have liked to spring on
993: Dunstan, wrench the whip from his hand, and flog him to within an inch
994: of his life; and no bodily fear could have deterred him; but he was
995: mastered by another sort of fear, which was fed by feelings stronger
996: even than his resentment. When he spoke again, it was in a
997: half-conciliatory tone.
998: 
999: “Well, you mean no nonsense about the horse, eh? You’ll sell him all
1000: fair, and hand over the money? If you don’t, you know, everything ’ull
1001: go to smash, for I’ve got nothing else to trust to. And you’ll have
1002: less pleasure in pulling the house over my head, when your own skull’s
1003: to be broken too.”
1004: 
1005: “Aye, aye,” said Dunstan, rising; “all right. I thought you’d come
1006: round. I’m the fellow to bring old Bryce up to the scratch. I’ll get
1007: you a hundred and twenty for him, if I get you a penny.”
1008: 
1009: “But it’ll perhaps rain cats and dogs to-morrow, as it did yesterday,
1010: and then you can’t go,” said Godfrey, hardly knowing whether he wished
1011: for that obstacle or not.
1012: 
1013: “Not _it_,” said Dunstan. “I’m always lucky in my weather. It might
1014: rain if you wanted to go yourself. You never hold trumps, you know—I
1015: always do. You’ve got the beauty, you see, and I’ve got the luck, so
1016: you must keep me by you for your crooked sixpence; you’ll _ne_-ver get
1017: along without me.”
1018: 
1019: “Confound you, hold your tongue!” said Godfrey, impetuously. “And take
1020: care to keep sober to-morrow, else you’ll get pitched on your head
1021: coming home, and Wildfire might be the worse for it.”
1022: 
1023: “Make your tender heart easy,” said Dunstan, opening the door. “You
1024: never knew me see double when I’d got a bargain to make; it ’ud spoil
1025: the fun. Besides, whenever I fall, I’m warranted to fall on my legs.”
1026: 
1027: With that, Dunstan slammed the door behind him, and left Godfrey to
1028: that bitter rumination on his personal circumstances which was now
1029: unbroken from day to day save by the excitement of sporting, drinking,
1030: card-playing, or the rarer and less oblivious pleasure of seeing Miss
1031: Nancy Lammeter. The subtle and varied pains springing from the higher
1032: sensibility that accompanies higher culture, are perhaps less pitiable
1033: than that dreary absence of impersonal enjoyment and consolation which
1034: leaves ruder minds to the perpetual urgent companionship of their own
1035: griefs and discontents. The lives of those rural forefathers, whom we
1036: are apt to think very prosaic figures—men whose only work was to ride
1037: round their land, getting heavier and heavier in their saddles, and who
1038: passed the rest of their days in the half-listless gratification of
1039: senses dulled by monotony—had a certain pathos in them nevertheless.
1040: Calamities came to _them_ too, and their early errors carried hard
1041: consequences: perhaps the love of some sweet maiden, the image of
1042: purity, order, and calm, had opened their eyes to the vision of a life
1043: in which the days would not seem too long, even without rioting; but
1044: the maiden was lost, and the vision passed away, and then what was left
1045: to them, especially when they had become too heavy for the hunt, or for
1046: carrying a gun over the furrows, but to drink and get merry, or to
1047: drink and get angry, so that they might be independent of variety, and
1048: say over again with eager emphasis the things they had said already any
1049: time that twelvemonth? Assuredly, among these flushed and dull-eyed men
1050: there were some whom—thanks to their native human-kindness—even riot
1051: could never drive into brutality; men who, when their cheeks were
1052: fresh, had felt the keen point of sorrow or remorse, had been pierced
1053: by the reeds they leaned on, or had lightly put their limbs in fetters
1054: from which no struggle could loose them; and under these sad
1055: circumstances, common to us all, their thoughts could find no
1056: resting-place outside the ever-trodden round of their own petty
1057: history.
1058: 
1059: That, at least, was the condition of Godfrey Cass in this
1060: six-and-twentieth year of his life. A movement of compunction, helped
1061: by those small indefinable influences which every personal relation
1062: exerts on a pliant nature, had urged him into a secret marriage, which
1063: was a blight on his life. It was an ugly story of low passion,
1064: delusion, and waking from delusion, which needs not to be dragged from
1065: the privacy of Godfrey’s bitter memory. He had long known that the
1066: delusion was partly due to a trap laid for him by Dunstan, who saw in
1067: his brother’s degrading marriage the means of gratifying at once his
1068: jealous hate and his cupidity. And if Godfrey could have felt himself
1069: simply a victim, the iron bit that destiny had put into his mouth would
1070: have chafed him less intolerably. If the curses he muttered half aloud
1071: when he was alone had had no other object than Dunstan’s diabolical
1072: cunning, he might have shrunk less from the consequences of avowal. But
1073: he had something else to curse—his own vicious folly, which now seemed
1074: as mad and unaccountable to him as almost all our follies and vices do
1075: when their promptings have long passed away. For four years he had
1076: thought of Nancy Lammeter, and wooed her with tacit patient worship, as
1077: the woman who made him think of the future with joy: she would be his
1078: wife, and would make home lovely to him, as his father’s home had never
1079: been; and it would be easy, when she was always near, to shake off
1080: those foolish habits that were no pleasures, but only a feverish way of
1081: annulling vacancy. Godfrey’s was an essentially domestic nature, bred
1082: up in a home where the hearth had no smiles, and where the daily habits
1083: were not chastised by the presence of household order. His easy
1084: disposition made him fall in unresistingly with the family courses, but
1085: the need of some tender permanent affection, the longing for some
1086: influence that would make the good he preferred easy to pursue, caused
1087: the neatness, purity, and liberal orderliness of the Lammeter
1088: household, sunned by the smile of Nancy, to seem like those fresh
1089: bright hours of the morning when temptations go to sleep and leave the
1090: ear open to the voice of the good angel, inviting to industry,
1091: sobriety, and peace. And yet the hope of this paradise had not been
1092: enough to save him from a course which shut him out of it for ever.
1093: Instead of keeping fast hold of the strong silken rope by which Nancy
1094: would have drawn him safe to the green banks where it was easy to step
1095: firmly, he had let himself be dragged back into mud and slime, in which
1096: it was useless to struggle. He had made ties for himself which robbed
1097: him of all wholesome motive, and were a constant exasperation.
1098: 
1099: Still, there was one position worse than the present: it was the
1100: position he would be in when the ugly secret was disclosed; and the
1101: desire that continually triumphed over every other was that of warding
1102: off the evil day, when he would have to bear the consequences of his
1103: father’s violent resentment for the wound inflicted on his family
1104: pride—would have, perhaps, to turn his back on that hereditary ease and
1105: dignity which, after all, was a sort of reason for living, and would
1106: carry with him the certainty that he was banished for ever from the
1107: sight and esteem of Nancy Lammeter. The longer the interval, the more
1108: chance there was of deliverance from some, at least, of the hateful
1109: consequences to which he had sold himself; the more opportunities
1110: remained for him to snatch the strange gratification of seeing Nancy,
1111: and gathering some faint indications of her lingering regard. Towards
1112: this gratification he was impelled, fitfully, every now and then, after
1113: having passed weeks in which he had avoided her as the far-off
1114: bright-winged prize that only made him spring forward and find his
1115: chain all the more galling. One of those fits of yearning was on him
1116: now, and it would have been strong enough to have persuaded him to
1117: trust Wildfire to Dunstan rather than disappoint the yearning, even if
1118: he had not had another reason for his disinclination towards the
1119: morrow’s hunt. That other reason was the fact that the morning’s meet
1120: was near Batherley, the market-town where the unhappy woman lived,
1121: whose image became more odious to him every day; and to his thought the
1122: whole vicinage was haunted by her. The yoke a man creates for himself
1123: by wrong-doing will breed hate in the kindliest nature; and the
1124: good-humoured, affectionate-hearted Godfrey Cass was fast becoming a
1125: bitter man, visited by cruel wishes, that seemed to enter, and depart,
1126: and enter again, like demons who had found in him a ready-garnished
1127: home.
1128: 
1129: What was he to do this evening to pass the time? He might as well go to
1130: the Rainbow, and hear the talk about the cock-fighting: everybody was
1131: there, and what else was there to be done? Though, for his own part, he
1132: did not care a button for cock-fighting. Snuff, the brown spaniel, who
1133: had placed herself in front of him, and had been watching him for some
1134: time, now jumped up in impatience for the expected caress. But Godfrey
1135: thrust her away without looking at her, and left the room, followed
1136: humbly by the unresenting Snuff—perhaps because she saw no other career
1137: open to her.
1138: 
1139: 
1140: 
1141: CHAPTER IV.
1142: 
1143: Dunstan Cass, setting off in the raw morning, at the judiciously quiet
1144: pace of a man who is obliged to ride to cover on his hunter, had to
1145: take his way along the lane which, at its farther extremity, passed by
1146: the piece of unenclosed ground called the Stone-pit, where stood the
1147: cottage, once a stone-cutter’s shed, now for fifteen years inhabited by
1148: Silas Marner. The spot looked very dreary at this season, with the
1149: moist trodden clay about it, and the red, muddy water high up in the
1150: deserted quarry. That was Dunstan’s first thought as he approached it;
1151: the second was, that the old fool of a weaver, whose loom he heard
1152: rattling already, had a great deal of money hidden somewhere. How was
1153: it that he, Dunstan Cass, who had often heard talk of Marner’s
1154: miserliness, had never thought of suggesting to Godfrey that he should
1155: frighten or persuade the old fellow into lending the money on the
1156: excellent security of the young Squire’s prospects? The resource
1157: occurred to him now as so easy and agreeable, especially as Marner’s
1158: hoard was likely to be large enough to leave Godfrey a handsome surplus
1159: beyond his immediate needs, and enable him to accommodate his faithful
1160: brother, that he had almost turned the horse’s head towards home again.
1161: Godfrey would be ready enough to accept the suggestion: he would snatch
1162: eagerly at a plan that might save him from parting with Wildfire. But
1163: when Dunstan’s meditation reached this point, the inclination to go on
1164: grew strong and prevailed. He didn’t want to give Godfrey that
1165: pleasure: he preferred that Master Godfrey should be vexed. Moreover,
1166: Dunstan enjoyed the self-important consciousness of having a horse to
1167: sell, and the opportunity of driving a bargain, swaggering, and
1168: possibly taking somebody in. He might have all the satisfaction
1169: attendant on selling his brother’s horse, and not the less have the
1170: further satisfaction of setting Godfrey to borrow Marner’s money. So he
1171: rode on to cover.
1172: 
1173: Bryce and Keating were there, as Dunstan was quite sure they would
1174: be—he was such a lucky fellow.
1175: 
1176: “Heyday!” said Bryce, who had long had his eye on Wildfire, “you’re on
1177: your brother’s horse to-day: how’s that?”
1178: 
1179: “Oh, I’ve swopped with him,” said Dunstan, whose delight in lying,
1180: grandly independent of utility, was not to be diminished by the
1181: likelihood that his hearer would not believe him—“Wildfire’s mine now.”
1182: 
1183: “What! has he swopped with you for that big-boned hack of yours?” said
1184: Bryce, quite aware that he should get another lie in answer.
1185: 
1186: “Oh, there was a little account between us,” said Dunsey, carelessly,
1187: “and Wildfire made it even. I accommodated him by taking the horse,
1188: though it was against my will, for I’d got an itch for a mare o’
1189: Jortin’s—as rare a bit o’ blood as ever you threw your leg across. But
1190: I shall keep Wildfire, now I’ve got him, though I’d a bid of a hundred
1191: and fifty for him the other day, from a man over at Flitton—he’s buying
1192: for Lord Cromleck—a fellow with a cast in his eye, and a green
1193: waistcoat. But I mean to stick to Wildfire: I shan’t get a better at a
1194: fence in a hurry. The mare’s got more blood, but she’s a bit too weak
1195: in the hind-quarters.”
1196: 
1197: Bryce of course divined that Dunstan wanted to sell the horse, and
1198: Dunstan knew that he divined it (horse-dealing is only one of many
1199: human transactions carried on in this ingenious manner); and they both
1200: considered that the bargain was in its first stage, when Bryce replied
1201: ironically—
1202: 
1203: “I wonder at that now; I wonder you mean to keep him; for I never heard
1204: of a man who didn’t want to sell his horse getting a bid of half as
1205: much again as the horse was worth. You’ll be lucky if you get a
1206: hundred.”
1207: 
1208: Keating rode up now, and the transaction became more complicated. It
1209: ended in the purchase of the horse by Bryce for a hundred and twenty,
1210: to be paid on the delivery of Wildfire, safe and sound, at the
1211: Batherley stables. It did occur to Dunsey that it might be wise for him
1212: to give up the day’s hunting, proceed at once to Batherley, and, having
1213: waited for Bryce’s return, hire a horse to carry him home with the
1214: money in his pocket. But the inclination for a run, encouraged by
1215: confidence in his luck, and by a draught of brandy from his
1216: pocket-pistol at the conclusion of the bargain, was not easy to
1217: overcome, especially with a horse under him that would take the fences
1218: to the admiration of the field. Dunstan, however, took one fence too
1219: many, and got his horse pierced with a hedge-stake. His own
1220: ill-favoured person, which was quite unmarketable, escaped without
1221: injury; but poor Wildfire, unconscious of his price, turned on his
1222: flank and painfully panted his last. It happened that Dunstan, a short
1223: time before, having had to get down to arrange his stirrup, had
1224: muttered a good many curses at this interruption, which had thrown him
1225: in the rear of the hunt near the moment of glory, and under this
1226: exasperation had taken the fences more blindly. He would soon have been
1227: up with the hounds again, when the fatal accident happened; and hence
1228: he was between eager riders in advance, not troubling themselves about
1229: what happened behind them, and far-off stragglers, who were as likely
1230: as not to pass quite aloof from the line of road in which Wildfire had
1231: fallen. Dunstan, whose nature it was to care more for immediate
1232: annoyances than for remote consequences, no sooner recovered his legs,
1233: and saw that it was all over with Wildfire, than he felt a satisfaction
1234: at the absence of witnesses to a position which no swaggering could
1235: make enviable. Reinforcing himself, after his shake, with a little
1236: brandy and much swearing, he walked as fast as he could to a coppice on
1237: his right hand, through which it occurred to him that he could make his
1238: way to Batherley without danger of encountering any member of the hunt.
1239: His first intention was to hire a horse there and ride home forthwith,
1240: for to walk many miles without a gun in his hand, and along an ordinary
1241: road, was as much out of the question to him as to other spirited young
1242: men of his kind. He did not much mind about taking the bad news to
1243: Godfrey, for he had to offer him at the same time the resource of
1244: Marner’s money; and if Godfrey kicked, as he always did, at the notion
1245: of making a fresh debt from which he himself got the smallest share of
