1246: advantage, why, he wouldn’t kick long: Dunstan felt sure he could worry
1247: Godfrey into anything. The idea of Marner’s money kept growing in
1248: vividness, now the want of it had become immediate; the prospect of
1249: having to make his appearance with the muddy boots of a pedestrian at
1250: Batherley, and to encounter the grinning queries of stablemen, stood
1251: unpleasantly in the way of his impatience to be back at Raveloe and
1252: carry out his felicitous plan; and a casual visitation of his
1253: waistcoat-pocket, as he was ruminating, awakened his memory to the fact
1254: that the two or three small coins his forefinger encountered there were
1255: of too pale a colour to cover that small debt, without payment of which
1256: the stable-keeper had declared he would never do any more business with
1257: Dunsey Cass. After all, according to the direction in which the run had
1258: brought him, he was not so very much farther from home than he was from
1259: Batherley; but Dunsey, not being remarkable for clearness of head, was
1260: only led to this conclusion by the gradual perception that there were
1261: other reasons for choosing the unprecedented course of walking home. It
1262: was now nearly four o’clock, and a mist was gathering: the sooner he
1263: got into the road the better. He remembered having crossed the road and
1264: seen the finger-post only a little while before Wildfire broke down;
1265: so, buttoning his coat, twisting the lash of his hunting-whip compactly
1266: round the handle, and rapping the tops of his boots with a
1267: self-possessed air, as if to assure himself that he was not at all
1268: taken by surprise, he set off with the sense that he was undertaking a
1269: remarkable feat of bodily exertion, which somehow and at some time he
1270: should be able to dress up and magnify to the admiration of a select
1271: circle at the Rainbow. When a young gentleman like Dunsey is reduced to
1272: so exceptional a mode of locomotion as walking, a whip in his hand is a
1273: desirable corrective to a too bewildering dreamy sense of unwontedness
1274: in his position; and Dunstan, as he went along through the gathering
1275: mist, was always rapping his whip somewhere. It was Godfrey’s whip,
1276: which he had chosen to take without leave because it had a gold handle;
1277: of course no one could see, when Dunstan held it, that the name
1278: _Godfrey Cass_ was cut in deep letters on that gold handle—they could
1279: only see that it was a very handsome whip. Dunsey was not without fear
1280: that he might meet some acquaintance in whose eyes he would cut a
1281: pitiable figure, for mist is no screen when people get close to each
1282: other; but when he at last found himself in the well-known Raveloe
1283: lanes without having met a soul, he silently remarked that that was
1284: part of his usual good luck. But now the mist, helped by the evening
1285: darkness, was more of a screen than he desired, for it hid the ruts
1286: into which his feet were liable to slip—hid everything, so that he had
1287: to guide his steps by dragging his whip along the low bushes in advance
1288: of the hedgerow. He must soon, he thought, be getting near the opening
1289: at the Stone-pits: he should find it out by the break in the hedgerow.
1290: He found it out, however, by another circumstance which he had not
1291: expected—namely, by certain gleams of light, which he presently guessed
1292: to proceed from Silas Marner’s cottage. That cottage and the money
1293: hidden within it had been in his mind continually during his walk, and
1294: he had been imagining ways of cajoling and tempting the weaver to part
1295: with the immediate possession of his money for the sake of receiving
1296: interest. Dunstan felt as if there must be a little frightening added
1297: to the cajolery, for his own arithmetical convictions were not clear
1298: enough to afford him any forcible demonstration as to the advantages of
1299: interest; and as for security, he regarded it vaguely as a means of
1300: cheating a man by making him believe that he would be paid. Altogether,
1301: the operation on the miser’s mind was a task that Godfrey would be sure
1302: to hand over to his more daring and cunning brother: Dunstan had made
1303: up his mind to that; and by the time he saw the light gleaming through
1304: the chinks of Marner’s shutters, the idea of a dialogue with the weaver
1305: had become so familiar to him, that it occurred to him as quite a
1306: natural thing to make the acquaintance forthwith. There might be
1307: several conveniences attending this course: the weaver had possibly got
1308: a lantern, and Dunstan was tired of feeling his way. He was still
1309: nearly three-quarters of a mile from home, and the lane was becoming
1310: unpleasantly slippery, for the mist was passing into rain. He turned up
1311: the bank, not without some fear lest he might miss the right way, since
1312: he was not certain whether the light were in front or on the side of
1313: the cottage. But he felt the ground before him cautiously with his
1314: whip-handle, and at last arrived safely at the door. He knocked loudly,
1315: rather enjoying the idea that the old fellow would be frightened at the
1316: sudden noise. He heard no movement in reply: all was silence in the
1317: cottage. Was the weaver gone to bed, then? If so, why had he left a
1318: light? That was a strange forgetfulness in a miser. Dunstan knocked
1319: still more loudly, and, without pausing for a reply, pushed his fingers
1320: through the latch-hole, intending to shake the door and pull the
1321: latch-string up and down, not doubting that the door was fastened. But,
1322: to his surprise, at this double motion the door opened, and he found
1323: himself in front of a bright fire which lit up every corner of the
1324: cottage—the bed, the loom, the three chairs, and the table—and showed
1325: him that Marner was not there.
1326: 
1327: Nothing at that moment could be much more inviting to Dunsey than the
1328: bright fire on the brick hearth: he walked in and seated himself by it
1329: at once. There was something in front of the fire, too, that would have
1330: been inviting to a hungry man, if it had been in a different stage of
1331: cooking. It was a small bit of pork suspended from the kettle-hanger by
1332: a string passed through a large door-key, in a way known to primitive
1333: housekeepers unpossessed of jacks. But the pork had been hung at the
1334: farthest extremity of the hanger, apparently to prevent the roasting
1335: from proceeding too rapidly during the owner’s absence. The old staring
1336: simpleton had hot meat for his supper, then? thought Dunstan. People
1337: had always said he lived on mouldy bread, on purpose to check his
1338: appetite. But where could he be at this time, and on such an evening,
1339: leaving his supper in this stage of preparation, and his door
1340: unfastened? Dunstan’s own recent difficulty in making his way suggested
1341: to him that the weaver had perhaps gone outside his cottage to fetch in
1342: fuel, or for some such brief purpose, and had slipped into the
1343: Stone-pit. That was an interesting idea to Dunstan, carrying
1344: consequences of entire novelty. If the weaver was dead, who had a right
1345: to his money? Who would know where his money was hidden? _Who would
1346: know that anybody had come to take it away?_ He went no farther into
1347: the subtleties of evidence: the pressing question, “Where _is_ the
1348: money?” now took such entire possession of him as to make him quite
1349: forget that the weaver’s death was not a certainty. A dull mind, once
1350: arriving at an inference that flatters a desire, is rarely able to
1351: retain the impression that the notion from which the inference started
1352: was purely problematic. And Dunstan’s mind was as dull as the mind of a
1353: possible felon usually is. There were only three hiding-places where he
1354: had ever heard of cottagers’ hoards being found: the thatch, the bed,
1355: and a hole in the floor. Marner’s cottage had no thatch; and Dunstan’s
1356: first act, after a train of thought made rapid by the stimulus of
1357: cupidity, was to go up to the bed; but while he did so, his eyes
1358: travelled eagerly over the floor, where the bricks, distinct in the
1359: fire-light, were discernible under the sprinkling of sand. But not
1360: everywhere; for there was one spot, and one only, which was quite
1361: covered with sand, and sand showing the marks of fingers, which had
1362: apparently been careful to spread it over a given space. It was near
1363: the treddles of the loom. In an instant Dunstan darted to that spot,
1364: swept away the sand with his whip, and, inserting the thin end of the
1365: hook between the bricks, found that they were loose. In haste he lifted
1366: up two bricks, and saw what he had no doubt was the object of his
1367: search; for what could there be but money in those two leathern bags?
1368: And, from their weight, they must be filled with guineas. Dunstan felt
1369: round the hole, to be certain that it held no more; then hastily
1370: replaced the bricks, and spread the sand over them. Hardly more than
1371: five minutes had passed since he entered the cottage, but it seemed to
1372: Dunstan like a long while; and though he was without any distinct
1373: recognition of the possibility that Marner might be alive, and might
1374: re-enter the cottage at any moment, he felt an undefinable dread laying
1375: hold on him, as he rose to his feet with the bags in his hand. He would
1376: hasten out into the darkness, and then consider what he should do with
1377: the bags. He closed the door behind him immediately, that he might shut
1378: in the stream of light: a few steps would be enough to carry him beyond
1379: betrayal by the gleams from the shutter-chinks and the latch-hole. The
1380: rain and darkness had got thicker, and he was glad of it; though it was
1381: awkward walking with both hands filled, so that it was as much as he
1382: could do to grasp his whip along with one of the bags. But when he had
1383: gone a yard or two, he might take his time. So he stepped forward into
1384: the darkness.
1385: 
1386: 
1387: 
1388: CHAPTER V.
1389: 
1390: When Dunstan Cass turned his back on the cottage, Silas Marner was not
1391: more than a hundred yards away from it, plodding along from the village
1392: with a sack thrown round his shoulders as an overcoat, and with a horn
1393: lantern in his hand. His legs were weary, but his mind was at ease,
1394: free from the presentiment of change. The sense of security more
1395: frequently springs from habit than from conviction, and for this reason
1396: it often subsists after such a change in the conditions as might have
1397: been expected to suggest alarm. The lapse of time during which a given
1398: event has not happened, is, in this logic of habit, constantly alleged
1399: as a reason why the event should never happen, even when the lapse of
1400: time is precisely the added condition which makes the event imminent. A
1401: man will tell you that he has worked in a mine for forty years unhurt
1402: by an accident as a reason why he should apprehend no danger, though
1403: the roof is beginning to sink; and it is often observable, that the
1404: older a man gets, the more difficult it is to him to retain a believing
1405: conception of his own death. This influence of habit was necessarily
1406: strong in a man whose life was so monotonous as Marner’s—who saw no new
1407: people and heard of no new events to keep alive in him the idea of the
1408: unexpected and the changeful; and it explains simply enough, why his
1409: mind could be at ease, though he had left his house and his treasure
1410: more defenceless than usual. Silas was thinking with double complacency
1411: of his supper: first, because it would be hot and savoury; and
1412: secondly, because it would cost him nothing. For the little bit of pork
1413: was a present from that excellent housewife, Miss Priscilla Lammeter,
1414: to whom he had this day carried home a handsome piece of linen; and it
1415: was only on occasion of a present like this, that Silas indulged
1416: himself with roast-meat. Supper was his favourite meal, because it came
1417: at his time of revelry, when his heart warmed over his gold; whenever
1418: he had roast-meat, he always chose to have it for supper. But this
1419: evening, he had no sooner ingeniously knotted his string fast round his
1420: bit of pork, twisted the string according to rule over his door-key,
1421: passed it through the handle, and made it fast on the hanger, than he
1422: remembered that a piece of very fine twine was indispensable to his
1423: “setting up” a new piece of work in his loom early in the morning. It
1424: had slipped his memory, because, in coming from Mr. Lammeter’s, he had
1425: not had to pass through the village; but to lose time by going on
1426: errands in the morning was out of the question. It was a nasty fog to
1427: turn out into, but there were things Silas loved better than his own
1428: comfort; so, drawing his pork to the extremity of the hanger, and
1429: arming himself with his lantern and his old sack, he set out on what,
1430: in ordinary weather, would have been a twenty minutes’ errand. He could
1431: not have locked his door without undoing his well-knotted string and
1432: retarding his supper; it was not worth his while to make that
1433: sacrifice. What thief would find his way to the Stone-pits on such a
1434: night as this? and why should he come on this particular night, when he
1435: had never come through all the fifteen years before? These questions
1436: were not distinctly present in Silas’s mind; they merely serve to
1437: represent the vaguely-felt foundation of his freedom from anxiety.
1438: 
1439: He reached his door in much satisfaction that his errand was done: he
1440: opened it, and to his short-sighted eyes everything remained as he had
1441: left it, except that the fire sent out a welcome increase of heat. He
1442: trod about the floor while putting by his lantern and throwing aside
1443: his hat and sack, so as to merge the marks of Dunstan’s feet on the
1444: sand in the marks of his own nailed boots. Then he moved his pork
1445: nearer to the fire, and sat down to the agreeable business of tending
1446: the meat and warming himself at the same time.
1447: 
1448: Any one who had looked at him as the red light shone upon his pale
1449: face, strange straining eyes, and meagre form, would perhaps have
1450: understood the mixture of contemptuous pity, dread, and suspicion with
1451: which he was regarded by his neighbours in Raveloe. Yet few men could
1452: be more harmless than poor Marner. In his truthful simple soul, not
1453: even the growing greed and worship of gold could beget any vice
1454: directly injurious to others. The light of his faith quite put out, and
1455: his affections made desolate, he had clung with all the force of his
1456: nature to his work and his money; and like all objects to which a man
1457: devotes himself, they had fashioned him into correspondence with
1458: themselves. His loom, as he wrought in it without ceasing, had in its
1459: turn wrought on him, and confirmed more and more the monotonous craving
1460: for its monotonous response. His gold, as he hung over it and saw it
1461: grow, gathered his power of loving together into a hard isolation like
1462: its own.
1463: 
1464: As soon as he was warm he began to think it would be a long while to
1465: wait till after supper before he drew out his guineas, and it would be
1466: pleasant to see them on the table before him as he ate his unwonted
1467: feast. For joy is the best of wine, and Silas’s guineas were a golden
1468: wine of that sort.
1469: 
1470: He rose and placed his candle unsuspectingly on the floor near his
1471: loom, swept away the sand without noticing any change, and removed the
1472: bricks. The sight of the empty hole made his heart leap violently, but
1473: the belief that his gold was gone could not come at once—only terror,
1474: and the eager effort to put an end to the terror. He passed his
1475: trembling hand all about the hole, trying to think it possible that his
1476: eyes had deceived him; then he held the candle in the hole and examined
1477: it curiously, trembling more and more. At last he shook so violently
1478: that he let fall the candle, and lifted his hands to his head, trying
1479: to steady himself, that he might think. Had he put his gold somewhere
1480: else, by a sudden resolution last night, and then forgotten it? A man
1481: falling into dark waters seeks a momentary footing even on sliding
1482: stones; and Silas, by acting as if he believed in false hopes, warded
1483: off the moment of despair. He searched in every corner, he turned his
1484: bed over, and shook it, and kneaded it; he looked in his brick oven
1485: where he laid his sticks. When there was no other place to be searched,
1486: he kneeled down again and felt once more all round the hole. There was
1487: no untried refuge left for a moment’s shelter from the terrible truth.
1488: 
1489: Yes, there was a sort of refuge which always comes with the prostration
1490: of thought under an overpowering passion: it was that expectation of
1491: impossibilities, that belief in contradictory images, which is still
1492: distinct from madness, because it is capable of being dissipated by the
1493: external fact. Silas got up from his knees trembling, and looked round
1494: at the table: didn’t the gold lie there after all? The table was bare.
1495: Then he turned and looked behind him—looked all round his dwelling,
1496: seeming to strain his brown eyes after some possible appearance of the
1497: bags where he had already sought them in vain. He could see every
1498: object in his cottage—and his gold was not there.
1499: 
1500: Again he put his trembling hands to his head, and gave a wild ringing
1501: scream, the cry of desolation. For a few moments after, he stood
1502: motionless; but the cry had relieved him from the first maddening
1503: pressure of the truth. He turned, and tottered towards his loom, and
1504: got into the seat where he worked, instinctively seeking this as the
1505: strongest assurance of reality.
1506: 
1507: And now that all the false hopes had vanished, and the first shock of
1508: certainty was past, the idea of a thief began to present itself, and he
1509: entertained it eagerly, because a thief might be caught and made to
1510: restore the gold. The thought brought some new strength with it, and he
1511: started from his loom to the door. As he opened it the rain beat in
1512: upon him, for it was falling more and more heavily. There were no
1513: footsteps to be tracked on such a night—footsteps? When had the thief
1514: come? During Silas’s absence in the daytime the door had been locked,
1515: and there had been no marks of any inroad on his return by daylight.
1516: And in the evening, too, he said to himself, everything was the same as
1517: when he had left it. The sand and bricks looked as if they had not been
1518: moved. _Was_ it a thief who had taken the bags? or was it a cruel power
1519: that no hands could reach, which had delighted in making him a second
1520: time desolate? He shrank from this vaguer dread, and fixed his mind
1521: with struggling effort on the robber with hands, who could be reached
1522: by hands. His thoughts glanced at all the neighbours who had made any
1523: remarks, or asked any questions which he might now regard as a ground
1524: of suspicion. There was Jem Rodney, a known poacher, and otherwise
1525: disreputable: he had often met Marner in his journeys across the
1526: fields, and had said something jestingly about the weaver’s money; nay,
1527: he had once irritated Marner, by lingering at the fire when he called
1528: to light his pipe, instead of going about his business. Jem Rodney was
1529: the man—there was ease in the thought. Jem could be found and made to
1530: restore the money: Marner did not want to punish him, but only to get
1531: back his gold which had gone from him, and left his soul like a forlorn
1532: traveller on an unknown desert. The robber must be laid hold of.
1533: Marner’s ideas of legal authority were confused, but he felt that he
1534: must go and proclaim his loss; and the great people in the village—the
1535: clergyman, the constable, and Squire Cass—would make Jem Rodney, or
1536: somebody else, deliver up the stolen money. He rushed out in the rain,
1537: under the stimulus of this hope, forgetting to cover his head, not
1538: caring to fasten his door; for he felt as if he had nothing left to
1539: lose. He ran swiftly, till want of breath compelled him to slacken his
1540: pace as he was entering the village at the turning close to the
1541: Rainbow.
1542: 
1543: The Rainbow, in Marner’s view, was a place of luxurious resort for rich
1544: and stout husbands, whose wives had superfluous stores of linen; it was
1545: the place where he was likely to find the powers and dignities of
1546: Raveloe, and where he could most speedily make his loss public. He
1547: lifted the latch, and turned into the bright bar or kitchen on the
1548: right hand, where the less lofty customers of the house were in the
1549: habit of assembling, the parlour on the left being reserved for the
1550: more select society in which Squire Cass frequently enjoyed the double
1551: pleasure of conviviality and condescension. But the parlour was dark
1552: to-night, the chief personages who ornamented its circle being all at
1553: Mrs. Osgood’s birthday dance, as Godfrey Cass was. And in consequence
1554: of this, the party on the high-screened seats in the kitchen was more
1555: numerous than usual; several personages, who would otherwise have been
1556: admitted into the parlour and enlarged the opportunity of hectoring and
1557: condescension for their betters, being content this evening to vary
1558: their enjoyment by taking their spirits-and-water where they could
1559: themselves hector and condescend in company that called for beer.
1560: 
1561: 
1562: 
1563: CHAPTER VI.
1564: 
1565: The conversation, which was at a high pitch of animation when Silas
1566: approached the door of the Rainbow, had, as usual, been slow and
1567: intermittent when the company first assembled. The pipes began to be
1568: puffed in a silence which had an air of severity; the more important
1569: customers, who drank spirits and sat nearest the fire, staring at each
1570: other as if a bet were depending on the first man who winked; while the
1571: beer-drinkers, chiefly men in fustian jackets and smock-frocks, kept
1572: their eyelids down and rubbed their hands across their mouths, as if
1573: their draughts of beer were a funereal duty attended with embarrassing
1574: sadness. At last Mr. Snell, the landlord, a man of a neutral
1575: disposition, accustomed to stand aloof from human differences as those
1576: of beings who were all alike in need of liquor, broke silence, by
1577: saying in a doubtful tone to his cousin the butcher—
1578: 
1579: “Some folks ’ud say that was a fine beast you druv in yesterday, Bob?”
1580: 
1581: The butcher, a jolly, smiling, red-haired man, was not disposed to
1582: answer rashly. He gave a few puffs before he spat and replied, “And
1583: they wouldn’t be fur wrong, John.”
1584: 
1585: After this feeble delusive thaw, the silence set in as severely as
1586: before.
1587: 
1588: “Was it a red Durham?” said the farrier, taking up the thread of
1589: discourse after the lapse of a few minutes.
1590: 
1591: The farrier looked at the landlord, and the landlord looked at the
1592: butcher, as the person who must take the responsibility of answering.
1593: 
1594: “Red it was,” said the butcher, in his good-humoured husky treble—“and
1595: a Durham it was.”
1596: 
1597: “Then you needn’t tell _me_ who you bought it of,” said the farrier,
1598: looking round with some triumph; “I know who it is has got the red
1599: Durhams o’ this country-side. And she’d a white star on her brow, I’ll
1600: bet a penny?” The farrier leaned forward with his hands on his knees as
1601: he put this question, and his eyes twinkled knowingly.
1602: 
1603: “Well; yes—she might,” said the butcher, slowly, considering that he
1604: was giving a decided affirmative. “I don’t say contrairy.”
1605: 
1606: “I knew that very well,” said the farrier, throwing himself backward
1607: again, and speaking defiantly; “if _I_ don’t know Mr. Lammeter’s cows,
1608: I should like to know who does—that’s all. And as for the cow you’ve
1609: bought, bargain or no bargain, I’ve been at the drenching of
1610: her—contradick me who will.”
1611: 
1612: The farrier looked fierce, and the mild butcher’s conversational spirit
1613: was roused a little.
1614: 
1615: “I’m not for contradicking no man,” he said; “I’m for peace and
1616: quietness. Some are for cutting long ribs—I’m for cutting ’em short
1617: myself; but _I_ don’t quarrel with ’em. All I say is, it’s a lovely
1618: carkiss—and anybody as was reasonable, it ’ud bring tears into their
1619: eyes to look at it.”
1620: 
1621: “Well, it’s the cow as I drenched, whatever it is,” pursued the
1622: farrier, angrily; “and it was Mr. Lammeter’s cow, else you told a lie
1623: when you said it was a red Durham.”
1624: 
1625: “I tell no lies,” said the butcher, with the same mild huskiness as
1626: before, “and I contradick none—not if a man was to swear himself black:
1627: he’s no meat o’ mine, nor none o’ my bargains. All I say is, it’s a
1628: lovely carkiss. And what I say, I’ll stick to; but I’ll quarrel wi’ no
1629: man.”
1630: 
1631: “No,” said the farrier, with bitter sarcasm, looking at the company
1632: generally; “and p’rhaps you aren’t pig-headed; and p’rhaps you didn’t
1633: say the cow was a red Durham; and p’rhaps you didn’t say she’d got a
1634: star on her brow—stick to that, now you’re at it.”
1635: 
1636: “Come, come,” said the landlord; “let the cow alone. The truth lies
1637: atween you: you’re both right and both wrong, as I allays say. And as
1638: for the cow’s being Mr. Lammeter’s, I say nothing to that; but this I
1639: say, as the Rainbow’s the Rainbow. And for the matter o’ that, if the
1640: talk is to be o’ the Lammeters, _you_ know the most upo’ that head, eh,
1641: Mr. Macey? You remember when first Mr. Lammeter’s father come into
1642: these parts, and took the Warrens?”
1643: 
1644: Mr. Macey, tailor and parish-clerk, the latter of which functions
1645: rheumatism had of late obliged him to share with a small-featured young
1646: man who sat opposite him, held his white head on one side, and twirled
1647: his thumbs with an air of complacency, slightly seasoned with
1648: criticism. He smiled pityingly, in answer to the landlord’s appeal, and
1649: said—
1650: 
1651: “Aye, aye; I know, I know; but I let other folks talk. I’ve laid by
1652: now, and gev up to the young uns. Ask them as have been to school at
1653: Tarley: they’ve learnt pernouncing; that’s come up since my day.”
1654: 
1655: “If you’re pointing at me, Mr. Macey,” said the deputy clerk, with an
1656: air of anxious propriety, “I’m nowise a man to speak out of my place.
1657: As the psalm says—
1658: 
1659: “I know what’s right, nor only so,
1660: But also practise what I know.”
1661: 
1662: 
1663: “Well, then, I wish you’d keep hold o’ the tune, when it’s set for you;
1664: if you’re for prac_tis_ing, I wish you’d prac_tise_ that,” said a large
1665: jocose-looking man, an excellent wheelwright in his week-day capacity,
1666: but on Sundays leader of the choir. He winked, as he spoke, at two of
1667: the company, who were known officially as the “bassoon” and the
1668: “key-bugle”, in the confidence that he was expressing the sense of the
1669: musical profession in Raveloe.
1670: 
1671: Mr. Tookey, the deputy-clerk, who shared the unpopularity common to
1672: deputies, turned very red, but replied, with careful moderation—“Mr.
1673: Winthrop, if you’ll bring me any proof as I’m in the wrong, I’m not the
1674: man to say I won’t alter. But there’s people set up their own ears for
1675: a standard, and expect the whole choir to follow ’em. There may be two
1676: opinions, I hope.”
1677: 
1678: “Aye, aye,” said Mr. Macey, who felt very well satisfied with this
1679: attack on youthful presumption; “you’re right there, Tookey: there’s
1680: allays two ’pinions; there’s the ’pinion a man has of himsen, and
1681: there’s the ’pinion other folks have on him. There’d be two ’pinions
1682: about a cracked bell, if the bell could hear itself.”
1683: 
1684: “Well, Mr. Macey,” said poor Tookey, serious amidst the general
1685: laughter, “I undertook to partially fill up the office of parish-clerk
1686: by Mr. Crackenthorp’s desire, whenever your infirmities should make you
1687: unfitting; and it’s one of the rights thereof to sing in the choir—else
1688: why have you done the same yourself?”
1689: 
1690: “Ah! but the old gentleman and you are two folks,” said Ben Winthrop.
1691: “The old gentleman’s got a gift. Why, the Squire used to invite him to
1692: take a glass, only to hear him sing the “Red Rovier”; didn’t he, Mr.
1693: Macey? It’s a nat’ral gift. There’s my little lad Aaron, he’s got a
1694: gift—he can sing a tune off straight, like a throstle. But as for you,
1695: Master Tookey, you’d better stick to your “Amens”: your voice is well
1696: enough when you keep it up in your nose. It’s your inside as isn’t
1697: right made for music: it’s no better nor a hollow stalk.”
1698: 
1699: This kind of unflinching frankness was the most piquant form of joke to
1700: the company at the Rainbow, and Ben Winthrop’s insult was felt by
1701: everybody to have capped Mr. Macey’s epigram.
1702: 
1703: “I see what it is plain enough,” said Mr. Tookey, unable to keep cool
1704: any longer. “There’s a consperacy to turn me out o’ the choir, as I
1705: shouldn’t share the Christmas money—that’s where it is. But I shall
1706: speak to Mr. Crackenthorp; I’ll not be put upon by no man.”
1707: 
1708: “Nay, nay, Tookey,” said Ben Winthrop. “We’ll pay you your share to
1709: keep out of it—that’s what we’ll do. There’s things folks ’ud pay to be
1710: rid on, besides varmin.”
1711: 
1712: “Come, come,” said the landlord, who felt that paying people for their
1713: absence was a principle dangerous to society; “a joke’s a joke. We’re
1714: all good friends here, I hope. We must give and take. You’re both right
1715: and you’re both wrong, as I say. I agree wi’ Mr. Macey here, as there’s
1716: two opinions; and if mine was asked, I should say they’re both right.
1717: Tookey’s right and Winthrop’s right, and they’ve only got to split the
1718: difference and make themselves even.”
1719: 
1720: The farrier was puffing his pipe rather fiercely, in some contempt at
1721: this trivial discussion. He had no ear for music himself, and never
1722: went to church, as being of the medical profession, and likely to be in
1723: requisition for delicate cows. But the butcher, having music in his
1724: soul, had listened with a divided desire for Tookey’s defeat and for
1725: the preservation of the peace.
1726: 
1727: “To be sure,” he said, following up the landlord’s conciliatory view,
1728: “we’re fond of our old clerk; it’s nat’ral, and him used to be such a
1729: singer, and got a brother as is known for the first fiddler in this
1730: country-side. Eh, it’s a pity but what Solomon lived in our village,
1731: and could give us a tune when we liked; eh, Mr. Macey? I’d keep him in
1732: liver and lights for nothing—that I would.”
1733: 
1734: “Aye, aye,” said Mr. Macey, in the height of complacency; “our family’s
1735: been known for musicianers as far back as anybody can tell. But them
1736: things are dying out, as I tell Solomon every time he comes round;
1737: there’s no voices like what there used to be, and there’s nobody
1738: remembers what we remember, if it isn’t the old crows.”
1739: 
1740: “Aye, you remember when first Mr. Lammeter’s father come into these
1741: parts, don’t you, Mr. Macey?” said the landlord.
1742: 
1743: “I should think I did,” said the old man, who had now gone through that
1744: complimentary process necessary to bring him up to the point of
1745: narration; “and a fine old gentleman he was—as fine, and finer nor the
1746: Mr. Lammeter as now is. He came from a bit north’ard, so far as I could
1747: ever make out. But there’s nobody rightly knows about those parts: only
1748: it couldn’t be far north’ard, nor much different from this country, for
1749: he brought a fine breed o’ sheep with him, so there must be pastures
1750: there, and everything reasonable. We heared tell as he’d sold his own
1751: land to come and take the Warrens, and that seemed odd for a man as had
1752: land of his own, to come and rent a farm in a strange place. But they
1753: said it was along of his wife’s dying; though there’s reasons in things
1754: as nobody knows on—that’s pretty much what I’ve made out; yet some
1755: folks are so wise, they’ll find you fifty reasons straight off, and all
1756: the while the real reason’s winking at ’em in the corner, and they
1757: niver see’t. Howsomever, it was soon seen as we’d got a new parish’ner
1758: as know’d the rights and customs o’ things, and kep a good house, and
1759: was well looked on by everybody. And the young man—that’s the Mr.
1760: Lammeter as now is, for he’d niver a sister—soon begun to court Miss
1761: Osgood, that’s the sister o’ the Mr. Osgood as now is, and a fine
1762: handsome lass she was—eh, you can’t think—they pretend this young lass
1763: is like her, but that’s the way wi’ people as don’t know what come
1764: before ’em. _I_ should know, for I helped the old rector, Mr. Drumlow
1765: as was, I helped him marry ’em.”
1766: 
1767: Here Mr. Macey paused; he always gave his narrative in instalments,
1768: expecting to be questioned according to precedent.
1769: 
1770: “Aye, and a partic’lar thing happened, didn’t it, Mr. Macey, so as you
1771: were likely to remember that marriage?” said the landlord, in a
1772: congratulatory tone.
1773: 
1774: “I should think there did—a _very_ partic’lar thing,” said Mr. Macey,
1775: nodding sideways. “For Mr. Drumlow—poor old gentleman, I was fond on
1776: him, though he’d got a bit confused in his head, what wi’ age and wi’
1777: taking a drop o’ summat warm when the service come of a cold morning.
1778: And young Mr. Lammeter, he’d have no way but he must be married in
1779: Janiwary, which, to be sure, ’s a unreasonable time to be married in,
1780: for it isn’t like a christening or a burying, as you can’t help; and so
1781: Mr. Drumlow—poor old gentleman, I was fond on him—but when he come to
1782: put the questions, he put ’em by the rule o’ contrairy, like, and he
1783: says, “Wilt thou have this man to thy wedded wife?” says he, and then
1784: he says, “Wilt thou have this woman to thy wedded husband?” says he.
1785: But the partic’larest thing of all is, as nobody took any notice on it
1786: but me, and they answered straight off “yes”, like as if it had been me
1787: saying “Amen” i’ the right place, without listening to what went
1788: before.”
1789: 
1790: “But _you_ knew what was going on well enough, didn’t you, Mr. Macey?
1791: You were live enough, eh?” said the butcher.
1792: 
1793: “Lor bless you!” said Mr. Macey, pausing, and smiling in pity at the
1794: impotence of his hearer’s imagination—“why, I was all of a tremble: it
1795: was as if I’d been a coat pulled by the two tails, like; for I couldn’t
1796: stop the parson, I couldn’t take upon me to do that; and yet I said to
1797: myself, I says, “Suppose they shouldn’t be fast married, ’cause the
1798: words are contrairy?” and my head went working like a mill, for I was
1799: allays uncommon for turning things over and seeing all round ’em; and I
1800: says to myself, “Is’t the meanin’ or the words as makes folks fast i’
1801: wedlock?” For the parson meant right, and the bride and bridegroom
1802: meant right. But then, when I come to think on it, meanin’ goes but a
1803: little way i’ most things, for you may mean to stick things together
1804: and your glue may be bad, and then where are you? And so I says to
1805: mysen, “It isn’t the meanin’, it’s the glue.” And I was worreted as if
1806: I’d got three bells to pull at once, when we went into the vestry, and
1807: they begun to sign their names. But where’s the use o’ talking?—you
1808: can’t think what goes on in a ’cute man’s inside.”
1809: 
1810: “But you held in for all that, didn’t you, Mr. Macey?” said the
1811: landlord.
1812: 
1813: “Aye, I held in tight till I was by mysen wi’ Mr. Drumlow, and then I
1814: out wi’ everything, but respectful, as I allays did. And he made light
1815: on it, and he says, “Pooh, pooh, Macey, make yourself easy,” he says;
1816: “it’s neither the meaning nor the words—it’s the re_ges_ter does
1817: it—that’s the glue.” So you see he settled it easy; for parsons and
1818: doctors know everything by heart, like, so as they aren’t worreted wi’
1819: thinking what’s the rights and wrongs o’ things, as I’n been many and
1820: many’s the time. And sure enough the wedding turned out all right, on’y
1821: poor Mrs. Lammeter—that’s Miss Osgood as was—died afore the lasses was
1822: growed up; but for prosperity and everything respectable, there’s no
1823: family more looked on.”
1824: 
1825: Every one of Mr. Macey’s audience had heard this story many times, but
1826: it was listened to as if it had been a favourite tune, and at certain
1827: points the puffing of the pipes was momentarily suspended, that the
1828: listeners might give their whole minds to the expected words. But there
1829: was more to come; and Mr. Snell, the landlord, duly put the leading
1830: question.
1831: 
1832: “Why, old Mr. Lammeter had a pretty fortin, didn’t they say, when he
1833: come into these parts?”
1834: 
1835: “Well, yes,” said Mr. Macey; “but I daresay it’s as much as this Mr.
1836: Lammeter’s done to keep it whole. For there was allays a talk as nobody
1837: could get rich on the Warrens: though he holds it cheap, for it’s what
1838: they call Charity Land.”
1839: 
1840: “Aye, and there’s few folks know so well as you how it come to be
1841: Charity Land, eh, Mr. Macey?” said the butcher.
1842: 
1843: “How should they?” said the old clerk, with some contempt. “Why, my
1844: grandfather made the grooms’ livery for that Mr. Cliff as came and
1845: built the big stables at the Warrens. Why, they’re stables four times
1846: as big as Squire Cass’s, for he thought o’ nothing but hosses and
1847: hunting, Cliff didn’t—a Lunnon tailor, some folks said, as had gone mad
1848: wi’ cheating. For he couldn’t ride; lor bless you! they said he’d got
1849: no more grip o’ the hoss than if his legs had been cross-sticks: my
1850: grandfather heared old Squire Cass say so many and many a time. But
1851: ride he would, as if Old Harry had been a-driving him; and he’d a son,
1852: a lad o’ sixteen; and nothing would his father have him do, but he must
1853: ride and ride—though the lad was frighted, they said. And it was a
1854: common saying as the father wanted to ride the tailor out o’ the lad,
1855: and make a gentleman on him—not but what I’m a tailor myself, but in
1856: respect as God made me such, I’m proud on it, for “Macey, tailor”, ’s
1857: been wrote up over our door since afore the Queen’s heads went out on
1858: the shillings. But Cliff, he was ashamed o’ being called a tailor, and
1859: he was sore vexed as his riding was laughed at, and nobody o’ the
1860: gentlefolks hereabout could abide him. Howsomever, the poor lad got
1861: sickly and died, and the father didn’t live long after him, for he got
1862: queerer nor ever, and they said he used to go out i’ the dead o’ the
1863: night, wi’ a lantern in his hand, to the stables, and set a lot o’
1864: lights burning, for he got as he couldn’t sleep; and there he’d stand,
1865: cracking his whip and looking at his hosses; and they said it was a
1866: mercy as the stables didn’t get burnt down wi’ the poor dumb creaturs
1867: in ’em. But at last he died raving, and they found as he’d left all his
1868: property, Warrens and all, to a Lunnon Charity, and that’s how the
1869: Warrens come to be Charity Land; though, as for the stables, Mr.
1870: Lammeter never uses ’em—they’re out o’ all charicter—lor bless you! if
1871: you was to set the doors a-banging in ’em, it ’ud sound like thunder
1872: half o’er the parish.”
1873: 
1874: “Aye, but there’s more going on in the stables than what folks see by
1875: daylight, eh, Mr. Macey?” said the landlord.
1876: 
1877: “Aye, aye; go that way of a dark night, that’s all,” said Mr. Macey,
1878: winking mysteriously, “and then make believe, if you like, as you
1879: didn’t see lights i’ the stables, nor hear the stamping o’ the hosses,
1880: nor the cracking o’ the whips, and howling, too, if it’s tow’rt
1881: daybreak. “Cliff’s Holiday” has been the name of it ever sin’ I were a
1882: boy; that’s to say, some said as it was the holiday Old Harry gev him
1883: from roasting, like. That’s what my father told me, and he was a
1884: reasonable man, though there’s folks nowadays know what happened afore
1885: they were born better nor they know their own business.”
1886: 
1887: “What do you say to that, eh, Dowlas?” said the landlord, turning to
1888: the farrier, who was swelling with impatience for his cue. “There’s a
1889: nut for _you_ to crack.”
1890: 
1891: Mr. Dowlas was the negative spirit in the company, and was proud of his
1892: position.
1893: 
1894: “Say? I say what a man _should_ say as doesn’t shut his eyes to look at
1895: a finger-post. I say, as I’m ready to wager any man ten pound, if he’ll
1896: stand out wi’ me any dry night in the pasture before the Warren
1897: stables, as we shall neither see lights nor hear noises, if it isn’t
1898: the blowing of our own noses. That’s what I say, and I’ve said it many
1899: a time; but there’s nobody ’ull ventur a ten-pun’ note on their ghos’es
1900: as they make so sure of.”
1901: 
1902: “Why, Dowlas, that’s easy betting, that is,” said Ben Winthrop. “You
1903: might as well bet a man as he wouldn’t catch the rheumatise if he stood
1904: up to ’s neck in the pool of a frosty night. It ’ud be fine fun for a
1905: man to win his bet as he’d catch the rheumatise. Folks as believe in
1906: Cliff’s Holiday aren’t agoing to ventur near it for a matter o’ ten
1907: pound.”
1908: 
1909: “If Master Dowlas wants to know the truth on it,” said Mr. Macey, with
1910: a sarcastic smile, tapping his thumbs together, “he’s no call to lay
1911: any bet—let him go and stan’ by himself—there’s nobody ’ull hinder him;
1912: and then he can let the parish’ners know if they’re wrong.”
1913: 
1914: “Thank you! I’m obliged to you,” said the farrier, with a snort of
1915: scorn. “If folks are fools, it’s no business o’ mine. _I_ don’t want to
1916: make out the truth about ghos’es: I know it a’ready. But I’m not
1917: against a bet—everything fair and open. Let any man bet me ten pound as
1918: I shall see Cliff’s Holiday, and I’ll go and stand by myself. I want no
1919: company. I’d as lief do it as I’d fill this pipe.”
1920: 
1921: “Ah, but who’s to watch you, Dowlas, and see you do it? That’s no fair
1922: bet,” said the butcher.
1923: 
1924: “No fair bet?” replied Mr. Dowlas, angrily. “I should like to hear any
1925: man stand up and say I want to bet unfair. Come now, Master Lundy, I
1926: should like to hear you say it.”
1927: 
1928: “Very like you would,” said the butcher. “But it’s no business o’ mine.
1929: You’re none o’ my bargains, and I aren’t a-going to try and ’bate your
1930: price. If anybody ’ll bid for you at your own vallying, let him. I’m
1931: for peace and quietness, I am.”
1932: 
1933: “Yes, that’s what every yapping cur is, when you hold a stick up at
1934: him,” said the farrier. “But I’m afraid o’ neither man nor ghost, and
1935: I’m ready to lay a fair bet. _I_ aren’t a turn-tail cur.”
1936: 
1937: “Aye, but there’s this in it, Dowlas,” said the landlord, speaking in a
1938: tone of much candour and tolerance. “There’s folks, i’ my opinion, they
1939: can’t see ghos’es, not if they stood as plain as a pike-staff before
1940: ’em. And there’s reason i’ that. For there’s my wife, now, can’t smell,
1941: not if she’d the strongest o’ cheese under her nose. I never see’d a
1942: ghost myself; but then I says to myself, “Very like I haven’t got the
1943: smell for ’em.” I mean, putting a ghost for a smell, or else
1944: contrairiways. And so, I’m for holding with both sides; for, as I say,
1945: the truth lies between ’em. And if Dowlas was to go and stand, and say
1946: he’d never seen a wink o’ Cliff’s Holiday all the night through, I’d
1947: back him; and if anybody said as Cliff’s Holiday was certain sure, for
1948: all that, I’d back _him_ too. For the smell’s what I go by.”
1949: 
1950: The landlord’s analogical argument was not well received by the
1951: farrier—a man intensely opposed to compromise.
1952: 
1953: “Tut, tut,” he said, setting down his glass with refreshed irritation;
1954: “what’s the smell got to do with it? Did ever a ghost give a man a
1955: black eye? That’s what I should like to know. If ghos’es want me to
1956: believe in ’em, let ’em leave off skulking i’ the dark and i’ lone
1957: places—let ’em come where there’s company and candles.”
1958: 
1959: “As if ghos’es ’ud want to be believed in by anybody so ignirant!” said
1960: Mr. Macey, in deep disgust at the farrier’s crass incompetence to
1961: apprehend the conditions of ghostly phenomena.
1962: 
1963: 
1964: 
1965: CHAPTER VII.
1966: 
1967: Yet the next moment there seemed to be some evidence that ghosts had a
1968: more condescending disposition than Mr. Macey attributed to them; for
1969: the pale thin figure of Silas Marner was suddenly seen standing in the
1970: warm light, uttering no word, but looking round at the company with his
1971: strange unearthly eyes. The long pipes gave a simultaneous movement,
1972: like the antennae of startled insects, and every man present, not
1973: excepting even the sceptical farrier, had an impression that he saw,
1974: not Silas Marner in the flesh, but an apparition; for the door by which
1975: Silas had entered was hidden by the high-screened seats, and no one had
1976: noticed his approach. Mr. Macey, sitting a long way off the ghost,
1977: might be supposed to have felt an argumentative triumph, which would
1978: tend to neutralize his share of the general alarm. Had he not always
1979: said that when Silas Marner was in that strange trance of his, his soul
1980: went loose from his body? Here was the demonstration: nevertheless, on
1981: the whole, he would have been as well contented without it. For a few
1982: moments there was a dead silence, Marner’s want of breath and agitation
1983: not allowing him to speak. The landlord, under the habitual sense that
1984: he was bound to keep his house open to all company, and confident in
1985: the protection of his unbroken neutrality, at last took on himself the
1986: task of adjuring the ghost.
1987: 
1988: “Master Marner,” he said, in a conciliatory tone, “what’s lacking to
1989: you? What’s your business here?”
1990: 
1991: “Robbed!” said Silas, gaspingly. “I’ve been robbed! I want the
1992: constable—and the Justice—and Squire Cass—and Mr. Crackenthorp.”
1993: 
1994: “Lay hold on him, Jem Rodney,” said the landlord, the idea of a ghost
1995: subsiding; “he’s off his head, I doubt. He’s wet through.”
1996: 
1997: Jem Rodney was the outermost man, and sat conveniently near Marner’s
1998: standing-place; but he declined to give his services.
1999: 
2000: “Come and lay hold on him yourself, Mr. Snell, if you’ve a mind,” said
2001: Jem, rather sullenly. “He’s been robbed, and murdered too, for what I
2002: know,” he added, in a muttering tone.
2003: 
2004: “Jem Rodney!” said Silas, turning and fixing his strange eyes on the
2005: suspected man.
2006: 
2007: “Aye, Master Marner, what do you want wi’ me?” said Jem, trembling a
2008: little, and seizing his drinking-can as a defensive weapon.
2009: 
2010: “If it was you stole my money,” said Silas, clasping his hands
2011: entreatingly, and raising his voice to a cry, “give it me back—and I
2012: won’t meddle with you. I won’t set the constable on you. Give it me
2013: back, and I’ll let you—I’ll let you have a guinea.”
2014: 
2015: “Me stole your money!” said Jem, angrily. “I’ll pitch this can at your
2016: eye if you talk o’ _my_ stealing your money.”
2017: 
2018: “Come, come, Master Marner,” said the landlord, now rising resolutely,
2019: and seizing Marner by the shoulder, “if you’ve got any information to
2020: lay, speak it out sensible, and show as you’re in your right mind, if
2021: you expect anybody to listen to you. You’re as wet as a drownded rat.
2022: Sit down and dry yourself, and speak straight forrard.”
2023: 
2024: “Ah, to be sure, man,” said the farrier, who began to feel that he had
2025: not been quite on a par with himself and the occasion. “Let’s have no
2026: more staring and screaming, else we’ll have you strapped for a madman.
2027: That was why I didn’t speak at the first—thinks I, the man’s run mad.”
2028: 
2029: “Aye, aye, make him sit down,” said several voices at once, well
2030: pleased that the reality of ghosts remained still an open question.
2031: 
2032: The landlord forced Marner to take off his coat, and then to sit down
2033: on a chair aloof from every one else, in the centre of the circle and
2034: in the direct rays of the fire. The weaver, too feeble to have any
2035: distinct purpose beyond that of getting help to recover his money,
2036: submitted unresistingly. The transient fears of the company were now
2037: forgotten in their strong curiosity, and all faces were turned towards
2038: Silas, when the landlord, having seated himself again, said—
2039: 
2040: “Now then, Master Marner, what’s this you’ve got to say—as you’ve been
2041: robbed? Speak out.”
2042: 
2043: “He’d better not say again as it was me robbed him,” cried Jem Rodney,
2044: hastily. “What could I ha’ done with his money? I could as easy steal
2045: the parson’s surplice, and wear it.”
2046: 
2047: “Hold your tongue, Jem, and let’s hear what he’s got to say,” said the
2048: landlord. “Now then, Master Marner.”
2049: 
2050: Silas now told his story, under frequent questioning as the mysterious
2051: character of the robbery became evident.
2052: 
2053: This strangely novel situation of opening his trouble to his Raveloe
2054: neighbours, of sitting in the warmth of a hearth not his own, and
2055: feeling the presence of faces and voices which were his nearest promise
2056: of help, had doubtless its influence on Marner, in spite of his
2057: passionate preoccupation with his loss. Our consciousness rarely
2058: registers the beginning of a growth within us any more than without us:
2059: there have been many circulations of the sap before we detect the
2060: smallest sign of the bud.
2061: 
2062: The slight suspicion with which his hearers at first listened to him,
2063: gradually melted away before the convincing simplicity of his distress:
2064: it was impossible for the neighbours to doubt that Marner was telling
2065: the truth, not because they were capable of arguing at once from the
2066: nature of his statements to the absence of any motive for making them
2067: falsely, but because, as Mr. Macey observed, “Folks as had the devil to
2068: back ’em were not likely to be so mushed” as poor Silas was. Rather,
2069: from the strange fact that the robber had left no traces, and had
2070: happened to know the nick of time, utterly incalculable by mortal
2071: agents, when Silas would go away from home without locking his door,
2072: the more probable conclusion seemed to be, that his disreputable
2073: intimacy in that quarter, if it ever existed, had been broken up, and
2074: that, in consequence, this ill turn had been done to Marner by somebody
2075: it was quite in vain to set the constable after. Why this preternatural
2076: felon should be obliged to wait till the door was left unlocked, was a
2077: question which did not present itself.
2078: 
2079: “It isn’t Jem Rodney as has done this work, Master Marner,” said the
2080: landlord. “You mustn’t be a-casting your eye at poor Jem. There may be
2081: a bit of a reckoning against Jem for the matter of a hare or so, if
2082: anybody was bound to keep their eyes staring open, and niver to wink;
2083: but Jem’s been a-sitting here drinking his can, like the decentest man
2084: i’ the parish, since before you left your house, Master Marner, by your
2085: own account.”
2086: 
2087: “Aye, aye,” said Mr. Macey; “let’s have no accusing o’ the innicent.
2088: That isn’t the law. There must be folks to swear again’ a man before he
2089: can be ta’en up. Let’s have no accusing o’ the innicent, Master
2090: Marner.”
2091: 
2092: Memory was not so utterly torpid in Silas that it could not be awakened
2093: by these words. With a movement of compunction as new and strange to
2094: him as everything else within the last hour, he started from his chair
2095: and went close up to Jem, looking at him as if he wanted to assure
2096: himself of the expression in his face.
2097: 
2098: “I was wrong,” he said—“yes, yes—I ought to have thought. There’s
2099: nothing to witness against you, Jem. Only you’d been into my house
2100: oftener than anybody else, and so you came into my head. I don’t accuse
2101: you—I won’t accuse anybody—only,” he added, lifting up his hands to his
2102: head, and turning away with bewildered misery, “I try—I try to think
2103: where my guineas can be.”
2104: 
2105: “Aye, aye, they’re gone where it’s hot enough to melt ’em, I doubt,”
2106: said Mr. Macey.
2107: 
2108: “Tchuh!” said the farrier. And then he asked, with a cross-examining
2109: air, “How much money might there be in the bags, Master Marner?”
2110: 
2111: “Two hundred and seventy-two pounds, twelve and sixpence, last night
2112: when I counted it,” said Silas, seating himself again, with a groan.
2113: 
2114: “Pooh! why, they’d be none so heavy to carry. Some tramp’s been in,
2115: that’s all; and as for the no footmarks, and the bricks and the sand
2116: being all right—why, your eyes are pretty much like a insect’s, Master
2117: Marner; they’re obliged to look so close, you can’t see much at a time.
2118: It’s my opinion as, if I’d been you, or you’d been me—for it comes to
2119: the same thing—you wouldn’t have thought you’d found everything as you
2120: left it. But what I vote is, as two of the sensiblest o’ the company
2121: should go with you to Master Kench, the constable’s—he’s ill i’ bed, I
2122: know that much—and get him to appoint one of us his deppity; for that’s
2123: the law, and I don’t think anybody ’ull take upon him to contradick me
2124: there. It isn’t much of a walk to Kench’s; and then, if it’s me as is
2125: deppity, I’ll go back with you, Master Marner, and examine your
2126: premises; and if anybody’s got any fault to find with that, I’ll thank
2127: him to stand up and say it out like a man.”
2128: 
2129: By this pregnant speech the farrier had re-established his
2130: self-complacency, and waited with confidence to hear himself named as
2131: one of the superlatively sensible men.
2132: 
2133: “Let us see how the night is, though,” said the landlord, who also
2134: considered himself personally concerned in this proposition. “Why, it
2135: rains heavy still,” he said, returning from the door.
2136: 
2137: “Well, I’m not the man to be afraid o’ the rain,” said the farrier.
2138: “For it’ll look bad when Justice Malam hears as respectable men like us
2139: had a information laid before ’em and took no steps.”
2140: 
2141: The landlord agreed with this view, and after taking the sense of the
2142: company, and duly rehearsing a small ceremony known in high
2143: ecclesiastical life as the _nolo episcopari_, he consented to take on
2144: himself the chill dignity of going to Kench’s. But to the farrier’s
2145: strong disgust, Mr. Macey now started an objection to his proposing
2146: himself as a deputy-constable; for that oracular old gentleman,
2147: claiming to know the law, stated, as a fact delivered to him by his
2148: father, that no doctor could be a constable.
2149: 
2150: “And you’re a doctor, I reckon, though you’re only a cow-doctor—for a
2151: fly’s a fly, though it may be a hoss-fly,” concluded Mr. Macey,
2152: wondering a little at his own “’cuteness”.
2153: 
2154: There was a hot debate upon this, the farrier being of course
2155: indisposed to renounce the quality of doctor, but contending that a
2156: doctor could be a constable if he liked—the law meant, he needn’t be
2157: one if he didn’t like. Mr. Macey thought this was nonsense, since the
2158: law was not likely to be fonder of doctors than of other folks.
2159: Moreover, if it was in the nature of doctors more than of other men not
2160: to like being constables, how came Mr. Dowlas to be so eager to act in
2161: that capacity?
2162: 
2163: “_I_ don’t want to act the constable,” said the farrier, driven into a
2164: corner by this merciless reasoning; “and there’s no man can say it of
2165: me, if he’d tell the truth. But if there’s to be any jealousy and
2166: en_vy_ing about going to Kench’s in the rain, let them go as like
2167: it—you won’t get me to go, I can tell you.”
2168: 
2169: By the landlord’s intervention, however, the dispute was accommodated.
2170: Mr. Dowlas consented to go as a second person disinclined to act
2171: officially; and so poor Silas, furnished with some old coverings,
2172: turned out with his two companions into the rain again, thinking of the
2173: long night-hours before him, not as those do who long to rest, but as
2174: those who expect to “watch for the morning”.
2175: 
2176: 
2177: 
2178: CHAPTER VIII.
2179: 
2180: When Godfrey Cass returned from Mrs. Osgood’s party at midnight, he was
2181: not much surprised to learn that Dunsey had not come home. Perhaps he
2182: had not sold Wildfire, and was waiting for another chance—perhaps, on
2183: that foggy afternoon, he had preferred housing himself at the Red Lion
2184: at Batherley for the night, if the run had kept him in that
2185: neighbourhood; for he was not likely to feel much concern about leaving
2186: his brother in suspense. Godfrey’s mind was too full of Nancy
2187: Lammeter’s looks and behaviour, too full of the exasperation against
2188: himself and his lot, which the sight of her always produced in him, for
2189: him to give much thought to Wildfire, or to the probabilities of
2190: Dunstan’s conduct.
2191: 
2192: The next morning the whole village was excited by the story of the
2193: robbery, and Godfrey, like every one else, was occupied in gathering
2194: and discussing news about it, and in visiting the Stone-pits. The rain
2195: had washed away all possibility of distinguishing foot-marks, but a
2196: close investigation of the spot had disclosed, in the direction
2197: opposite to the village, a tinder-box, with a flint and steel, half
2198: sunk in the mud. It was not Silas’s tinder-box, for the only one he had
2199: ever had was still standing on his shelf; and the inference generally
2200: accepted was, that the tinder-box in the ditch was somehow connected
2201: with the robbery. A small minority shook their heads, and intimated
2202: their opinion that it was not a robbery to have much light thrown on it
2203: by tinder-boxes, that Master Marner’s tale had a queer look with it,
2204: and that such things had been known as a man’s doing himself a
2205: mischief, and then setting the justice to look for the doer. But when
2206: questioned closely as to their grounds for this opinion, and what
2207: Master Marner had to gain by such false pretences, they only shook
2208: their heads as before, and observed that there was no knowing what some
2209: folks counted gain; moreover, that everybody had a right to their own
2210: opinions, grounds or no grounds, and that the weaver, as everybody
2211: knew, was partly crazy. Mr. Macey, though he joined in the defence of
2212: Marner against all suspicions of deceit, also pooh-poohed the
2213: tinder-box; indeed, repudiated it as a rather impious suggestion,
2214: tending to imply that everything must be done by human hands, and that
2215: there was no power which could make away with the guineas without
2216: moving the bricks. Nevertheless, he turned round rather sharply on Mr.
2217: Tookey, when the zealous deputy, feeling that this was a view of the
2218: case peculiarly suited to a parish-clerk, carried it still farther, and
2219: doubted whether it was right to inquire into a robbery at all when the
2220: circumstances were so mysterious.
2221: 
2222: “As if,” concluded Mr. Tookey—“as if there was nothing but what could
2223: be made out by justices and constables.”
2224: 
2225: “Now, don’t you be for overshooting the mark, Tookey,” said Mr. Macey,
2226: nodding his head aside admonishingly. “That’s what you’re allays at; if
2227: I throw a stone and hit, you think there’s summat better than hitting,
2228: and you try to throw a stone beyond. What I said was against the
2229: tinder-box: I said nothing against justices and constables, for they’re
2230: o’ King George’s making, and it ’ud be ill-becoming a man in a parish
2231: office to fly out again’ King George.”
2232: 
2233: While these discussions were going on amongst the group outside the
2234: Rainbow, a higher consultation was being carried on within, under the
2235: presidency of Mr. Crackenthorp, the rector, assisted by Squire Cass and
2236: other substantial parishioners. It had just occurred to Mr. Snell, the
2237: landlord—he being, as he observed, a man accustomed to put two and two
2238: together—to connect with the tinder-box, which, as deputy-constable, he
2239: himself had had the honourable distinction of finding, certain
2240: recollections of a pedlar who had called to drink at the house about a
2241: month before, and had actually stated that he carried a tinder-box
2242: about with him to light his pipe. Here, surely, was a clue to be
2243: followed out. And as memory, when duly impregnated with ascertained
2244: facts, is sometimes surprisingly fertile, Mr. Snell gradually recovered
2245: a vivid impression of the effect produced on him by the pedlar’s
2246: countenance and conversation. He had a “look with his eye” which fell
2247: unpleasantly on Mr. Snell’s sensitive organism. To be sure, he didn’t
2248: say anything particular—no, except that about the tinder-box—but it
2249: isn’t what a man says, it’s the way he says it. Moreover, he had a
2250: swarthy foreignness of complexion which boded little honesty.
2251: 
2252: “Did he wear ear-rings?” Mr. Crackenthorp wished to know, having some
2253: acquaintance with foreign customs.
2254: 
2255: “Well—stay—let me see,” said Mr. Snell, like a docile clairvoyante, who
2256: would really not make a mistake if she could help it. After stretching
2257: the corners of his mouth and contracting his eyes, as if he were trying
2258: to see the ear-rings, he appeared to give up the effort, and said,
2259: “Well, he’d got ear-rings in his box to sell, so it’s nat’ral to
2260: suppose he might wear ’em. But he called at every house, a’most, in the
2261: village; there’s somebody else, mayhap, saw ’em in his ears, though I
2262: can’t take upon me rightly to say.”
2263: 
2264: Mr. Snell was correct in his surmise, that somebody else would remember
2265: the pedlar’s ear-rings. For on the spread of inquiry among the
2266: villagers it was stated with gathering emphasis, that the parson had
2267: wanted to know whether the pedlar wore ear-rings in his ears, and an
2268: impression was created that a great deal depended on the eliciting of
2269: this fact. Of course, every one who heard the question, not having any
2270: distinct image of the pedlar as _without_ ear-rings, immediately had an
2271: image of him _with_ ear-rings, larger or smaller, as the case might be;
2272: and the image was presently taken for a vivid recollection, so that the
2273: glazier’s wife, a well-intentioned woman, not given to lying, and whose
2274: house was among the cleanest in the village, was ready to declare, as
2275: sure as ever she meant to take the sacrament the very next Christmas
2276: that was ever coming, that she had seen big ear-rings, in the shape of
2277: the young moon, in the pedlar’s two ears; while Jinny Oates, the
2278: cobbler’s daughter, being a more imaginative person, stated not only
2279: that she had seen them too, but that they had made her blood creep, as
2280: it did at that very moment while there she stood.
2281: 
2282: Also, by way of throwing further light on this clue of the tinder-box,
2283: a collection was made of all the articles purchased from the pedlar at
2284: various houses, and carried to the Rainbow to be exhibited there. In
2285: fact, there was a general feeling in the village, that for the
2286: clearing-up of this robbery there must be a great deal done at the
2287: Rainbow, and that no man need offer his wife an excuse for going there
2288: while it was the scene of severe public duties.
2289: 
2290: Some disappointment was felt, and perhaps a little indignation also,
2291: when it became known that Silas Marner, on being questioned by the
2292: Squire and the parson, had retained no other recollection of the pedlar
2293: than that he had called at his door, but had not entered his house,
2294: having turned away at once when Silas, holding the door ajar, had said
2295: that he wanted nothing. This had been Silas’s testimony, though he
2296: clutched strongly at the idea of the pedlar’s being the culprit, if
2297: only because it gave him a definite image of a whereabout for his gold
2298: after it had been taken away from its hiding-place: he could see it now
2299: in the pedlar’s box. But it was observed with some irritation in the
2300: village, that anybody but a “blind creatur” like Marner would have seen
2301: the man prowling about, for how came he to leave his tinder-box in the
2302: ditch close by, if he hadn’t been lingering there? Doubtless, he had
2303: made his observations when he saw Marner at the door. Anybody might
2304: know—and only look at him—that the weaver was a half-crazy miser. It
2305: was a wonder the pedlar hadn’t murdered him; men of that sort, with
2306: rings in their ears, had been known for murderers often and often;
2307: there had been one tried at the ’sizes, not so long ago but what there
2308: were people living who remembered it.
2309: 
2310: Godfrey Cass, indeed, entering the Rainbow during one of Mr. Snell’s
2311: frequently repeated recitals of his testimony, had treated it lightly,
2312: stating that he himself had bought a pen-knife of the pedlar, and
2313: thought him a merry grinning fellow enough; it was all nonsense, he
2314: said, about the man’s evil looks. But this was spoken of in the village
2315: as the random talk of youth, “as if it was only Mr. Snell who had seen
2316: something odd about the pedlar!” On the contrary, there were at least
2317: half-a-dozen who were ready to go before Justice Malam, and give in
2318: much more striking testimony than any the landlord could furnish. It
2319: was to be hoped Mr. Godfrey would not go to Tarley and throw cold water
2320: on what Mr. Snell said there, and so prevent the justice from drawing
2321: up a warrant. He was suspected of intending this, when, after mid-day,
2322: he was seen setting off on horseback in the direction of Tarley.
2323: 
2324: But by this time Godfrey’s interest in the robbery had faded before his
2325: growing anxiety about Dunstan and Wildfire, and he was going, not to
2326: Tarley, but to Batherley, unable to rest in uncertainty about them any
2327: longer. The possibility that Dunstan had played him the ugly trick of
2328: riding away with Wildfire, to return at the end of a month, when he had
2329: gambled away or otherwise squandered the price of the horse, was a fear
2330: that urged itself upon him more, even, than the thought of an
2331: accidental injury; and now that the dance at Mrs. Osgood’s was past, he
2332: was irritated with himself that he had trusted his horse to Dunstan.
2333: Instead of trying to still his fears, he encouraged them, with that
2334: superstitious impression which clings to us all, that if we expect evil
2335: very strongly it is the less likely to come; and when he heard a horse
2336: approaching at a trot, and saw a hat rising above a hedge beyond an
2337: angle of the lane, he felt as if his conjuration had succeeded. But no
2338: sooner did the horse come within sight, than his heart sank again. It
2339: was not Wildfire; and in a few moments more he discerned that the rider
2340: was not Dunstan, but Bryce, who pulled up to speak, with a face that
2341: implied something disagreeable.
2342: 
2343: “Well, Mr. Godfrey, that’s a lucky brother of yours, that Master
2344: Dunsey, isn’t he?”
2345: 
2346: “What do you mean?” said Godfrey, hastily.
2347: 
2348: “Why, hasn’t he been home yet?” said Bryce.
2349: 
2350: “Home? no. What has happened? Be quick. What has he done with my
2351: horse?”
2352: 
2353: “Ah, I thought it was yours, though he pretended you had parted with it
2354: to him.”
2355: 
2356: “Has he thrown him down and broken his knees?” said Godfrey, flushed
2357: with exasperation.
2358: 
2359: “Worse than that,” said Bryce. “You see, I’d made a bargain with him to
2360: buy the horse for a hundred and twenty—a swinging price, but I always
2361: liked the horse. And what does he do but go and stake him—fly at a
2362: hedge with stakes in it, atop of a bank with a ditch before it. The
2363: horse had been dead a pretty good while when he was found. So he hasn’t
2364: been home since, has he?”
2365: 
2366: “Home? no,” said Godfrey, “and he’d better keep away. Confound me for a
2367: fool! I might have known this would be the end of it.”
2368: 
2369: “Well, to tell you the truth,” said Bryce, “after I’d bargained for the
2370: horse, it did come into my head that he might be riding and selling the
2371: horse without your knowledge, for I didn’t believe it was his own. I
2372: knew Master Dunsey was up to his tricks sometimes. But where can he be
2373: gone? He’s never been seen at Batherley. He couldn’t have been hurt,
2374: for he must have walked off.”
2375: 
2376: “Hurt?” said Godfrey, bitterly. “He’ll never be hurt—he’s made to hurt
2377: other people.”
2378: 
2379: “And so you _did_ give him leave to sell the horse, eh?” said Bryce.
2380: 
2381: “Yes; I wanted to part with the horse—he was always a little too hard
2382: in the mouth for me,” said Godfrey; his pride making him wince under
2383: the idea that Bryce guessed the sale to be a matter of necessity. “I
2384: was going to see after him—I thought some mischief had happened. I’ll
2385: go back now,” he added, turning the horse’s head, and wishing he could
2386: get rid of Bryce; for he felt that the long-dreaded crisis in his life
2387: was close upon him. “You’re coming on to Raveloe, aren’t you?”
2388: 
2389: “Well, no, not now,” said Bryce. “I _was_ coming round there, for I had
2390: to go to Flitton, and I thought I might as well take you in my way, and
2391: just let you know all I knew myself about the horse. I suppose Master
2392: Dunsey didn’t like to show himself till the ill news had blown over a
2393: bit. He’s perhaps gone to pay a visit at the Three Crowns, by
2394: Whitbridge—I know he’s fond of the house.”
2395: 
2396: “Perhaps he is,” said Godfrey, rather absently. Then rousing himself,
2397: he said, with an effort at carelessness, “We shall hear of him soon
2398: enough, I’ll be bound.”
2399: 
2400: “Well, here’s my turning,” said Bryce, not surprised to perceive that
2401: Godfrey was rather “down”; “so I’ll bid you good-day, and wish I may
2402: bring you better news another time.”
2403: 
2404: Godfrey rode along slowly, representing to himself the scene of
2405: confession to his father from which he felt that there was now no
2406: longer any escape. The revelation about the money must be made the very
2407: next morning; and if he withheld the rest, Dunstan would be sure to
2408: come back shortly, and, finding that he must bear the brunt of his
2409: father’s anger, would tell the whole story out of spite, even though he
2410: had nothing to gain by it. There was one step, perhaps, by which he
2411: might still win Dunstan’s silence and put off the evil day: he might
2412: tell his father that he had himself spent the money paid to him by
2413: Fowler; and as he had never been guilty of such an offence before, the
2414: affair would blow over after a little storming. But Godfrey could not
2415: bend himself to this. He felt that in letting Dunstan have the money,
2416: he had already been guilty of a breach of trust hardly less culpable
2417: than that of spending the money directly for his own behoof; and yet
2418: there was a distinction between the two acts which made him feel that
2419: the one was so much more blackening than the other as to be intolerable
2420: to him.
2421: 
2422: “I don’t pretend to be a good fellow,” he said to himself; “but I’m not
2423: a scoundrel—at least, I’ll stop short somewhere. I’ll bear the
2424: consequences of what I _have_ done sooner than make believe I’ve done
2425: what I never would have done. I’d never have spent the money for my own
2426: pleasure—I was tortured into it.”
2427: 
2428: Through the remainder of this day Godfrey, with only occasional
2429: fluctuations, kept his will bent in the direction of a complete avowal
2430: to his father, and he withheld the story of Wildfire’s loss till the
2431: next morning, that it might serve him as an introduction to heavier
2432: matter. The old Squire was accustomed to his son’s frequent absence
2433: from home, and thought neither Dunstan’s nor Wildfire’s non-appearance
2434: a matter calling for remark. Godfrey said to himself again and again,
2435: that if he let slip this one opportunity of confession, he might never
2436: have another; the revelation might be made even in a more odious way
2437: than by Dunstan’s malignity: _she_ might come as she had threatened to
2438: do. And then he tried to make the scene easier to himself by rehearsal:
2439: he made up his mind how he would pass from the admission of his
2440: weakness in letting Dunstan have the money to the fact that Dunstan had
2441: a hold on him which he had been unable to shake off, and how he would
2442: work up his father to expect something very bad before he told him the
2443: fact. The old Squire was an implacable man: he made resolutions in
2444: violent anger, and he was not to be moved from them after his anger had
2445: subsided—as fiery volcanic matters cool and harden into rock. Like many
2446: violent and implacable men, he allowed evils to grow under favour of
2447: his own heedlessness, till they pressed upon him with exasperating
2448: force, and then he turned round with fierce severity and became
2449: unrelentingly hard. This was his system with his tenants: he allowed
2450: them to get into arrears, neglect their fences, reduce their stock,
2451: sell their straw, and otherwise go the wrong way,—and then, when he
2452: became short of money in consequence of this indulgence, he took the
2453: hardest measures and would listen to no appeal. Godfrey knew all this,
2454: and felt it with the greater force because he had constantly suffered
2455: annoyance from witnessing his father’s sudden fits of unrelentingness,
2456: for which his own habitual irresolution deprived him of all sympathy.
2457: (He was not critical on the faulty indulgence which preceded these
2458: fits; _that_ seemed to him natural enough.) Still there was just the
2459: chance, Godfrey thought, that his father’s pride might see this
2460: marriage in a light that would induce him to hush it up, rather than
2461: turn his son out and make the family the talk of the country for ten
2462: miles round.
2463: 
2464: This was the view of the case that Godfrey managed to keep before him
2465: pretty closely till midnight, and he went to sleep thinking that he had
2466: done with inward debating. But when he awoke in the still morning
2467: darkness he found it impossible to reawaken his evening thoughts; it
2468: was as if they had been tired out and were not to be roused to further
2469: work. Instead of arguments for confession, he could now feel the
2470: presence of nothing but its evil consequences: the old dread of
2471: disgrace came back—the old shrinking from the thought of raising a
2472: hopeless barrier between himself and Nancy—the old disposition to rely
2473: on chances which might be favourable to him, and save him from
2474: betrayal. Why, after all, should he cut off the hope of them by his own
2475: act? He had seen the matter in a wrong light yesterday. He had been in
2476: a rage with Dunstan, and had thought of nothing but a thorough break-up
2477: of their mutual understanding; but what it would be really wisest for
2478: him to do, was to try and soften his father’s anger against Dunsey, and
2479: keep things as nearly as possible in their old condition. If Dunsey did
2480: not come back for a few days (and Godfrey did not know but that the
2481: rascal had enough money in his pocket to enable him to keep away still
2482: longer), everything might blow over.
2483: 
2484: 
2485: 
2486: CHAPTER IX.
2487: 
2488: Godfrey rose and took his own breakfast earlier than usual, but
2489: lingered in the wainscoted parlour till his younger brothers had
2490: finished their meal and gone out; awaiting his father, who always took
2491: a walk with his managing-man before breakfast. Every one breakfasted at
2492: a different hour in the Red House, and the Squire was always the
2493: latest, giving a long chance to a rather feeble morning appetite before
2494: he tried it. The table had been spread with substantial eatables nearly
2495: two hours before he presented himself—a tall, stout man of sixty, with
2496: a face in which the knit brow and rather hard glance seemed
2497: contradicted by the slack and feeble mouth. His person showed marks of
2498: habitual neglect, his dress was slovenly; and yet there was something
2499: in the presence of the old Squire distinguishable from that of the
2500: ordinary farmers in the parish, who were perhaps every whit as refined
2501: as he, but, having slouched their way through life with a consciousness
2502: of being in the vicinity of their “betters”, wanted that
2503: self-possession and authoritativeness of voice and carriage which
2504: belonged to a man who thought of superiors as remote existences with
2505: whom he had personally little more to do than with America or the
2506: stars. The Squire had been used to parish homage all his life, used to
2507: the presupposition that his family, his tankards, and everything that
2508: was his, were the oldest and best; and as he never associated with any
2509: gentry higher than himself, his opinion was not disturbed by
2510: comparison.
2511: 
2512: He glanced at his son as he entered the room, and said, “What, sir!
2513: haven’t _you_ had your breakfast yet?” but there was no pleasant
