2514: morning greeting between them; not because of any unfriendliness, but
2515: because the sweet flower of courtesy is not a growth of such homes as
2516: the Red House.
2517: 
2518: “Yes, sir,” said Godfrey, “I’ve had my breakfast, but I was waiting to
2519: speak to you.”
2520: 
2521: “Ah! well,” said the Squire, throwing himself indifferently into his
2522: chair, and speaking in a ponderous coughing fashion, which was felt in
2523: Raveloe to be a sort of privilege of his rank, while he cut a piece of
2524: beef, and held it up before the deer-hound that had come in with him.
2525: “Ring the bell for my ale, will you? You youngsters’ business is your
2526: own pleasure, mostly. There’s no hurry about it for anybody but
2527: yourselves.”
2528: 
2529: The Squire’s life was quite as idle as his sons’, but it was a fiction
2530: kept up by himself and his contemporaries in Raveloe that youth was
2531: exclusively the period of folly, and that their aged wisdom was
2532: constantly in a state of endurance mitigated by sarcasm. Godfrey
2533: waited, before he spoke again, until the ale had been brought and the
2534: door closed—an interval during which Fleet, the deer-hound, had
2535: consumed enough bits of beef to make a poor man’s holiday dinner.
2536: 
2537: “There’s been a cursed piece of ill-luck with Wildfire,” he began;
2538: “happened the day before yesterday.”
2539: 
2540: “What! broke his knees?” said the Squire, after taking a draught of
2541: ale. “I thought you knew how to ride better than that, sir. I never
2542: threw a horse down in my life. If I had, I might ha’ whistled for
2543: another, for _my_ father wasn’t quite so ready to unstring as some
2544: other fathers I know of. But they must turn over a new leaf—_they_
2545: must. What with mortgages and arrears, I’m as short o’ cash as a
2546: roadside pauper. And that fool Kimble says the newspaper’s talking
2547: about peace. Why, the country wouldn’t have a leg to stand on. Prices
2548: ’ud run down like a jack, and I should never get my arrears, not if I
2549: sold all the fellows up. And there’s that damned Fowler, I won’t put up
2550: with him any longer; I’ve told Winthrop to go to Cox this very day. The
2551: lying scoundrel told me he’d be sure to pay me a hundred last month. He
2552: takes advantage because he’s on that outlying farm, and thinks I shall
2553: forget him.”
2554: 
2555: The Squire had delivered this speech in a coughing and interrupted
2556: manner, but with no pause long enough for Godfrey to make it a pretext
2557: for taking up the word again. He felt that his father meant to ward off
2558: any request for money on the ground of the misfortune with Wildfire,
2559: and that the emphasis he had thus been led to lay on his shortness of
2560: cash and his arrears was likely to produce an attitude of mind the
2561: utmost unfavourable for his own disclosure. But he must go on, now he
2562: had begun.
2563: 
2564: “It’s worse than breaking the horse’s knees—he’s been staked and
2565: killed,” he said, as soon as his father was silent, and had begun to
2566: cut his meat. “But I wasn’t thinking of asking you to buy me another
2567: horse; I was only thinking I’d lost the means of paying you with the
2568: price of Wildfire, as I’d meant to do. Dunsey took him to the hunt to
2569: sell him for me the other day, and after he’d made a bargain for a
2570: hundred and twenty with Bryce, he went after the hounds, and took some
2571: fool’s leap or other that did for the horse at once. If it hadn’t been
2572: for that, I should have paid you a hundred pounds this morning.”
2573: 
2574: The Squire had laid down his knife and fork, and was staring at his son
2575: in amazement, not being sufficiently quick of brain to form a probable
2576: guess as to what could have caused so strange an inversion of the
2577: paternal and filial relations as this proposition of his son to pay him
2578: a hundred pounds.
2579: 
2580: “The truth is, sir—I’m very sorry—I was quite to blame,” said Godfrey.
2581: “Fowler did pay that hundred pounds. He paid it to me, when I was over
2582: there one day last month. And Dunsey bothered me for the money, and I
2583: let him have it, because I hoped I should be able to pay it you before
2584: this.”
2585: 
2586: The Squire was purple with anger before his son had done speaking, and
2587: found utterance difficult. “You let Dunsey have it, sir? And how long
2588: have you been so thick with Dunsey that you must _collogue_ with him to
2589: embezzle my money? Are you turning out a scamp? I tell you I won’t have
2590: it. I’ll turn the whole pack of you out of the house together, and
2591: marry again. I’d have you to remember, sir, my property’s got no entail
2592: on it;—since my grandfather’s time the Casses can do as they like with
2593: their land. Remember that, sir. Let Dunsey have the money! Why should
2594: you let Dunsey have the money? There’s some lie at the bottom of it.”
2595: 
2596: “There’s no lie, sir,” said Godfrey. “I wouldn’t have spent the money
2597: myself, but Dunsey bothered me, and I was a fool, and let him have it.
2598: But I meant to pay it, whether he did or not. That’s the whole story. I
2599: never meant to embezzle money, and I’m not the man to do it. You never
2600: knew me do a dishonest trick, sir.”
2601: 
2602: “Where’s Dunsey, then? What do you stand talking there for? Go and
2603: fetch Dunsey, as I tell you, and let him give account of what he wanted
2604: the money for, and what he’s done with it. He shall repent it. I’ll
2605: turn him out. I said I would, and I’ll do it. He shan’t brave me. Go
2606: and fetch him.”
2607: 
2608: “Dunsey isn’t come back, sir.”
2609: 
2610: “What! did he break his own neck, then?” said the Squire, with some
2611: disgust at the idea that, in that case, he could not fulfil his threat.
2612: 
2613: “No, he wasn’t hurt, I believe, for the horse was found dead, and
2614: Dunsey must have walked off. I daresay we shall see him again
2615: by-and-by. I don’t know where he is.”
2616: 
2617: “And what must you be letting him have my money for? Answer me that,”
2618: said the Squire, attacking Godfrey again, since Dunsey was not within
2619: reach.
2620: 
2621: “Well, sir, I don’t know,” said Godfrey, hesitatingly. That was a
2622: feeble evasion, but Godfrey was not fond of lying, and, not being
2623: sufficiently aware that no sort of duplicity can long flourish without
2624: the help of vocal falsehoods, he was quite unprepared with invented
2625: motives.
2626: 
2627: “You don’t know? I tell you what it is, sir. You’ve been up to some
2628: trick, and you’ve been bribing him not to tell,” said the Squire, with
2629: a sudden acuteness which startled Godfrey, who felt his heart beat
2630: violently at the nearness of his father’s guess. The sudden alarm
2631: pushed him on to take the next step—a very slight impulse suffices for
2632: that on a downward road.
2633: 
2634: “Why, sir,” he said, trying to speak with careless ease, “it was a
2635: little affair between me and Dunsey; it’s no matter to anybody else.
2636: It’s hardly worth while to pry into young men’s fooleries: it wouldn’t
2637: have made any difference to you, sir, if I’d not had the bad luck to
2638: lose Wildfire. I should have paid you the money.”
2639: 
2640: “Fooleries! Pshaw! it’s time you’d done with fooleries. And I’d have
2641: you know, sir, you _must_ ha’ done with ’em,” said the Squire, frowning
2642: and casting an angry glance at his son. “Your goings-on are not what I
2643: shall find money for any longer. There’s my grandfather had his stables
2644: full o’ horses, and kept a good house, too, and in worse times, by what
2645: I can make out; and so might I, if I hadn’t four good-for-nothing
2646: fellows to hang on me like horse-leeches. I’ve been too good a father
2647: to you all—that’s what it is. But I shall pull up, sir.”
2648: 
2649: Godfrey was silent. He was not likely to be very penetrating in his
2650: judgments, but he had always had a sense that his father’s indulgence
2651: had not been kindness, and had had a vague longing for some discipline
2652: that would have checked his own errant weakness and helped his better
2653: will. The Squire ate his bread and meat hastily, took a deep draught of
2654: ale, then turned his chair from the table, and began to speak again.
2655: 
2656: “It’ll be all the worse for you, you know—you’d need try and help me
2657: keep things together.”
2658: 
2659: “Well, sir, I’ve often offered to take the management of things, but
2660: you know you’ve taken it ill always, and seemed to think I wanted to
2661: push you out of your place.”
2662: 
2663: “I know nothing o’ your offering or o’ my taking it ill,” said the
2664: Squire, whose memory consisted in certain strong impressions unmodified
2665: by detail; “but I know, one while you seemed to be thinking o’
2666: marrying, and I didn’t offer to put any obstacles in your way, as some
2667: fathers would. I’d as lieve you married Lammeter’s daughter as anybody.
2668: I suppose, if I’d said you nay, you’d ha’ kept on with it; but, for
2669: want o’ contradiction, you’ve changed your mind. You’re a shilly-shally
2670: fellow: you take after your poor mother. She never had a will of her
2671: own; a woman has no call for one, if she’s got a proper man for her
2672: husband. But _your_ wife had need have one, for you hardly know your
2673: own mind enough to make both your legs walk one way. The lass hasn’t
2674: said downright she won’t have you, has she?”
2675: 
2676: “No,” said Godfrey, feeling very hot and uncomfortable; “but I don’t
2677: think she will.”
2678: 
2679: “Think! why haven’t you the courage to ask her? Do you stick to it, you
2680: want to have _her_—that’s the thing?”
2681: 
2682: “There’s no other woman I want to marry,” said Godfrey, evasively.
2683: 
2684: “Well, then, let me make the offer for you, that’s all, if you haven’t
2685: the pluck to do it yourself. Lammeter isn’t likely to be loath for his
2686: daughter to marry into _my_ family, I should think. And as for the
2687: pretty lass, she wouldn’t have her cousin—and there’s nobody else, as I
2688: see, could ha’ stood in your way.”
2689: 
2690: “I’d rather let it be, please sir, at present,” said Godfrey, in alarm.
2691: “I think she’s a little offended with me just now, and I should like to
2692: speak for myself. A man must manage these things for himself.”
2693: 
2694: “Well, speak, then, and manage it, and see if you can’t turn over a new
2695: leaf. That’s what a man must do when he thinks o’ marrying.”
2696: 
2697: “I don’t see how I can think of it at present, sir. You wouldn’t like
2698: to settle me on one of the farms, I suppose, and I don’t think she’d
2699: come to live in this house with all my brothers. It’s a different sort
2700: of life to what she’s been used to.”
2701: 
2702: “Not come to live in this house? Don’t tell me. You ask her, that’s
2703: all,” said the Squire, with a short, scornful laugh.
2704: 
2705: “I’d rather let the thing be, at present, sir,” said Godfrey. “I hope
2706: you won’t try to hurry it on by saying anything.”
2707: 
2708: “I shall do what I choose,” said the Squire, “and I shall let you know
2709: I’m master; else you may turn out and find an estate to drop into
2710: somewhere else. Go out and tell Winthrop not to go to Cox’s, but wait
2711: for me. And tell ’em to get my horse saddled. And stop: look out and
2712: get that hack o’ Dunsey’s sold, and hand me the money, will you? He’ll
2713: keep no more hacks at my expense. And if you know where he’s sneaking—I
2714: daresay you do—you may tell him to spare himself the journey o’ coming
2715: back home. Let him turn ostler, and keep himself. He shan’t hang on me
2716: any more.”
2717: 
2718: “I don’t know where he is, sir; and if I did, it isn’t my place to tell
2719: him to keep away,” said Godfrey, moving towards the door.
2720: 
2721: “Confound it, sir, don’t stay arguing, but go and order my horse,” said
2722: the Squire, taking up a pipe.
2723: 
2724: Godfrey left the room, hardly knowing whether he were more relieved by
2725: the sense that the interview was ended without having made any change
2726: in his position, or more uneasy that he had entangled himself still
2727: further in prevarication and deceit. What had passed about his
2728: proposing to Nancy had raised a new alarm, lest by some after-dinner
2729: words of his father’s to Mr. Lammeter he should be thrown into the
2730: embarrassment of being obliged absolutely to decline her when she
2731: seemed to be within his reach. He fled to his usual refuge, that of
2732: hoping for some unforeseen turn of fortune, some favourable chance
2733: which would save him from unpleasant consequences—perhaps even justify
2734: his insincerity by manifesting its prudence. And in this point of
2735: trusting to some throw of fortune’s dice, Godfrey can hardly be called
2736: specially old-fashioned. Favourable Chance, I fancy, is the god of all
2737: men who follow their own devices instead of obeying a law they believe
2738: in. Let even a polished man of these days get into a position he is
2739: ashamed to avow, and his mind will be bent on all the possible issues
2740: that may deliver him from the calculable results of that position. Let
2741: him live outside his income, or shirk the resolute honest work that
2742: brings wages, and he will presently find himself dreaming of a possible
2743: benefactor, a possible simpleton who may be cajoled into using his
2744: interest, a possible state of mind in some possible person not yet
2745: forthcoming. Let him neglect the responsibilities of his office, and he
2746: will inevitably anchor himself on the chance that the thing left undone
2747: may turn out not to be of the supposed importance. Let him betray his
2748: friend’s confidence, and he will adore that same cunning complexity
2749: called Chance, which gives him the hope that his friend will never
2750: know. Let him forsake a decent craft that he may pursue the gentilities
2751: of a profession to which nature never called him, and his religion will
2752: infallibly be the worship of blessed Chance, which he will believe in
2753: as the mighty creator of success. The evil principle deprecated in that
2754: religion is the orderly sequence by which the seed brings forth a crop
2755: after its kind.
2756: 
2757: 
2758: 
2759: CHAPTER X.
2760: 
2761: Justice Malam was naturally regarded in Tarley and Raveloe as a man of
2762: capacious mind, seeing that he could draw much wider conclusions
2763: without evidence than could be expected of his neighbours who were not
2764: on the Commission of the Peace. Such a man was not likely to neglect
2765: the clue of the tinder-box, and an inquiry was set on foot concerning a
2766: pedlar, name unknown, with curly black hair and a foreign complexion,
2767: carrying a box of cutlery and jewellery, and wearing large rings in his
2768: ears. But either because inquiry was too slow-footed to overtake him,
2769: or because the description applied to so many pedlars that inquiry did
2770: not know how to choose among them, weeks passed away, and there was no
2771: other result concerning the robbery than a gradual cessation of the
2772: excitement it had caused in Raveloe. Dunstan Cass’s absence was hardly
2773: a subject of remark: he had once before had a quarrel with his father,
2774: and had gone off, nobody knew whither, to return at the end of six
2775: weeks, take up his old quarters unforbidden, and swagger as usual. His
2776: own family, who equally expected this issue, with the sole difference
2777: that the Squire was determined this time to forbid him the old
2778: quarters, never mentioned his absence; and when his uncle Kimble or Mr.
2779: Osgood noticed it, the story of his having killed Wildfire, and
2780: committed some offence against his father, was enough to prevent
2781: surprise. To connect the fact of Dunsey’s disappearance with that of
2782: the robbery occurring on the same day, lay quite away from the track of
2783: every one’s thought—even Godfrey’s, who had better reason than any one
2784: else to know what his brother was capable of. He remembered no mention
2785: of the weaver between them since the time, twelve years ago, when it
2786: was their boyish sport to deride him; and, besides, his imagination
2787: constantly created an _alibi_ for Dunstan: he saw him continually in
2788: some congenial haunt, to which he had walked off on leaving
2789: Wildfire—saw him sponging on chance acquaintances, and meditating a
2790: return home to the old amusement of tormenting his elder brother. Even
2791: if any brain in Raveloe had put the said two facts together, I doubt
2792: whether a combination so injurious to the prescriptive respectability
2793: of a family with a mural monument and venerable tankards, would not
2794: have been suppressed as of unsound tendency. But Christmas puddings,
2795: brawn, and abundance of spirituous liquors, throwing the mental
2796: originality into the channel of nightmare, are great preservatives
2797: against a dangerous spontaneity of waking thought.
2798: 
2799: When the robbery was talked of at the Rainbow and elsewhere, in good
2800: company, the balance continued to waver between the rational
2801: explanation founded on the tinder-box, and the theory of an
2802: impenetrable mystery that mocked investigation. The advocates of the
2803: tinder-box-and-pedlar view considered the other side a muddle-headed
2804: and credulous set, who, because they themselves were wall-eyed,
2805: supposed everybody else to have the same blank outlook; and the
2806: adherents of the inexplicable more than hinted that their antagonists
2807: were animals inclined to crow before they had found any corn—mere
2808: skimming-dishes in point of depth—whose clear-sightedness consisted in
2809: supposing there was nothing behind a barn-door because they couldn’t
2810: see through it; so that, though their controversy did not serve to
2811: elicit the fact concerning the robbery, it elicited some true opinions
2812: of collateral importance.
2813: 
2814: But while poor Silas’s loss served thus to brush the slow current of
2815: Raveloe conversation, Silas himself was feeling the withering
2816: desolation of that bereavement about which his neighbours were arguing
2817: at their ease. To any one who had observed him before he lost his gold,
2818: it might have seemed that so withered and shrunken a life as his could
2819: hardly be susceptible of a bruise, could hardly endure any subtraction
2820: but such as would put an end to it altogether. But in reality it had
2821: been an eager life, filled with immediate purpose which fenced him in
2822: from the wide, cheerless unknown. It had been a clinging life; and
2823: though the object round which its fibres had clung was a dead disrupted
2824: thing, it satisfied the need for clinging. But now the fence was broken
2825: down—the support was snatched away. Marner’s thoughts could no longer
2826: move in their old round, and were baffled by a blank like that which
2827: meets a plodding ant when the earth has broken away on its homeward
2828: path. The loom was there, and the weaving, and the growing pattern in
2829: the cloth; but the bright treasure in the hole under his feet was gone;
2830: the prospect of handling and counting it was gone: the evening had no
2831: phantasm of delight to still the poor soul’s craving. The thought of
2832: the money he would get by his actual work could bring no joy, for its
2833: meagre image was only a fresh reminder of his loss; and hope was too
2834: heavily crushed by the sudden blow for his imagination to dwell on the
2835: growth of a new hoard from that small beginning.
2836: 
2837: He filled up the blank with grief. As he sat weaving, he every now and
2838: then moaned low, like one in pain: it was the sign that his thoughts
2839: had come round again to the sudden chasm—to the empty evening-time. And
2840: all the evening, as he sat in his loneliness by his dull fire, he
2841: leaned his elbows on his knees, and clasped his head with his hands,
2842: and moaned very low—not as one who seeks to be heard.
2843: 
2844: And yet he was not utterly forsaken in his trouble. The repulsion
2845: Marner had always created in his neighbours was partly dissipated by
2846: the new light in which this misfortune had shown him. Instead of a man
2847: who had more cunning than honest folks could come by, and, what was
2848: worse, had not the inclination to use that cunning in a neighbourly
2849: way, it was now apparent that Silas had not cunning enough to keep his
2850: own. He was generally spoken of as a “poor mushed creatur”; and that
2851: avoidance of his neighbours, which had before been referred to his
2852: ill-will and to a probable addiction to worse company, was now
2853: considered mere craziness.
2854: 
2855: This change to a kindlier feeling was shown in various ways. The odour
2856: of Christmas cooking being on the wind, it was the season when
2857: superfluous pork and black puddings are suggestive of charity in
2858: well-to-do families; and Silas’s misfortune had brought him uppermost
2859: in the memory of housekeepers like Mrs. Osgood. Mr. Crackenthorp, too,
2860: while he admonished Silas that his money had probably been taken from
2861: him because he thought too much of it and never came to church,
2862: enforced the doctrine by a present of pigs’ pettitoes, well calculated
2863: to dissipate unfounded prejudices against the clerical character.
2864: Neighbours who had nothing but verbal consolation to give showed a
2865: disposition not only to greet Silas and discuss his misfortune at some
2866: length when they encountered him in the village, but also to take the
2867: trouble of calling at his cottage and getting him to repeat all the
2868: details on the very spot; and then they would try to cheer him by
2869: saying, “Well, Master Marner, you’re no worse off nor other poor folks,
2870: after all; and if you was to be crippled, the parish ’ud give you a
2871: ’lowance.”
2872: 
2873: I suppose one reason why we are seldom able to comfort our neighbours
2874: with our words is that our goodwill gets adulterated, in spite of
2875: ourselves, before it can pass our lips. We can send black puddings and
2876: pettitoes without giving them a flavour of our own egoism; but language
2877: is a stream that is almost sure to smack of a mingled soil. There was a
2878: fair proportion of kindness in Raveloe; but it was often of a beery and
2879: bungling sort, and took the shape least allied to the complimentary and
2880: hypocritical.
2881: 
2882: Mr. Macey, for example, coming one evening expressly to let Silas know
2883: that recent events had given him the advantage of standing more
2884: favourably in the opinion of a man whose judgment was not formed
2885: lightly, opened the conversation by saying, as soon as he had seated
2886: himself and adjusted his thumbs—
2887: 
2888: “Come, Master Marner, why, you’ve no call to sit a-moaning. You’re a
2889: deal better off to ha’ lost your money, nor to ha’ kep it by foul
2890: means. I used to think, when you first come into these parts, as you
2891: were no better nor you should be; you were younger a deal than what you
2892: are now; but you were allays a staring, white-faced creatur, partly
2893: like a bald-faced calf, as I may say. But there’s no knowing: it isn’t
2894: every queer-looksed thing as Old Harry’s had the making of—I mean,
2895: speaking o’ toads and such; for they’re often harmless, like, and
2896: useful against varmin. And it’s pretty much the same wi’ you, as fur as
2897: I can see. Though as to the yarbs and stuff to cure the breathing, if
2898: you brought that sort o’ knowledge from distant parts, you might ha’
2899: been a bit freer of it. And if the knowledge wasn’t well come by, why,
2900: you might ha’ made up for it by coming to church reg’lar; for, as for
2901: the children as the Wise Woman charmed, I’ve been at the christening of
2902: ’em again and again, and they took the water just as well. And that’s
2903: reasonable; for if Old Harry’s a mind to do a bit o’ kindness for a
2904: holiday, like, who’s got anything against it? That’s my thinking; and
2905: I’ve been clerk o’ this parish forty year, and I know, when the parson
2906: and me does the cussing of a Ash Wednesday, there’s no cussing o’ folks
2907: as have a mind to be cured without a doctor, let Kimble say what he
2908: will. And so, Master Marner, as I was saying—for there’s windings i’
2909: things as they may carry you to the fur end o’ the prayer-book afore
2910: you get back to ’em—my advice is, as you keep up your sperrits; for as
2911: for thinking you’re a deep un, and ha’ got more inside you nor ’ull
2912: bear daylight, I’m not o’ that opinion at all, and so I tell the
2913: neighbours. For, says I, you talk o’ Master Marner making out a
2914: tale—why, it’s nonsense, that is: it ’ud take a ’cute man to make a
2915: tale like that; and, says I, he looked as scared as a rabbit.”
2916: 
2917: During this discursive address Silas had continued motionless in his
2918: previous attitude, leaning his elbows on his knees, and pressing his
2919: hands against his head. Mr. Macey, not doubting that he had been
2920: listened to, paused, in the expectation of some appreciatory reply, but
2921: Marner remained silent. He had a sense that the old man meant to be
2922: good-natured and neighbourly; but the kindness fell on him as sunshine
2923: falls on the wretched—he had no heart to taste it, and felt that it was
2924: very far off him.
2925: 
2926: “Come, Master Marner, have you got nothing to say to that?” said Mr.
2927: Macey at last, with a slight accent of impatience.
2928: 
2929: “Oh,” said Marner, slowly, shaking his head between his hands, “I thank
2930: you—thank you—kindly.”
2931: 
2932: “Aye, aye, to be sure: I thought you would,” said Mr. Macey; “and my
2933: advice is—have you got a Sunday suit?”
2934: 
2935: “No,” said Marner.
2936: 
2937: “I doubted it was so,” said Mr. Macey. “Now, let me advise you to get a
2938: Sunday suit: there’s Tookey, he’s a poor creatur, but he’s got my
2939: tailoring business, and some o’ my money in it, and he shall make a
2940: suit at a low price, and give you trust, and then you can come to
2941: church, and be a bit neighbourly. Why, you’ve never heared me say
2942: “Amen” since you come into these parts, and I recommend you to lose no
2943: time, for it’ll be poor work when Tookey has it all to himself, for I
2944: mayn’t be equil to stand i’ the desk at all, come another winter.” Here
2945: Mr. Macey paused, perhaps expecting some sign of emotion in his hearer;
2946: but not observing any, he went on. “And as for the money for the suit
2947: o’ clothes, why, you get a matter of a pound a-week at your weaving,
2948: Master Marner, and you’re a young man, eh, for all you look so mushed.
2949: Why, you couldn’t ha’ been five-and-twenty when you come into these
2950: parts, eh?”
2951: 
2952: Silas started a little at the change to a questioning tone, and
2953: answered mildly, “I don’t know; I can’t rightly say—it’s a long while
2954: since.”
2955: 
2956: After receiving such an answer as this, it is not surprising that Mr.
2957: Macey observed, later on in the evening at the Rainbow, that Marner’s
2958: head was “all of a muddle”, and that it was to be doubted if he ever
2959: knew when Sunday came round, which showed him a worse heathen than many
2960: a dog.
2961: 
2962: Another of Silas’s comforters, besides Mr. Macey, came to him with a
2963: mind highly charged on the same topic. This was Mrs. Winthrop, the
2964: wheelwright’s wife. The inhabitants of Raveloe were not severely
2965: regular in their church-going, and perhaps there was hardly a person in
2966: the parish who would not have held that to go to church every Sunday in
2967: the calendar would have shown a greedy desire to stand well with
2968: Heaven, and get an undue advantage over their neighbours—a wish to be
2969: better than the “common run”, that would have implied a reflection on
2970: those who had had godfathers and godmothers as well as themselves, and
2971: had an equal right to the burying-service. At the same time, it was
2972: understood to be requisite for all who were not household servants, or
2973: young men, to take the sacrament at one of the great festivals: Squire
2974: Cass himself took it on Christmas-day; while those who were held to be
2975: “good livers” went to church with greater, though still with moderate,
2976: frequency.
2977: 
2978: Mrs. Winthrop was one of these: she was in all respects a woman of
2979: scrupulous conscience, so eager for duties that life seemed to offer
2980: them too scantily unless she rose at half-past four, though this threw
2981: a scarcity of work over the more advanced hours of the morning, which
2982: it was a constant problem with her to remove. Yet she had not the
2983: vixenish temper which is sometimes supposed to be a necessary condition
2984: of such habits: she was a very mild, patient woman, whose nature it was
2985: to seek out all the sadder and more serious elements of life, and
2986: pasture her mind upon them. She was the person always first thought of
2987: in Raveloe when there was illness or death in a family, when leeches
2988: were to be applied, or there was a sudden disappointment in a monthly
2989: nurse. She was a “comfortable woman”—good-looking, fresh-complexioned,
2990: having her lips always slightly screwed, as if she felt herself in a
2991: sick-room with the doctor or the clergyman present. But she was never
2992: whimpering; no one had seen her shed tears; she was simply grave and
2993: inclined to shake her head and sigh, almost imperceptibly, like a
2994: funereal mourner who is not a relation. It seemed surprising that Ben
2995: Winthrop, who loved his quart-pot and his joke, got along so well with
2996: Dolly; but she took her husband’s jokes and joviality as patiently as
2997: everything else, considering that “men _would_ be so”, and viewing the
2998: stronger sex in the light of animals whom it had pleased Heaven to make
2999: naturally troublesome, like bulls and turkey-cocks.
3000: 
3001: This good wholesome woman could hardly fail to have her mind drawn
3002: strongly towards Silas Marner, now that he appeared in the light of a
3003: sufferer; and one Sunday afternoon she took her little boy Aaron with
3004: her, and went to call on Silas, carrying in her hand some small
3005: lard-cakes, flat paste-like articles much esteemed in Raveloe. Aaron,
3006: an apple-cheeked youngster of seven, with a clean starched frill which
3007: looked like a plate for the apples, needed all his adventurous
3008: curiosity to embolden him against the possibility that the big-eyed
3009: weaver might do him some bodily injury; and his dubiety was much
3010: increased when, on arriving at the Stone-pits, they heard the
3011: mysterious sound of the loom.
3012: 
3013: “Ah, it is as I thought,” said Mrs. Winthrop, sadly.
3014: 
3015: They had to knock loudly before Silas heard them; but when he did come
3016: to the door he showed no impatience, as he would once have done, at a
3017: visit that had been unasked for and unexpected. Formerly, his heart had
3018: been as a locked casket with its treasure inside; but now the casket
3019: was empty, and the lock was broken. Left groping in darkness, with his
3020: prop utterly gone, Silas had inevitably a sense, though a dull and
3021: half-despairing one, that if any help came to him it must come from
3022: without; and there was a slight stirring of expectation at the sight of
3023: his fellow-men, a faint consciousness of dependence on their goodwill.
3024: He opened the door wide to admit Dolly, but without otherwise returning
3025: her greeting than by moving the armchair a few inches as a sign that
3026: she was to sit down in it. Dolly, as soon as she was seated, removed
3027: the white cloth that covered her lard-cakes, and said in her gravest
3028: way—
3029: 
3030: “I’d a baking yisterday, Master Marner, and the lard-cakes turned out
3031: better nor common, and I’d ha’ asked you to accept some, if you’d
3032: thought well. I don’t eat such things myself, for a bit o’ bread’s what
3033: I like from one year’s end to the other; but men’s stomichs are made so
3034: comical, they want a change—they do, I know, God help ’em.”
3035: 
3036: Dolly sighed gently as she held out the cakes to Silas, who thanked her
3037: kindly and looked very close at them, absently, being accustomed to
3038: look so at everything he took into his hand—eyed all the while by the
3039: wondering bright orbs of the small Aaron, who had made an outwork of
3040: his mother’s chair, and was peeping round from behind it.
3041: 
3042: “There’s letters pricked on ’em,” said Dolly. “I can’t read ’em myself,
3043: and there’s nobody, not Mr. Macey himself, rightly knows what they
3044: mean; but they’ve a good meaning, for they’re the same as is on the
3045: pulpit-cloth at church. What are they, Aaron, my dear?”
3046: 
3047: Aaron retreated completely behind his outwork.
3048: 
3049: “Oh, go, that’s naughty,” said his mother, mildly. “Well, whativer the
3050: letters are, they’ve a good meaning; and it’s a stamp as has been in
3051: our house, Ben says, ever since he was a little un, and his mother used
3052: to put it on the cakes, and I’ve allays put it on too; for if there’s
3053: any good, we’ve need of it i’ this world.”
3054: 
3055: “It’s I. H. S.,” said Silas, at which proof of learning Aaron peeped
3056: round the chair again.
3057: 
3058: “Well, to be sure, you can read ’em off,” said Dolly. “Ben’s read ’em
3059: to me many and many a time, but they slip out o’ my mind again; the
3060: more’s the pity, for they’re good letters, else they wouldn’t be in the
3061: church; and so I prick ’em on all the loaves and all the cakes, though
3062: sometimes they won’t hold, because o’ the rising—for, as I said, if
3063: there’s any good to be got we’ve need of it i’ this world—that we have;
3064: and I hope they’ll bring good to you, Master Marner, for it’s wi’ that
3065: will I brought you the cakes; and you see the letters have held better
3066: nor common.”
3067: 
3068: Silas was as unable to interpret the letters as Dolly, but there was no
3069: possibility of misunderstanding the desire to give comfort that made
3070: itself heard in her quiet tones. He said, with more feeling than
3071: before—“Thank you—thank you kindly.” But he laid down the cakes and
3072: seated himself absently—drearily unconscious of any distinct benefit
3073: towards which the cakes and the letters, or even Dolly’s kindness,
3074: could tend for him.
3075: 
3076: “Ah, if there’s good anywhere, we’ve need of it,” repeated Dolly, who
3077: did not lightly forsake a serviceable phrase. She looked at Silas
3078: pityingly as she went on. “But you didn’t hear the church-bells this
3079: morning, Master Marner? I doubt you didn’t know it was Sunday. Living
3080: so lone here, you lose your count, I daresay; and then, when your loom
3081: makes a noise, you can’t hear the bells, more partic’lar now the frost
3082: kills the sound.”
3083: 
3084: “Yes, I did; I heard ’em,” said Silas, to whom Sunday bells were a mere
3085: accident of the day, and not part of its sacredness. There had been no
3086: bells in Lantern Yard.
3087: 
3088: “Dear heart!” said Dolly, pausing before she spoke again. “But what a
3089: pity it is you should work of a Sunday, and not clean yourself—if you
3090: _didn’t_ go to church; for if you’d a roasting bit, it might be as you
3091: couldn’t leave it, being a lone man. But there’s the bakehus, if you
3092: could make up your mind to spend a twopence on the oven now and
3093: then,—not every week, in course—I shouldn’t like to do that myself,—you
3094: might carry your bit o’ dinner there, for it’s nothing but right to
3095: have a bit o’ summat hot of a Sunday, and not to make it as you can’t
3096: know your dinner from Saturday. But now, upo’ Christmas-day, this
3097: blessed Christmas as is ever coming, if you was to take your dinner to
3098: the bakehus, and go to church, and see the holly and the yew, and hear
3099: the anthim, and then take the sacramen’, you’d be a deal the better,
3100: and you’d know which end you stood on, and you could put your trust i’
3101: Them as knows better nor we do, seein’ you’d ha’ done what it lies on
3102: us all to do.”
3103: 
3104: Dolly’s exhortation, which was an unusually long effort of speech for
3105: her, was uttered in the soothing persuasive tone with which she would
3106: have tried to prevail on a sick man to take his medicine, or a basin of
3107: gruel for which he had no appetite. Silas had never before been closely
3108: urged on the point of his absence from church, which had only been
3109: thought of as a part of his general queerness; and he was too direct
3110: and simple to evade Dolly’s appeal.
3111: 
3112: “Nay, nay,” he said, “I know nothing o’ church. I’ve never been to
3113: church.”
3114: 
3115: “No!” said Dolly, in a low tone of wonderment. Then bethinking herself
3116: of Silas’s advent from an unknown country, she said, “Could it ha’ been
3117: as they’d no church where you was born?”
3118: 
3119: “Oh, yes,” said Silas, meditatively, sitting in his usual posture of
3120: leaning on his knees, and supporting his head. “There was churches—a
3121: many—it was a big town. But I knew nothing of ’em—I went to chapel.”
3122: 
3123: Dolly was much puzzled at this new word, but she was rather afraid of
3124: inquiring further, lest “chapel” might mean some haunt of wickedness.
3125: After a little thought, she said—
3126: 
3127: “Well, Master Marner, it’s niver too late to turn over a new leaf, and
3128: if you’ve niver had no church, there’s no telling the good it’ll do
3129: you. For I feel so set up and comfortable as niver was, when I’ve been
3130: and heard the prayers, and the singing to the praise and glory o’ God,
3131: as Mr. Macey gives out—and Mr. Crackenthorp saying good words, and more
3132: partic’lar on Sacramen’ Day; and if a bit o’ trouble comes, I feel as I
3133: can put up wi’ it, for I’ve looked for help i’ the right quarter, and
3134: gev myself up to Them as we must all give ourselves up to at the last;
3135: and if we’n done our part, it isn’t to be believed as Them as are above
3136: us ’ull be worse nor we are, and come short o’ Their’n.”
3137: 
3138: Poor Dolly’s exposition of her simple Raveloe theology fell rather
3139: unmeaningly on Silas’s ears, for there was no word in it that could
3140: rouse a memory of what he had known as religion, and his comprehension
3141: was quite baffled by the plural pronoun, which was no heresy of
3142: Dolly’s, but only her way of avoiding a presumptuous familiarity. He
3143: remained silent, not feeling inclined to assent to the part of Dolly’s
3144: speech which he fully understood—her recommendation that he should go
3145: to church. Indeed, Silas was so unaccustomed to talk beyond the brief
3146: questions and answers necessary for the transaction of his simple
3147: business, that words did not easily come to him without the urgency of
3148: a distinct purpose.
3149: 
3150: But now, little Aaron, having become used to the weaver’s awful
3151: presence, had advanced to his mother’s side, and Silas, seeming to
3152: notice him for the first time, tried to return Dolly’s signs of
3153: good-will by offering the lad a bit of lard-cake. Aaron shrank back a
3154: little, and rubbed his head against his mother’s shoulder, but still
3155: thought the piece of cake worth the risk of putting his hand out for
3156: it.
3157: 
3158: “Oh, for shame, Aaron,” said his mother, taking him on her lap,
3159: however; “why, you don’t want cake again yet awhile. He’s wonderful
3160: hearty,” she went on, with a little sigh—“that he is, God knows. He’s
3161: my youngest, and we spoil him sadly, for either me or the father must
3162: allays hev him in our sight—that we must.”
3163: 
3164: She stroked Aaron’s brown head, and thought it must do Master Marner
3165: good to see such a “pictur of a child”. But Marner, on the other side
3166: of the hearth, saw the neat-featured rosy face as a mere dim round,
3167: with two dark spots in it.
3168: 
3169: “And he’s got a voice like a bird—you wouldn’t think,” Dolly went on;
3170: “he can sing a Christmas carril as his father’s taught him; and I take
3171: it for a token as he’ll come to good, as he can learn the good tunes so
3172: quick. Come, Aaron, stan’ up and sing the carril to Master Marner,
3173: come.”
3174: 
3175: Aaron replied by rubbing his forehead against his mother’s shoulder.
3176: 
3177: “Oh, that’s naughty,” said Dolly, gently. “Stan’ up, when mother tells
3178: you, and let me hold the cake till you’ve done.”
3179: 
3180: Aaron was not indisposed to display his talents, even to an ogre, under
3181: protecting circumstances; and after a few more signs of coyness,
3182: consisting chiefly in rubbing the backs of his hands over his eyes, and
3183: then peeping between them at Master Marner, to see if he looked anxious
3184: for the “carril”, he at length allowed his head to be duly adjusted,
3185: and standing behind the table, which let him appear above it only as
3186: far as his broad frill, so that he looked like a cherubic head
3187: untroubled with a body, he began with a clear chirp, and in a melody
3188: that had the rhythm of an industrious hammer
3189: 
3190: “God rest you, merry gentlemen,
3191: Let nothing you dismay,
3192: For Jesus Christ our Savior
3193: Was born on Christmas-day.”
3194: 
3195: 
3196: Dolly listened with a devout look, glancing at Marner in some
3197: confidence that this strain would help to allure him to church.
3198: 
3199: “That’s Christmas music,” she said, when Aaron had ended, and had
3200: secured his piece of cake again. “There’s no other music equil to the
3201: Christmas music—“Hark the erol angils sing.” And you may judge what it
3202: is at church, Master Marner, with the bassoon and the voices, as you
3203: can’t help thinking you’ve got to a better place a’ready—for I wouldn’t
3204: speak ill o’ this world, seeing as Them put us in it as knows best—but
3205: what wi’ the drink, and the quarrelling, and the bad illnesses, and the
3206: hard dying, as I’ve seen times and times, one’s thankful to hear of a
3207: better. The boy sings pretty, don’t he, Master Marner?”
3208: 
3209: “Yes,” said Silas, absently, “very pretty.”
3210: 
3211: The Christmas carol, with its hammer-like rhythm, had fallen on his
3212: ears as strange music, quite unlike a hymn, and could have none of the
3213: effect Dolly contemplated. But he wanted to show her that he was
3214: grateful, and the only mode that occurred to him was to offer Aaron a
3215: bit more cake.
3216: 
3217: “Oh, no, thank you, Master Marner,” said Dolly, holding down Aaron’s
3218: willing hands. “We must be going home now. And so I wish you good-bye,
3219: Master Marner; and if you ever feel anyways bad in your inside, as you
3220: can’t fend for yourself, I’ll come and clean up for you, and get you a
3221: bit o’ victual, and willing. But I beg and pray of you to leave off
3222: weaving of a Sunday, for it’s bad for soul and body—and the money as
3223: comes i’ that way ’ull be a bad bed to lie down on at the last, if it
3224: doesn’t fly away, nobody knows where, like the white frost. And you’ll
3225: excuse me being that free with you, Master Marner, for I wish you
3226: well—I do. Make your bow, Aaron.”
3227: 
3228: Silas said “Good-bye, and thank you kindly,” as he opened the door for
3229: Dolly, but he couldn’t help feeling relieved when she was gone—relieved
3230: that he might weave again and moan at his ease. Her simple view of life
3231: and its comforts, by which she had tried to cheer him, was only like a
3232: report of unknown objects, which his imagination could not fashion. The
3233: fountains of human love and of faith in a divine love had not yet been
3234: unlocked, and his soul was still the shrunken rivulet, with only this
3235: difference, that its little groove of sand was blocked up, and it
3236: wandered confusedly against dark obstruction.
3237: 
3238: And so, notwithstanding the honest persuasions of Mr. Macey and Dolly
3239: Winthrop, Silas spent his Christmas-day in loneliness, eating his meat
3240: in sadness of heart, though the meat had come to him as a neighbourly
3241: present. In the morning he looked out on the black frost that seemed to
3242: press cruelly on every blade of grass, while the half-icy red pool
3243: shivered under the bitter wind; but towards evening the snow began to
3244: fall, and curtained from him even that dreary outlook, shutting him
3245: close up with his narrow grief. And he sat in his robbed home through
3246: the livelong evening, not caring to close his shutters or lock his
3247: door, pressing his head between his hands and moaning, till the cold
3248: grasped him and told him that his fire was grey.
3249: 
3250: Nobody in this world but himself knew that he was the same Silas Marner
3251: who had once loved his fellow with tender love, and trusted in an
3252: unseen goodness. Even to himself that past experience had become dim.
3253: 
3254: But in Raveloe village the bells rang merrily, and the church was
3255: fuller than all through the rest of the year, with red faces among the
3256: abundant dark-green boughs—faces prepared for a longer service than
3257: usual by an odorous breakfast of toast and ale. Those green boughs, the
3258: hymn and anthem never heard but at Christmas—even the Athanasian Creed,
3259: which was discriminated from the others only as being longer and of
3260: exceptional virtue, since it was only read on rare occasions—brought a
3261: vague exulting sense, for which the grown men could as little have
3262: found words as the children, that something great and mysterious had
3263: been done for them in heaven above and in earth below, which they were
3264: appropriating by their presence. And then the red faces made their way
3265: through the black biting frost to their own homes, feeling themselves
3266: free for the rest of the day to eat, drink, and be merry, and using
3267: that Christian freedom without diffidence.
3268: 
3269: At Squire Cass’s family party that day nobody mentioned Dunstan—nobody
3270: was sorry for his absence, or feared it would be too long. The doctor
3271: and his wife, uncle and aunt Kimble, were there, and the annual
3272: Christmas talk was carried through without any omissions, rising to the
3273: climax of Mr. Kimble’s experience when he walked the London hospitals
3274: thirty years back, together with striking professional anecdotes then
3275: gathered. Whereupon cards followed, with aunt Kimble’s annual failure
3276: to follow suit, and uncle Kimble’s irascibility concerning the odd
3277: trick which was rarely explicable to him, when it was not on his side,
3278: without a general visitation of tricks to see that they were formed on
3279: sound principles: the whole being accompanied by a strong steaming
3280: odour of spirits-and-water.
3281: 
3282: But the party on Christmas-day, being a strictly family party, was not
3283: the pre-eminently brilliant celebration of the season at the Red House.
3284: It was the great dance on New Year’s Eve that made the glory of Squire
3285: Cass’s hospitality, as of his forefathers’, time out of mind. This was
3286: the occasion when all the society of Raveloe and Tarley, whether old
3287: acquaintances separated by long rutty distances, or cooled
3288: acquaintances separated by misunderstandings concerning runaway calves,
3289: or acquaintances founded on intermittent condescension, counted on
3290: meeting and on comporting themselves with mutual appropriateness. This
3291: was the occasion on which fair dames who came on pillions sent their
3292: bandboxes before them, supplied with more than their evening costume;
3293: for the feast was not to end with a single evening, like a paltry town
3294: entertainment, where the whole supply of eatables is put on the table
3295: at once, and bedding is scanty. The Red House was provisioned as if for
3296: a siege; and as for the spare feather-beds ready to be laid on floors,
3297: they were as plentiful as might naturally be expected in a family that
3298: had killed its own geese for many generations.
3299: 
3300: Godfrey Cass was looking forward to this New Year’s Eve with a foolish
3301: reckless longing, that made him half deaf to his importunate companion,
3302: Anxiety.
3303: 
3304: “Dunsey will be coming home soon: there will be a great blow-up, and
3305: how will you bribe his spite to silence?” said Anxiety.
3306: 
3307: “Oh, he won’t come home before New Year’s Eve, perhaps,” said Godfrey;
3308: “and I shall sit by Nancy then, and dance with her, and get a kind look
3309: from her in spite of herself.”
3310: 
3311: “But money is wanted in another quarter,” said Anxiety, in a louder
3312: voice, “and how will you get it without selling your mother’s diamond
3313: pin? And if you don’t get it...?”
3314: 
3315: “Well, but something may happen to make things easier. At any rate,
3316: there’s one pleasure for me close at hand: Nancy is coming.”
3317: 
3318: “Yes, and suppose your father should bring matters to a pass that will
3319: oblige you to decline marrying her—and to give your reasons?”
3320: 
3321: “Hold your tongue, and don’t worry me. I can see Nancy’s eyes, just as
3322: they will look at me, and feel her hand in mine already.”
3323: 
3324: But Anxiety went on, though in noisy Christmas company; refusing to be
3325: utterly quieted even by much drinking.
3326: 
3327: 
3328: 
3329: CHAPTER XI.
3330: 
3331: Some women, I grant, would not appear to advantage seated on a pillion,
3332: and attired in a drab joseph and a drab beaver-bonnet, with a crown
3333: resembling a small stew-pan; for a garment suggesting a coachman’s
3334: greatcoat, cut out under an exiguity of cloth that would only allow of
3335: miniature capes, is not well adapted to conceal deficiencies of
3336: contour, nor is drab a colour that will throw sallow cheeks into lively
3337: contrast. It was all the greater triumph to Miss Nancy Lammeter’s
3338: beauty that she looked thoroughly bewitching in that costume, as,
3339: seated on the pillion behind her tall, erect father, she held one arm
3340: round him, and looked down, with open-eyed anxiety, at the treacherous
3341: snow-covered pools and puddles, which sent up formidable splashings of
3342: mud under the stamp of Dobbin’s foot. A painter would, perhaps, have
3343: preferred her in those moments when she was free from
3344: self-consciousness; but certainly the bloom on her cheeks was at its
3345: highest point of contrast with the surrounding drab when she arrived at
3346: the door of the Red House, and saw Mr. Godfrey Cass ready to lift her
3347: from the pillion. She wished her sister Priscilla had come up at the
3348: same time behind the servant, for then she would have contrived that
3349: Mr. Godfrey should have lifted off Priscilla first, and, in the
3350: meantime, she would have persuaded her father to go round to the
3351: horse-block instead of alighting at the door-steps. It was very
3352: painful, when you had made it quite clear to a young man that you were
3353: determined not to marry him, however much he might wish it, that he
3354: would still continue to pay you marked attentions; besides, why didn’t
3355: he always show the same attentions, if he meant them sincerely, instead
3356: of being so strange as Mr. Godfrey Cass was, sometimes behaving as if
3357: he didn’t want to speak to her, and taking no notice of her for weeks
3358: and weeks, and then, all on a sudden, almost making love again?
3359: Moreover, it was quite plain he had no real love for her, else he would
3360: not let people have _that_ to say of him which they did say. Did he
3361: suppose that Miss Nancy Lammeter was to be won by any man, squire or no
3362: squire, who led a bad life? That was not what she had been used to see
3363: in her own father, who was the soberest and best man in that
3364: country-side, only a little hot and hasty now and then, if things were
3365: not done to the minute.
3366: 
3367: All these thoughts rushed through Miss Nancy’s mind, in their habitual
3368: succession, in the moments between her first sight of Mr. Godfrey Cass
3369: standing at the door and her own arrival there. Happily, the Squire
3370: came out too and gave a loud greeting to her father, so that, somehow,
3371: under cover of this noise she seemed to find concealment for her
3372: confusion and neglect of any suitably formal behaviour, while she was
3373: being lifted from the pillion by strong arms which seemed to find her
3374: ridiculously small and light. And there was the best reason for
3375: hastening into the house at once, since the snow was beginning to fall
3376: again, threatening an unpleasant journey for such guests as were still
3377: on the road. These were a small minority; for already the afternoon was
3378: beginning to decline, and there would not be too much time for the
3379: ladies who came from a distance to attire themselves in readiness for
3380: the early tea which was to inspirit them for the dance.
3381: 
3382: There was a buzz of voices through the house, as Miss Nancy entered,
3383: mingled with the scrape of a fiddle preluding in the kitchen; but the
3384: Lammeters were guests whose arrival had evidently been thought of so
3385: much that it had been watched for from the windows, for Mrs. Kimble,
3386: who did the honours at the Red House on these great occasions, came
3387: forward to meet Miss Nancy in the hall, and conduct her up-stairs. Mrs.
3388: Kimble was the Squire’s sister, as well as the doctor’s wife—a double
3389: dignity, with which her diameter was in direct proportion; so that, a
3390: journey up-stairs being rather fatiguing to her, she did not oppose
3391: Miss Nancy’s request to be allowed to find her way alone to the Blue
3392: Room, where the Miss Lammeters’ bandboxes had been deposited on their
3393: arrival in the morning.
3394: 
3395: There was hardly a bedroom in the house where feminine compliments were
3396: not passing and feminine toilettes going forward, in various stages, in
3397: space made scanty by extra beds spread upon the floor; and Miss Nancy,
3398: as she entered the Blue Room, had to make her little formal curtsy to a
3399: group of six. On the one hand, there were ladies no less important than
3400: the two Miss Gunns, the wine merchant’s daughters from Lytherly,
3401: dressed in the height of fashion, with the tightest skirts and the
3402: shortest waists, and gazed at by Miss Ladbrook (of the Old Pastures)
3403: with a shyness not unsustained by inward criticism. Partly, Miss
3404: Ladbrook felt that her own skirt must be regarded as unduly lax by the
3405: Miss Gunns, and partly, that it was a pity the Miss Gunns did not show
3406: that judgment which she herself would show if she were in their place,
3407: by stopping a little on this side of the fashion. On the other hand,
3408: Mrs. Ladbrook was standing in skull-cap and front, with her turban in
3409: her hand, curtsying and smiling blandly and saying, “After you, ma’am,”
3410: to another lady in similar circumstances, who had politely offered the
3411: precedence at the looking-glass.
3412: 
3413: But Miss Nancy had no sooner made her curtsy than an elderly lady came
3414: forward, whose full white muslin kerchief, and mob-cap round her curls
3415: of smooth grey hair, were in daring contrast with the puffed yellow
3416: satins and top-knotted caps of her neighbours. She approached Miss
3417: Nancy with much primness, and said, with a slow, treble suavity—
3418: 
3419: “Niece, I hope I see you well in health.” Miss Nancy kissed her aunt’s
3420: cheek dutifully, and answered, with the same sort of amiable primness,
3421: “Quite well, I thank you, aunt; and I hope I see you the same.”
3422: 
3423: “Thank you, niece; I keep my health for the present. And how is my
3424: brother-in-law?”
3425: 
3426: These dutiful questions and answers were continued until it was
3427: ascertained in detail that the Lammeters were all as well as usual, and
3428: the Osgoods likewise, also that niece Priscilla must certainly arrive
3429: shortly, and that travelling on pillions in snowy weather was
3430: unpleasant, though a joseph was a great protection. Then Nancy was
3431: formally introduced to her aunt’s visitors, the Miss Gunns, as being
3432: the daughters of a mother known to _their_ mother, though now for the
3433: first time induced to make a journey into these parts; and these ladies
3434: were so taken by surprise at finding such a lovely face and figure in
3435: an out-of-the-way country place, that they began to feel some curiosity
3436: about the dress she would put on when she took off her joseph. Miss
3437: Nancy, whose thoughts were always conducted with the propriety and
3438: moderation conspicuous in her manners, remarked to herself that the
3439: Miss Gunns were rather hard-featured than otherwise, and that such very
3440: low dresses as they wore might have been attributed to vanity if their
3441: shoulders had been pretty, but that, being as they were, it was not
3442: reasonable to suppose that they showed their necks from a love of
3443: display, but rather from some obligation not inconsistent with sense
3444: and modesty. She felt convinced, as she opened her box, that this must
3445: be her aunt Osgood’s opinion, for Miss Nancy’s mind resembled her
3446: aunt’s to a degree that everybody said was surprising, considering the
3447: kinship was on Mr. Osgood’s side; and though you might not have
3448: supposed it from the formality of their greeting, there was a devoted
3449: attachment and mutual admiration between aunt and niece. Even Miss
3450: Nancy’s refusal of her cousin Gilbert Osgood (on the ground solely that
3451: he was her cousin), though it had grieved her aunt greatly, had not in
3452: the least cooled the preference which had determined her to leave Nancy
3453: several of her hereditary ornaments, let Gilbert’s future wife be whom
3454: she might.
3455: 
3456: Three of the ladies quickly retired, but the Miss Gunns were quite
3457: content that Mrs. Osgood’s inclination to remain with her niece gave
3458: them also a reason for staying to see the rustic beauty’s toilette. And
3459: it was really a pleasure—from the first opening of the bandbox, where
3460: everything smelt of lavender and rose-leaves, to the clasping of the
3461: small coral necklace that fitted closely round her little white neck.
3462: Everything belonging to Miss Nancy was of delicate purity and
3463: nattiness: not a crease was where it had no business to be, not a bit
3464: of her linen professed whiteness without fulfilling its profession; the
3465: very pins on her pincushion were stuck in after a pattern from which
3466: she was careful to allow no aberration; and as for her own person, it
3467: gave the same idea of perfect unvarying neatness as the body of a
3468: little bird. It is true that her light-brown hair was cropped behind
3469: like a boy’s, and was dressed in front in a number of flat rings, that
3470: lay quite away from her face; but there was no sort of coiffure that
3471: could make Miss Nancy’s cheek and neck look otherwise than pretty; and
3472: when at last she stood complete in her silvery twilled silk, her lace
3473: tucker, her coral necklace, and coral ear-drops, the Miss Gunns could
3474: see nothing to criticise except her hands, which bore the traces of
3475: butter-making, cheese-crushing, and even still coarser work. But Miss
3476: Nancy was not ashamed of that, for even while she was dressing she
3477: narrated to her aunt how she and Priscilla had packed their boxes
3478: yesterday, because this morning was baking morning, and since they were
3479: leaving home, it was desirable to make a good supply of meat-pies for
3480: the kitchen; and as she concluded this judicious remark, she turned to
3481: the Miss Gunns that she might not commit the rudeness of not including
3482: them in the conversation. The Miss Gunns smiled stiffly, and thought
3483: what a pity it was that these rich country people, who could afford to
3484: buy such good clothes (really Miss Nancy’s lace and silk were very
3485: costly), should be brought up in utter ignorance and vulgarity. She
3486: actually said “mate” for “meat”, “’appen” for “perhaps”, and “oss” for
3487: “horse”, which, to young ladies living in good Lytherly society, who
3488: habitually said ’orse, even in domestic privacy, and only said ’appen
3489: on the right occasions, was necessarily shocking. Miss Nancy, indeed,
3490: had never been to any school higher than Dame Tedman’s: her
3491: acquaintance with profane literature hardly went beyond the rhymes she
3492: had worked in her large sampler under the lamb and the shepherdess; and
3493: in order to balance an account, she was obliged to effect her
3494: subtraction by removing visible metallic shillings and sixpences from a
3495: visible metallic total. There is hardly a servant-maid in these days
3496: who is not better informed than Miss Nancy; yet she had the essential
3497: attributes of a lady—high veracity, delicate honour in her dealings,
3498: deference to others, and refined personal habits,—and lest these should
3499: not suffice to convince grammatical fair ones that her feelings can at
3500: all resemble theirs, I will add that she was slightly proud and
3501: exacting, and as constant in her affection towards a baseless opinion
3502: as towards an erring lover.
3503: 
3504: The anxiety about sister Priscilla, which had grown rather active by
3505: the time the coral necklace was clasped, was happily ended by the
3506: entrance of that cheerful-looking lady herself, with a face made blowsy
3507: by cold and damp. After the first questions and greetings, she turned
3508: to Nancy, and surveyed her from head to foot—then wheeled her round, to
3509: ascertain that the back view was equally faultless.
3510: 
3511: “What do you think o’ _these_ gowns, aunt Osgood?” said Priscilla,
3512: while Nancy helped her to unrobe.
3513: 
3514: “Very handsome indeed, niece,” said Mrs. Osgood, with a slight increase
3515: of formality. She always thought niece Priscilla too rough.
3516: 
3517: “I’m obliged to have the same as Nancy, you know, for all I’m five
3518: years older, and it makes me look yallow; for she never _will_ have
3519: anything without I have mine just like it, because she wants us to look
3520: like sisters. And I tell her, folks ’ull think it’s my weakness makes
3521: me fancy as I shall look pretty in what she looks pretty in. For I _am_
3522: ugly—there’s no denying that: I feature my father’s family. But, law! I
3523: don’t mind, do you?” Priscilla here turned to the Miss Gunns, rattling
3524: on in too much preoccupation with the delight of talking, to notice
3525: that her candour was not appreciated. “The pretty uns do for
3526: fly-catchers—they keep the men off us. I’ve no opinion o’ the men, Miss
3527: Gunn—I don’t know what _you_ have. And as for fretting and stewing
3528: about what _they_’ll think of you from morning till night, and making
3529: your life uneasy about what they’re doing when they’re out o’ your
3530: sight—as I tell Nancy, it’s a folly no woman need be guilty of, if
3531: she’s got a good father and a good home: let her leave it to them as
3532: have got no fortin, and can’t help themselves. As I say, Mr.
3533: Have-your-own-way is the best husband, and the only one I’d ever
3534: promise to obey. I know it isn’t pleasant, when you’ve been used to
3535: living in a big way, and managing hogsheads and all that, to go and put
3536: your nose in by somebody else’s fireside, or to sit down by yourself to
3537: a scrag or a knuckle; but, thank God! my father’s a sober man and
3538: likely to live; and if you’ve got a man by the chimney-corner, it
3539: doesn’t matter if he’s childish—the business needn’t be broke up.”
3540: 
3541: The delicate process of getting her narrow gown over her head without
3542: injury to her smooth curls, obliged Miss Priscilla to pause in this
3543: rapid survey of life, and Mrs. Osgood seized the opportunity of rising
3544: and saying—
3545: 
3546: “Well, niece, you’ll follow us. The Miss Gunns will like to go down.”
3547: 
3548: “Sister,” said Nancy, when they were alone, “you’ve offended the Miss
3549: Gunns, I’m sure.”
3550: 
3551: “What have I done, child?” said Priscilla, in some alarm.
3552: 
3553: “Why, you asked them if they minded about being ugly—you’re so very
3554: blunt.”
3555: 
3556: “Law, did I? Well, it popped out: it’s a mercy I said no more, for I’m
3557: a bad un to live with folks when they don’t like the truth. But as for
3558: being ugly, look at me, child, in this silver-coloured silk—I told you
3559: how it ’ud be—I look as yallow as a daffadil. Anybody ’ud say you
3560: wanted to make a mawkin of me.”
3561: 
3562: “No, Priscy, don’t say so. I begged and prayed of you not to let us
3563: have this silk if you’d like another better. I was willing to have
3564: _your_ choice, you know I was,” said Nancy, in anxious
3565: self-vindication.
3566: 
3567: “Nonsense, child! you know you’d set your heart on this; and reason
3568: good, for you’re the colour o’ cream. It ’ud be fine doings for you to
3569: dress yourself to suit _my_ skin. What I find fault with, is that
3570: notion o’ yours as I must dress myself just like you. But you do as you
3571: like with me—you always did, from when first you begun to walk. If you
3572: wanted to go the field’s length, the field’s length you’d go; and there
3573: was no whipping you, for you looked as prim and innicent as a daisy all
3574: the while.”
3575: 
3576: “Priscy,” said Nancy, gently, as she fastened a coral necklace, exactly
3577: like her own, round Priscilla’s neck, which was very far from being
3578: like her own, “I’m sure I’m willing to give way as far as is right, but
3579: who shouldn’t dress alike if it isn’t sisters? Would you have us go
3580: about looking as if we were no kin to one another—us that have got no
3581: mother and not another sister in the world? I’d do what was right, if I
3582: dressed in a gown dyed with cheese-colouring; and I’d rather you’d
3583: choose, and let me wear what pleases you.”
3584: 
3585: “There you go again! You’d come round to the same thing if one talked
3586: to you from Saturday night till Saturday morning. It’ll be fine fun to
3587: see how you’ll master your husband and never raise your voice above the
3588: singing o’ the kettle all the while. I like to see the men mastered!”
3589: 
3590: “Don’t talk _so_, Priscy,” said Nancy, blushing. “You know I don’t mean
3591: ever to be married.”
3592: 
3593: “Oh, you never mean a fiddlestick’s end!” said Priscilla, as she
3594: arranged her discarded dress, and closed her bandbox. “Who shall _I_
3595: have to work for when father’s gone, if you are to go and take notions
3596: in your head and be an old maid, because some folks are no better than
3597: they should be? I haven’t a bit o’ patience with you—sitting on an
3598: addled egg for ever, as if there was never a fresh un in the world. One
3599: old maid’s enough out o’ two sisters; and I shall do credit to a single
3600: life, for God A’mighty meant me for it. Come, we can go down now. I’m
3601: as ready as a mawkin _can_ be—there’s nothing awanting to frighten the
3602: crows, now I’ve got my ear-droppers in.”
3603: 
3604: As the two Miss Lammeters walked into the large parlour together, any
3605: one who did not know the character of both might certainly have
3606: supposed that the reason why the square-shouldered, clumsy,
3607: high-featured Priscilla wore a dress the facsimile of her pretty
3608: sister’s, was either the mistaken vanity of the one, or the malicious
3609: contrivance of the other in order to set off her own rare beauty. But
3610: the good-natured self-forgetful cheeriness and common-sense of
3611: Priscilla would soon have dissipated the one suspicion; and the modest
3612: calm of Nancy’s speech and manners told clearly of a mind free from all
3613: disavowed devices.
3614: 
3615: Places of honour had been kept for the Miss Lammeters near the head of
3616: the principal tea-table in the wainscoted parlour, now looking fresh
3617: and pleasant with handsome branches of holly, yew, and laurel, from the
3618: abundant growths of the old garden; and Nancy felt an inward flutter,
3619: that no firmness of purpose could prevent, when she saw Mr. Godfrey
3620: Cass advancing to lead her to a seat between himself and Mr.
3621: Crackenthorp, while Priscilla was called to the opposite side between
3622: her father and the Squire. It certainly did make some difference to
3623: Nancy that the lover she had given up was the young man of quite the
3624: highest consequence in the parish—at home in a venerable and unique
3625: parlour, which was the extremity of grandeur in her experience, a
3626: parlour where _she_ might one day have been mistress, with the
3627: consciousness that she was spoken of as “Madam Cass”, the Squire’s
3628: wife. These circumstances exalted her inward drama in her own eyes, and
3629: deepened the emphasis with which she declared to herself that not the
3630: most dazzling rank should induce her to marry a man whose conduct
3631: showed him careless of his character, but that, “love once, love
3632: always”, was the motto of a true and pure woman, and no man should ever
3633: have any right over her which would be a call on her to destroy the
3634: dried flowers that she treasured, and always would treasure, for
3635: Godfrey Cass’s sake. And Nancy was capable of keeping her word to
3636: herself under very trying conditions. Nothing but a becoming blush
3637: betrayed the moving thoughts that urged themselves upon her as she
3638: accepted the seat next to Mr. Crackenthorp; for she was so
3639: instinctively neat and adroit in all her actions, and her pretty lips
3640: met each other with such quiet firmness, that it would have been
3641: difficult for her to appear agitated.
3642: 
3643: It was not the rector’s practice to let a charming blush pass without
3644: an appropriate compliment. He was not in the least lofty or
3645: aristocratic, but simply a merry-eyed, small-featured, grey-haired man,
3646: with his chin propped by an ample, many-creased white neckcloth which
3647: seemed to predominate over every other point in his person, and somehow
3648: to impress its peculiar character on his remarks; so that to have
3649: considered his amenities apart from his cravat would have been a
3650: severe, and perhaps a dangerous, effort of abstraction.
3651: 
3652: “Ha, Miss Nancy,” he said, turning his head within his cravat and
3653: smiling down pleasantly upon her, “when anybody pretends this has been
3654: a severe winter, I shall tell them I saw the roses blooming on New
3655: Year’s Eve—eh, Godfrey, what do _you_ say?”
3656: 
3657: Godfrey made no reply, and avoided looking at Nancy very markedly; for
3658: though these complimentary personalities were held to be in excellent
3659: taste in old-fashioned Raveloe society, reverent love has a politeness
3660: of its own which it teaches to men otherwise of small schooling. But
3661: the Squire was rather impatient at Godfrey’s showing himself a dull
3662: spark in this way. By this advanced hour of the day, the Squire was
3663: always in higher spirits than we have seen him in at the
3664: breakfast-table, and felt it quite pleasant to fulfil the hereditary
3665: duty of being noisily jovial and patronizing: the large silver
3666: snuff-box was in active service and was offered without fail to all
3667: neighbours from time to time, however often they might have declined
3668: the favour. At present, the Squire had only given an express welcome to
3669: the heads of families as they appeared; but always as the evening
3670: deepened, his hospitality rayed out more widely, till he had tapped the
3671: youngest guests on the back and shown a peculiar fondness for their
3672: presence, in the full belief that they must feel their lives made happy
3673: by their belonging to a parish where there was such a hearty man as
3674: Squire Cass to invite them and wish them well. Even in this early stage
3675: of the jovial mood, it was natural that he should wish to supply his
3676: son’s deficiencies by looking and speaking for him.
3677: 
3678: “Aye, aye,” he began, offering his snuff-box to Mr. Lammeter, who for
3679: the second time bowed his head and waved his hand in stiff rejection of
3680: the offer, “us old fellows may wish ourselves young to-night, when we
3681: see the mistletoe-bough in the White Parlour. It’s true, most things
3682: are gone back’ard in these last thirty years—the country’s going down
3683: since the old king fell ill. But when I look at Miss Nancy here, I
3684: begin to think the lasses keep up their quality;—ding me if I remember
3685: a sample to match her, not when I was a fine young fellow, and thought
3686: a deal about my pigtail. No offence to you, madam,” he added, bending
3687: to Mrs. Crackenthorp, who sat by him, “I didn’t know _you_ when you
3688: were as young as Miss Nancy here.”
3689: 
3690: Mrs. Crackenthorp—a small blinking woman, who fidgeted incessantly with
3691: her lace, ribbons, and gold chain, turning her head about and making
3692: subdued noises, very much like a guinea-pig that twitches its nose and
3693: soliloquizes in all company indiscriminately—now blinked and fidgeted
3694: towards the Squire, and said, “Oh, no—no offence.”
3695: 
3696: This emphatic compliment of the Squire’s to Nancy was felt by others
3697: besides Godfrey to have a diplomatic significance; and her father gave
3698: a slight additional erectness to his back, as he looked across the
3699: table at her with complacent gravity. That grave and orderly senior was
3700: not going to bate a jot of his dignity by seeming elated at the notion
3701: of a match between his family and the Squire’s: he was gratified by any
3702: honour paid to his daughter; but he must see an alteration in several
3703: ways before his consent would be vouchsafed. His spare but healthy
3704: person, and high-featured firm face, that looked as if it had never
3705: been flushed by excess, was in strong contrast, not only with the
3706: Squire’s, but with the appearance of the Raveloe farmers generally—in
3707: accordance with a favourite saying of his own, that “breed was stronger
3708: than pasture”.
3709: 
3710: “Miss Nancy’s wonderful like what her mother was, though; isn’t she,
3711: Kimble?” said the stout lady of that name, looking round for her
3712: husband.
3713: 
3714: But Doctor Kimble (country apothecaries in old days enjoyed that title
3715: without authority of diploma), being a thin and agile man, was flitting
3716: about the room with his hands in his pockets, making himself agreeable
3717: to his feminine patients, with medical impartiality, and being welcomed
3718: everywhere as a doctor by hereditary right—not one of those miserable
3719: apothecaries who canvass for practice in strange neighbourhoods, and
3720: spend all their income in starving their one horse, but a man of
3721: substance, able to keep an extravagant table like the best of his
3722: patients. Time out of mind the Raveloe doctor had been a Kimble; Kimble
3723: was inherently a doctor’s name; and it was difficult to contemplate
3724: firmly the melancholy fact that the actual Kimble had no son, so that
3725: his practice might one day be handed over to a successor with the
3726: incongruous name of Taylor or Johnson. But in that case the wiser
3727: people in Raveloe would employ Dr. Blick of Flitton—as less unnatural.
3728: 
3729: “Did you speak to me, my dear?” said the authentic doctor, coming
3730: quickly to his wife’s side; but, as if foreseeing that she would be too
3731: much out of breath to repeat her remark, he went on immediately—“Ha,
3732: Miss Priscilla, the sight of you revives the taste of that
3733: super-excellent pork-pie. I hope the batch isn’t near an end.”
3734: 
3735: “Yes, indeed, it is, doctor,” said Priscilla; “but I’ll answer for it
3736: the next shall be as good. My pork-pies don’t turn out well by chance.”
3737: 
3738: “Not as your doctoring does, eh, Kimble?—because folks forget to take
3739: your physic, eh?” said the Squire, who regarded physic and doctors as
3740: many loyal churchmen regard the church and the clergy—tasting a joke
3741: against them when he was in health, but impatiently eager for their aid
3742: when anything was the matter with him. He tapped his box, and looked
3743: round with a triumphant laugh.
3744: 
3745: “Ah, she has a quick wit, my friend Priscilla has,” said the doctor,
3746: choosing to attribute the epigram to a lady rather than allow a
3747: brother-in-law that advantage over him. “She saves a little pepper to
3748: sprinkle over her talk—that’s the reason why she never puts too much
3749: into her pies. There’s my wife now, she never has an answer at her
3750: tongue’s end; but if I offend her, she’s sure to scarify my throat with
3751: black pepper the next day, or else give me the colic with watery
3752: greens. That’s an awful tit-for-tat.” Here the vivacious doctor made a
3753: pathetic grimace.
3754: 
3755: “Did you ever hear the like?” said Mrs. Kimble, laughing above her
3756: double chin with much good-humour, aside to Mrs. Crackenthorp, who
3757: blinked and nodded, and seemed to intend a smile, which, by the
3758: correlation of forces, went off in small twitchings and noises.
3759: 
3760: “I suppose that’s the sort of tit-for-tat adopted in your profession,
3761: Kimble, if you’ve a grudge against a patient,” said the rector.
3762: 
3763: “Never do have a grudge against our patients,” said Mr. Kimble, “except
3764: when they leave us: and then, you see, we haven’t the chance of
3765: prescribing for ’em. Ha, Miss Nancy,” he continued, suddenly skipping
3766: to Nancy’s side, “you won’t forget your promise? You’re to save a dance
3767: for me, you know.”
3768: 
3769: “Come, come, Kimble, don’t you be too for’ard,” said the Squire. “Give
3770: the young uns fair-play. There’s my son Godfrey’ll be wanting to have a
3771: round with you if you run off with Miss Nancy. He’s bespoke her for the
3772: first dance, I’ll be bound. Eh, sir! what do you say?” he continued,
3773: throwing himself backward, and looking at Godfrey. “Haven’t you asked
3774: Miss Nancy to open the dance with you?”
3775: 
3776: Godfrey, sorely uncomfortable under this significant insistence about
3777: Nancy, and afraid to think where it would end by the time his father
3778: had set his usual hospitable example of drinking before and after
3779: supper, saw no course open but to turn to Nancy and say, with as little
3780: awkwardness as possible—
3781: 
3782: “No; I’ve not asked her yet, but I hope she’ll consent—if somebody else
3783: hasn’t been before me.”
3784: 
3785: “No, I’ve not engaged myself,” said Nancy, quietly, though blushingly.
3786: (If Mr. Godfrey founded any hopes on her consenting to dance with him,
3787: he would soon be undeceived; but there was no need for her to be
3788: uncivil.)
3789:
