3790: “Then I hope you’ve no objections to dancing with me,” said Godfrey,
3791: beginning to lose the sense that there was anything uncomfortable in
3792: this arrangement.
3793: 
3794: “No, no objections,” said Nancy, in a cold tone.
3795: 
3796: “Ah, well, you’re a lucky fellow, Godfrey,” said uncle Kimble; “but
3797: you’re my godson, so I won’t stand in your way. Else I’m not so very
3798: old, eh, my dear?” he went on, skipping to his wife’s side again. “You
3799: wouldn’t mind my having a second after you were gone—not if I cried a
3800: good deal first?”
3801: 
3802: “Come, come, take a cup o’ tea and stop your tongue, do,” said
3803: good-humoured Mrs. Kimble, feeling some pride in a husband who must be
3804: regarded as so clever and amusing by the company generally. If he had
3805: only not been irritable at cards!
3806: 
3807: While safe, well-tested personalities were enlivening the tea in this
3808: way, the sound of the fiddle approaching within a distance at which it
3809: could be heard distinctly, made the young people look at each other
3810: with sympathetic impatience for the end of the meal.
3811: 
3812: “Why, there’s Solomon in the hall,” said the Squire, “and playing my
3813: fav’rite tune, _I_ believe—“The flaxen-headed ploughboy”—he’s for
3814: giving us a hint as we aren’t enough in a hurry to hear him play. Bob,”
3815: he called out to his third long-legged son, who was at the other end of
3816: the room, “open the door, and tell Solomon to come in. He shall give us
3817: a tune here.”
3818: 
3819: Bob obeyed, and Solomon walked in, fiddling as he walked, for he would
3820: on no account break off in the middle of a tune.
3821: 
3822: “Here, Solomon,” said the Squire, with loud patronage. “Round here, my
3823: man. Ah, I knew it was “The flaxen-headed ploughboy”: there’s no finer
3824: tune.”
3825: 
3826: Solomon Macey, a small hale old man with an abundant crop of long white
3827: hair reaching nearly to his shoulders, advanced to the indicated spot,
3828: bowing reverently while he fiddled, as much as to say that he respected
3829: the company, though he respected the key-note more. As soon as he had
3830: repeated the tune and lowered his fiddle, he bowed again to the Squire
3831: and the rector, and said, “I hope I see your honour and your reverence
3832: well, and wishing you health and long life and a happy New Year. And
3833: wishing the same to you, Mr. Lammeter, sir; and to the other gentlemen,
3834: and the madams, and the young lasses.”
3835: 
3836: As Solomon uttered the last words, he bowed in all directions
3837: solicitously, lest he should be wanting in due respect. But thereupon
3838: he immediately began to prelude, and fell into the tune which he knew
3839: would be taken as a special compliment by Mr. Lammeter.
3840: 
3841: “Thank ye, Solomon, thank ye,” said Mr. Lammeter when the fiddle paused
3842: again. “That’s “Over the hills and far away”, that is. My father used
3843: to say to me, whenever we heard that tune, “Ah, lad, _I_ come from over
3844: the hills and far away.” There’s a many tunes I don’t make head or tail
3845: of; but that speaks to me like the blackbird’s whistle. I suppose it’s
3846: the name: there’s a deal in the name of a tune.”
3847: 
3848: But Solomon was already impatient to prelude again, and presently broke
3849: with much spirit into “Sir Roger de Coverley”, at which there was a
3850: sound of chairs pushed back, and laughing voices.
3851: 
3852: “Aye, aye, Solomon, we know what that means,” said the Squire, rising.
3853: “It’s time to begin the dance, eh? Lead the way, then, and we’ll all
3854: follow you.”
3855: 
3856: So Solomon, holding his white head on one side, and playing vigorously,
3857: marched forward at the head of the gay procession into the White
3858: Parlour, where the mistletoe-bough was hung, and multitudinous tallow
3859: candles made rather a brilliant effect, gleaming from among the berried
3860: holly-boughs, and reflected in the old-fashioned oval mirrors fastened
3861: in the panels of the white wainscot. A quaint procession! Old Solomon,
3862: in his seedy clothes and long white locks, seemed to be luring that
3863: decent company by the magic scream of his fiddle—luring discreet
3864: matrons in turban-shaped caps, nay, Mrs. Crackenthorp herself, the
3865: summit of whose perpendicular feather was on a level with the Squire’s
3866: shoulder—luring fair lasses complacently conscious of very short waists
3867: and skirts blameless of front-folds—luring burly fathers in large
3868: variegated waistcoats, and ruddy sons, for the most part shy and
3869: sheepish, in short nether garments and very long coat-tails.
3870: 
3871: Already Mr. Macey and a few other privileged villagers, who were
3872: allowed to be spectators on these great occasions, were seated on
3873: benches placed for them near the door; and great was the admiration and
3874: satisfaction in that quarter when the couples had formed themselves for
3875: the dance, and the Squire led off with Mrs. Crackenthorp, joining hands
3876: with the rector and Mrs. Osgood. That was as it should be—that was what
3877: everybody had been used to—and the charter of Raveloe seemed to be
3878: renewed by the ceremony. It was not thought of as an unbecoming levity
3879: for the old and middle-aged people to dance a little before sitting
3880: down to cards, but rather as part of their social duties. For what were
3881: these if not to be merry at appropriate times, interchanging visits and
3882: poultry with due frequency, paying each other old-established
3883: compliments in sound traditional phrases, passing well-tried personal
3884: jokes, urging your guests to eat and drink too much out of hospitality,
3885: and eating and drinking too much in your neighbour’s house to show that
3886: you liked your cheer? And the parson naturally set an example in these
3887: social duties. For it would not have been possible for the Raveloe
3888: mind, without a peculiar revelation, to know that a clergyman should be
3889: a pale-faced memento of solemnities, instead of a reasonably faulty man
3890: whose exclusive authority to read prayers and preach, to christen,
3891: marry, and bury you, necessarily coexisted with the right to sell you
3892: the ground to be buried in and to take tithe in kind; on which last
3893: point, of course, there was a little grumbling, but not to the extent
3894: of irreligion—not of deeper significance than the grumbling at the
3895: rain, which was by no means accompanied with a spirit of impious
3896: defiance, but with a desire that the prayer for fine weather might be
3897: read forthwith.
3898: 
3899: There was no reason, then, why the rector’s dancing should not be
3900: received as part of the fitness of things quite as much as the
3901: Squire’s, or why, on the other hand, Mr. Macey’s official respect
3902: should restrain him from subjecting the parson’s performance to that
3903: criticism with which minds of extraordinary acuteness must necessarily
3904: contemplate the doings of their fallible fellow-men.
3905: 
3906: “The Squire’s pretty springe, considering his weight,” said Mr. Macey,
3907: “and he stamps uncommon well. But Mr. Lammeter beats ’em all for
3908: shapes: you see he holds his head like a sodger, and he isn’t so
3909: cushiony as most o’ the oldish gentlefolks—they run fat in general; and
3910: he’s got a fine leg. The parson’s nimble enough, but he hasn’t got much
3911: of a leg: it’s a bit too thick down’ard, and his knees might be a bit
3912: nearer wi’out damage; but he might do worse, he might do worse. Though
3913: he hasn’t that grand way o’ waving his hand as the Squire has.”
3914: 
3915: “Talk o’ nimbleness, look at Mrs. Osgood,” said Ben Winthrop, who was
3916: holding his son Aaron between his knees. “She trips along with her
3917: little steps, so as nobody can see how she goes—it’s like as if she had
3918: little wheels to her feet. She doesn’t look a day older nor last year:
3919: she’s the finest-made woman as is, let the next be where she will.”
3920: 
3921: “I don’t heed how the women are made,” said Mr. Macey, with some
3922: contempt. “They wear nayther coat nor breeches: you can’t make much out
3923: o’ their shapes.”
3924: 
3925: “Fayder,” said Aaron, whose feet were busy beating out the tune, “how
3926: does that big cock’s-feather stick in Mrs. Crackenthorp’s yead? Is
3927: there a little hole for it, like in my shuttle-cock?”
3928: 
3929: “Hush, lad, hush; that’s the way the ladies dress theirselves, that
3930: is,” said the father, adding, however, in an undertone to Mr. Macey,
3931: “It does make her look funny, though—partly like a short-necked bottle
3932: wi’ a long quill in it. Hey, by jingo, there’s the young Squire leading
3933: off now, wi’ Miss Nancy for partners! There’s a lass for you!—like a
3934: pink-and-white posy—there’s nobody ’ud think as anybody could be so
3935: pritty. I shouldn’t wonder if she’s Madam Cass some day, arter all—and
3936: nobody more rightfuller, for they’d make a fine match. You can find
3937: nothing against Master Godfrey’s shapes, Macey, _I_’ll bet a penny.”
3938: 
3939: Mr. Macey screwed up his mouth, leaned his head further on one side,
3940: and twirled his thumbs with a presto movement as his eyes followed
3941: Godfrey up the dance. At last he summed up his opinion.
3942: 
3943: “Pretty well down’ard, but a bit too round i’ the shoulder-blades. And
3944: as for them coats as he gets from the Flitton tailor, they’re a poor
3945: cut to pay double money for.”
3946: 
3947: “Ah, Mr. Macey, you and me are two folks,” said Ben, slightly indignant
3948: at this carping. “When I’ve got a pot o’ good ale, I like to swaller
3949: it, and do my inside good, i’stead o’ smelling and staring at it to see
3950: if I can’t find faut wi’ the brewing. I should like you to pick me out
3951: a finer-limbed young fellow nor Master Godfrey—one as ’ud knock you
3952: down easier, or ’s more pleasanter-looksed when he’s piert and merry.”
3953: 
3954: “Tchuh!” said Mr. Macey, provoked to increased severity, “he isn’t come
3955: to his right colour yet: he’s partly like a slack-baked pie. And I
3956: doubt he’s got a soft place in his head, else why should he be turned
3957: round the finger by that offal Dunsey as nobody’s seen o’ late, and let
3958: him kill that fine hunting hoss as was the talk o’ the country? And one
3959: while he was allays after Miss Nancy, and then it all went off again,
3960: like a smell o’ hot porridge, as I may say. That wasn’t my way when _I_
3961: went a-coorting.”
3962: 
3963: “Ah, but mayhap Miss Nancy hung off, like, and your lass didn’t,” said
3964: Ben.
3965: 
3966: “I should say she didn’t,” said Mr. Macey, significantly. “Before I
3967: said “sniff”, I took care to know as she’d say “snaff”, and pretty
3968: quick too. I wasn’t a-going to open _my_ mouth, like a dog at a fly,
3969: and snap it to again, wi’ nothing to swaller.”
3970: 
3971: “Well, I think Miss Nancy’s a-coming round again,” said Ben, “for
3972: Master Godfrey doesn’t look so down-hearted to-night. And I see he’s
3973: for taking her away to sit down, now they’re at the end o’ the dance:
3974: that looks like sweethearting, that does.”
3975: 
3976: The reason why Godfrey and Nancy had left the dance was not so tender
3977: as Ben imagined. In the close press of couples a slight accident had
3978: happened to Nancy’s dress, which, while it was short enough to show her
3979: neat ankle in front, was long enough behind to be caught under the
3980: stately stamp of the Squire’s foot, so as to rend certain stitches at
3981: the waist, and cause much sisterly agitation in Priscilla’s mind, as
3982: well as serious concern in Nancy’s. One’s thoughts may be much occupied
3983: with love-struggles, but hardly so as to be insensible to a disorder in
3984: the general framework of things. Nancy had no sooner completed her duty
3985: in the figure they were dancing than she said to Godfrey, with a deep
3986: blush, that she must go and sit down till Priscilla could come to her;
3987: for the sisters had already exchanged a short whisper and an open-eyed
3988: glance full of meaning. No reason less urgent than this could have
3989: prevailed on Nancy to give Godfrey this opportunity of sitting apart
3990: with her. As for Godfrey, he was feeling so happy and oblivious under
3991: the long charm of the country-dance with Nancy, that he got rather bold
3992: on the strength of her confusion, and was capable of leading her
3993: straight away, without leave asked, into the adjoining small parlour,
3994: where the card-tables were set.
3995: 
3996: “Oh no, thank you,” said Nancy, coldly, as soon as she perceived where
3997: he was going, “not in there. I’ll wait here till Priscilla’s ready to
3998: come to me. I’m sorry to bring you out of the dance and make myself
3999: troublesome.”
4000: 
4001: “Why, you’ll be more comfortable here by yourself,” said the artful
4002: Godfrey: “I’ll leave you here till your sister can come.” He spoke in
4003: an indifferent tone.
4004: 
4005: That was an agreeable proposition, and just what Nancy desired; why,
4006: then, was she a little hurt that Mr. Godfrey should make it? They
4007: entered, and she seated herself on a chair against one of the
4008: card-tables, as the stiffest and most unapproachable position she could
4009: choose.
4010: 
4011: “Thank you, sir,” she said immediately. “I needn’t give you any more
4012: trouble. I’m sorry you’ve had such an unlucky partner.”
4013: 
4014: “That’s very ill-natured of you,” said Godfrey, standing by her without
4015: any sign of intended departure, “to be sorry you’ve danced with me.”
4016: 
4017: “Oh, no, sir, I don’t mean to say what’s ill-natured at all,” said
4018: Nancy, looking distractingly prim and pretty. “When gentlemen have so
4019: many pleasures, one dance can matter but very little.”
4020: 
4021: “You know that isn’t true. You know one dance with you matters more to
4022: me than all the other pleasures in the world.”
4023: 
4024: It was a long, long while since Godfrey had said anything so direct as
4025: that, and Nancy was startled. But her instinctive dignity and
4026: repugnance to any show of emotion made her sit perfectly still, and
4027: only throw a little more decision into her voice, as she said—
4028: 
4029: “No, indeed, Mr. Godfrey, that’s not known to me, and I have very good
4030: reasons for thinking different. But if it’s true, I don’t wish to hear
4031: it.”
4032: 
4033: “Would you never forgive me, then, Nancy—never think well of me, let
4034: what would happen—would you never think the present made amends for the
4035: past? Not if I turned a good fellow, and gave up everything you didn’t
4036: like?”
4037: 
4038: Godfrey was half conscious that this sudden opportunity of speaking to
4039: Nancy alone had driven him beside himself; but blind feeling had got
4040: the mastery of his tongue. Nancy really felt much agitated by the
4041: possibility Godfrey’s words suggested, but this very pressure of
4042: emotion that she was in danger of finding too strong for her roused all
4043: her power of self-command.
4044: 
4045: “I should be glad to see a good change in anybody, Mr. Godfrey,” she
4046: answered, with the slightest discernible difference of tone, “but it
4047: ’ud be better if no change was wanted.”
4048: 
4049: “You’re very hard-hearted, Nancy,” said Godfrey, pettishly. “You might
4050: encourage me to be a better fellow. I’m very miserable—but you’ve no
4051: feeling.”
4052: 
4053: “I think those have the least feeling that act wrong to begin with,”
4054: said Nancy, sending out a flash in spite of herself. Godfrey was
4055: delighted with that little flash, and would have liked to go on and
4056: make her quarrel with him; Nancy was so exasperatingly quiet and firm.
4057: But she was not indifferent to him _yet_, though—
4058: 
4059: The entrance of Priscilla, bustling forward and saying, “Dear heart
4060: alive, child, let us look at this gown,” cut off Godfrey’s hopes of a
4061: quarrel.
4062: 
4063: “I suppose I must go now,” he said to Priscilla.
4064: 
4065: “It’s no matter to me whether you go or stay,” said that frank lady,
4066: searching for something in her pocket, with a preoccupied brow.
4067: 
4068: “Do _you_ want me to go?” said Godfrey, looking at Nancy, who was now
4069: standing up by Priscilla’s order.
4070: 
4071: “As you like,” said Nancy, trying to recover all her former coldness,
4072: and looking down carefully at the hem of her gown.
4073: 
4074: “Then I like to stay,” said Godfrey, with a reckless determination to
4075: get as much of this joy as he could to-night, and think nothing of the
4076: morrow.
4077: 
4078: 
4079: 
4080: CHAPTER XII.
4081: 
4082: While Godfrey Cass was taking draughts of forgetfulness from the sweet
4083: presence of Nancy, willingly losing all sense of that hidden bond which
4084: at other moments galled and fretted him so as to mingle irritation with
4085: the very sunshine, Godfrey’s wife was walking with slow uncertain steps
4086: through the snow-covered Raveloe lanes, carrying her child in her arms.
4087: 
4088: This journey on New Year’s Eve was a premeditated act of vengeance
4089: which she had kept in her heart ever since Godfrey, in a fit of
4090: passion, had told her he would sooner die than acknowledge her as his
4091: wife. There would be a great party at the Red House on New Year’s Eve,
4092: she knew: her husband would be smiling and smiled upon, hiding _her_
4093: existence in the darkest corner of his heart. But she would mar his
4094: pleasure: she would go in her dingy rags, with her faded face, once as
4095: handsome as the best, with her little child that had its father’s hair
4096: and eyes, and disclose herself to the Squire as his eldest son’s wife.
4097: It is seldom that the miserable can help regarding their misery as a
4098: wrong inflicted by those who are less miserable. Molly knew that the
4099: cause of her dingy rags was not her husband’s neglect, but the demon
4100: Opium to whom she was enslaved, body and soul, except in the lingering
4101: mother’s tenderness that refused to give him her hungry child. She knew
4102: this well; and yet, in the moments of wretched unbenumbed
4103: consciousness, the sense of her want and degradation transformed itself
4104: continually into bitterness towards Godfrey. _He_ was well off; and if
4105: she had her rights she would be well off too. The belief that he
4106: repented his marriage, and suffered from it, only aggravated her
4107: vindictiveness. Just and self-reproving thoughts do not come to us too
4108: thickly, even in the purest air, and with the best lessons of heaven
4109: and earth; how should those white-winged delicate messengers make their
4110: way to Molly’s poisoned chamber, inhabited by no higher memories than
4111: those of a barmaid’s paradise of pink ribbons and gentlemen’s jokes?
4112: 
4113: She had set out at an early hour, but had lingered on the road,
4114: inclined by her indolence to believe that if she waited under a warm
4115: shed the snow would cease to fall. She had waited longer than she knew,
4116: and now that she found herself belated in the snow-hidden ruggedness of
4117: the long lanes, even the animation of a vindictive purpose could not
4118: keep her spirit from failing. It was seven o’clock, and by this time
4119: she was not very far from Raveloe, but she was not familiar enough with
4120: those monotonous lanes to know how near she was to her journey’s end.
4121: She needed comfort, and she knew but one comforter—the familiar demon
4122: in her bosom; but she hesitated a moment, after drawing out the black
4123: remnant, before she raised it to her lips. In that moment the mother’s
4124: love pleaded for painful consciousness rather than oblivion—pleaded to
4125: be left in aching weariness, rather than to have the encircling arms
4126: benumbed so that they could not feel the dear burden. In another moment
4127: Molly had flung something away, but it was not the black remnant—it was
4128: an empty phial. And she walked on again under the breaking cloud, from
4129: which there came now and then the light of a quickly veiled star, for a
4130: freezing wind had sprung up since the snowing had ceased. But she
4131: walked always more and more drowsily, and clutched more and more
4132: automatically the sleeping child at her bosom.
4133: 
4134: Slowly the demon was working his will, and cold and weariness were his
4135: helpers. Soon she felt nothing but a supreme immediate longing that
4136: curtained off all futurity—the longing to lie down and sleep. She had
4137: arrived at a spot where her footsteps were no longer checked by a
4138: hedgerow, and she had wandered vaguely, unable to distinguish any
4139: objects, notwithstanding the wide whiteness around her, and the growing
4140: starlight. She sank down against a straggling furze bush, an easy
4141: pillow enough; and the bed of snow, too, was soft. She did not feel
4142: that the bed was cold, and did not heed whether the child would wake
4143: and cry for her. But her arms had not yet relaxed their instinctive
4144: clutch; and the little one slumbered on as gently as if it had been
4145: rocked in a lace-trimmed cradle.
4146: 
4147: But the complete torpor came at last: the fingers lost their tension,
4148: the arms unbent; then the little head fell away from the bosom, and the
4149: blue eyes opened wide on the cold starlight. At first there was a
4150: little peevish cry of “mammy”, and an effort to regain the pillowing
4151: arm and bosom; but mammy’s ear was deaf, and the pillow seemed to be
4152: slipping away backward. Suddenly, as the child rolled downward on its
4153: mother’s knees, all wet with snow, its eyes were caught by a bright
4154: glancing light on the white ground, and, with the ready transition of
4155: infancy, it was immediately absorbed in watching the bright living
4156: thing running towards it, yet never arriving. That bright living thing
4157: must be caught; and in an instant the child had slipped on all-fours,
4158: and held out one little hand to catch the gleam. But the gleam would
4159: not be caught in that way, and now the head was held up to see where
4160: the cunning gleam came from. It came from a very bright place; and the
4161: little one, rising on its legs, toddled through the snow, the old grimy
4162: shawl in which it was wrapped trailing behind it, and the queer little
4163: bonnet dangling at its back—toddled on to the open door of Silas
4164: Marner’s cottage, and right up to the warm hearth, where there was a
4165: bright fire of logs and sticks, which had thoroughly warmed the old
4166: sack (Silas’s greatcoat) spread out on the bricks to dry. The little
4167: one, accustomed to be left to itself for long hours without notice from
4168: its mother, squatted down on the sack, and spread its tiny hands
4169: towards the blaze, in perfect contentment, gurgling and making many
4170: inarticulate communications to the cheerful fire, like a new-hatched
4171: gosling beginning to find itself comfortable. But presently the warmth
4172: had a lulling effect, and the little golden head sank down on the old
4173: sack, and the blue eyes were veiled by their delicate half-transparent
4174: lids.
4175: 
4176: But where was Silas Marner while this strange visitor had come to his
4177: hearth? He was in the cottage, but he did not see the child. During the
4178: last few weeks, since he had lost his money, he had contracted the
4179: habit of opening his door and looking out from time to time, as if he
4180: thought that his money might be somehow coming back to him, or that
4181: some trace, some news of it, might be mysteriously on the road, and be
4182: caught by the listening ear or the straining eye. It was chiefly at
4183: night, when he was not occupied in his loom, that he fell into this
4184: repetition of an act for which he could have assigned no definite
4185: purpose, and which can hardly be understood except by those who have
4186: undergone a bewildering separation from a supremely loved object. In
4187: the evening twilight, and later whenever the night was not dark, Silas
4188: looked out on that narrow prospect round the Stone-pits, listening and
4189: gazing, not with hope, but with mere yearning and unrest.
4190: 
4191: This morning he had been told by some of his neighbours that it was New
4192: Year’s Eve, and that he must sit up and hear the old year rung out and
4193: the new rung in, because that was good luck, and might bring his money
4194: back again. This was only a friendly Raveloe-way of jesting with the
4195: half-crazy oddities of a miser, but it had perhaps helped to throw
4196: Silas into a more than usually excited state. Since the on-coming of
4197: twilight he had opened his door again and again, though only to shut it
4198: immediately at seeing all distance veiled by the falling snow. But the
4199: last time he opened it the snow had ceased, and the clouds were parting
4200: here and there. He stood and listened, and gazed for a long while—there
4201: was really something on the road coming towards him then, but he caught
4202: no sign of it; and the stillness and the wide trackless snow seemed to
4203: narrow his solitude, and touched his yearning with the chill of
4204: despair. He went in again, and put his right hand on the latch of the
4205: door to close it—but he did not close it: he was arrested, as he had
4206: been already since his loss, by the invisible wand of catalepsy, and
4207: stood like a graven image, with wide but sightless eyes, holding open
4208: his door, powerless to resist either the good or the evil that might
4209: enter there.
4210: 
4211: When Marner’s sensibility returned, he continued the action which had
4212: been arrested, and closed his door, unaware of the chasm in his
4213: consciousness, unaware of any intermediate change, except that the
4214: light had grown dim, and that he was chilled and faint. He thought he
4215: had been too long standing at the door and looking out. Turning towards
4216: the hearth, where the two logs had fallen apart, and sent forth only a
4217: red uncertain glimmer, he seated himself on his fireside chair, and was
4218: stooping to push his logs together, when, to his blurred vision, it
4219: seemed as if there were gold on the floor in front of the hearth.
4220: Gold!—his own gold—brought back to him as mysteriously as it had been
4221: taken away! He felt his heart begin to beat violently, and for a few
4222: moments he was unable to stretch out his hand and grasp the restored
4223: treasure. The heap of gold seemed to glow and get larger beneath his
4224: agitated gaze. He leaned forward at last, and stretched forth his hand;
4225: but instead of the hard coin with the familiar resisting outline, his
4226: fingers encountered soft warm curls. In utter amazement, Silas fell on
4227: his knees and bent his head low to examine the marvel: it was a
4228: sleeping child—a round, fair thing, with soft yellow rings all over its
4229: head. Could this be his little sister come back to him in a dream—his
4230: little sister whom he had carried about in his arms for a year before
4231: she died, when he was a small boy without shoes or stockings? That was
4232: the first thought that darted across Silas’s blank wonderment. _Was_ it
4233: a dream? He rose to his feet again, pushed his logs together, and,
4234: throwing on some dried leaves and sticks, raised a flame; but the flame
4235: did not disperse the vision—it only lit up more distinctly the little
4236: round form of the child, and its shabby clothing. It was very much like
4237: his little sister. Silas sank into his chair powerless, under the
4238: double presence of an inexplicable surprise and a hurrying influx of
4239: memories. How and when had the child come in without his knowledge? He
4240: had never been beyond the door. But along with that question, and
4241: almost thrusting it away, there was a vision of the old home and the
4242: old streets leading to Lantern Yard—and within that vision another, of
4243: the thoughts which had been present with him in those far-off scenes.
4244: The thoughts were strange to him now, like old friendships impossible
4245: to revive; and yet he had a dreamy feeling that this child was somehow
4246: a message come to him from that far-off life: it stirred fibres that
4247: had never been moved in Raveloe—old quiverings of tenderness—old
4248: impressions of awe at the presentiment of some Power presiding over his
4249: life; for his imagination had not yet extricated itself from the sense
4250: of mystery in the child’s sudden presence, and had formed no
4251: conjectures of ordinary natural means by which the event could have
4252: been brought about.
4253: 
4254: But there was a cry on the hearth: the child had awaked, and Marner
4255: stooped to lift it on his knee. It clung round his neck, and burst
4256: louder and louder into that mingling of inarticulate cries with “mammy”
4257: by which little children express the bewilderment of waking. Silas
4258: pressed it to him, and almost unconsciously uttered sounds of hushing
4259: tenderness, while he bethought himself that some of his porridge, which
4260: had got cool by the dying fire, would do to feed the child with if it
4261: were only warmed up a little.
4262: 
4263: He had plenty to do through the next hour. The porridge, sweetened with
4264: some dry brown sugar from an old store which he had refrained from
4265: using for himself, stopped the cries of the little one, and made her
4266: lift her blue eyes with a wide quiet gaze at Silas, as he put the spoon
4267: into her mouth. Presently she slipped from his knee and began to toddle
4268: about, but with a pretty stagger that made Silas jump up and follow her
4269: lest she should fall against anything that would hurt her. But she only
4270: fell in a sitting posture on the ground, and began to pull at her
4271: boots, looking up at him with a crying face as if the boots hurt her.
4272: He took her on his knee again, but it was some time before it occurred
4273: to Silas’s dull bachelor mind that the wet boots were the grievance,
4274: pressing on her warm ankles. He got them off with difficulty, and baby
4275: was at once happily occupied with the primary mystery of her own toes,
4276: inviting Silas, with much chuckling, to consider the mystery too. But
4277: the wet boots had at last suggested to Silas that the child had been
4278: walking on the snow, and this roused him from his entire oblivion of
4279: any ordinary means by which it could have entered or been brought into
4280: his house. Under the prompting of this new idea, and without waiting to
4281: form conjectures, he raised the child in his arms, and went to the
4282: door. As soon as he had opened it, there was the cry of “mammy” again,
4283: which Silas had not heard since the child’s first hungry waking.
4284: Bending forward, he could just discern the marks made by the little
4285: feet on the virgin snow, and he followed their track to the furze
4286: bushes. “Mammy!” the little one cried again and again, stretching
4287: itself forward so as almost to escape from Silas’s arms, before he
4288: himself was aware that there was something more than the bush before
4289: him—that there was a human body, with the head sunk low in the furze,
4290: and half-covered with the shaken snow.
4291: 
4292: 
4293: 
4294: CHAPTER XIII.
4295: 
4296: It was after the early supper-time at the Red House, and the
4297: entertainment was in that stage when bashfulness itself had passed into
4298: easy jollity, when gentlemen, conscious of unusual accomplishments,
4299: could at length be prevailed on to dance a hornpipe, and when the
4300: Squire preferred talking loudly, scattering snuff, and patting his
4301: visitors’ backs, to sitting longer at the whist-table—a choice
4302: exasperating to uncle Kimble, who, being always volatile in sober
4303: business hours, became intense and bitter over cards and brandy,
4304: shuffled before his adversary’s deal with a glare of suspicion, and
4305: turned up a mean trump-card with an air of inexpressible disgust, as if
4306: in a world where such things could happen one might as well enter on a
4307: course of reckless profligacy. When the evening had advanced to this
4308: pitch of freedom and enjoyment, it was usual for the servants, the
4309: heavy duties of supper being well over, to get their share of amusement
4310: by coming to look on at the dancing; so that the back regions of the
4311: house were left in solitude.
4312: 
4313: There were two doors by which the White Parlour was entered from the
4314: hall, and they were both standing open for the sake of air; but the
4315: lower one was crowded with the servants and villagers, and only the
4316: upper doorway was left free. Bob Cass was figuring in a hornpipe, and
4317: his father, very proud of this lithe son, whom he repeatedly declared
4318: to be just like himself in his young days in a tone that implied this
4319: to be the very highest stamp of juvenile merit, was the centre of a
4320: group who had placed themselves opposite the performer, not far from
4321: the upper door. Godfrey was standing a little way off, not to admire
4322: his brother’s dancing, but to keep sight of Nancy, who was seated in
4323: the group, near her father. He stood aloof, because he wished to avoid
4324: suggesting himself as a subject for the Squire’s fatherly jokes in
4325: connection with matrimony and Miss Nancy Lammeter’s beauty, which were
4326: likely to become more and more explicit. But he had the prospect of
4327: dancing with her again when the hornpipe was concluded, and in the
4328: meanwhile it was very pleasant to get long glances at her quite
4329: unobserved.
4330: 
4331: But when Godfrey was lifting his eyes from one of those long glances,
4332: they encountered an object as startling to him at that moment as if it
4333: had been an apparition from the dead. It _was_ an apparition from that
4334: hidden life which lies, like a dark by-street, behind the goodly
4335: ornamented facade that meets the sunlight and the gaze of respectable
4336: admirers. It was his own child, carried in Silas Marner’s arms. That
4337: was his instantaneous impression, unaccompanied by doubt, though he had
4338: not seen the child for months past; and when the hope was rising that
4339: he might possibly be mistaken, Mr. Crackenthorp and Mr. Lammeter had
4340: already advanced to Silas, in astonishment at this strange advent.
4341: Godfrey joined them immediately, unable to rest without hearing every
4342: word—trying to control himself, but conscious that if any one noticed
4343: him, they must see that he was white-lipped and trembling.
4344: 
4345: But now all eyes at that end of the room were bent on Silas Marner; the
4346: Squire himself had risen, and asked angrily, “How’s this?—what’s
4347: this?—what do you do coming in here in this way?”
4348: 
4349: “I’m come for the doctor—I want the doctor,” Silas had said, in the
4350: first moment, to Mr. Crackenthorp.
4351: 
4352: “Why, what’s the matter, Marner?” said the rector. “The doctor’s here;
4353: but say quietly what you want him for.”
4354: 
4355: “It’s a woman,” said Silas, speaking low, and half-breathlessly, just
4356: as Godfrey came up. “She’s dead, I think—dead in the snow at the
4357: Stone-pits—not far from my door.”
4358: 
4359: Godfrey felt a great throb: there was one terror in his mind at that
4360: moment: it was, that the woman might _not_ be dead. That was an evil
4361: terror—an ugly inmate to have found a nestling-place in Godfrey’s
4362: kindly disposition; but no disposition is a security from evil wishes
4363: to a man whose happiness hangs on duplicity.
4364: 
4365: “Hush, hush!” said Mr. Crackenthorp. “Go out into the hall there. I’ll
4366: fetch the doctor to you. Found a woman in the snow—and thinks she’s
4367: dead,” he added, speaking low to the Squire. “Better say as little
4368: about it as possible: it will shock the ladies. Just tell them a poor
4369: woman is ill from cold and hunger. I’ll go and fetch Kimble.”
4370: 
4371: By this time, however, the ladies had pressed forward, curious to know
4372: what could have brought the solitary linen-weaver there under such
4373: strange circumstances, and interested in the pretty child, who, half
4374: alarmed and half attracted by the brightness and the numerous company,
4375: now frowned and hid her face, now lifted up her head again and looked
4376: round placably, until a touch or a coaxing word brought back the frown,
4377: and made her bury her face with new determination.
4378: 
4379: “What child is it?” said several ladies at once, and, among the rest,
4380: Nancy Lammeter, addressing Godfrey.
4381: 
4382: “I don’t know—some poor woman’s who has been found in the snow, I
4383: believe,” was the answer Godfrey wrung from himself with a terrible
4384: effort. (“After all, _am_ I certain?” he hastened to add, silently, in
4385: anticipation of his own conscience.)
4386: 
4387: “Why, you’d better leave the child here, then, Master Marner,” said
4388: good-natured Mrs. Kimble, hesitating, however, to take those dingy
4389: clothes into contact with her own ornamented satin bodice. “I’ll tell
4390: one o’ the girls to fetch it.”
4391: 
4392: “No—no—I can’t part with it, I can’t let it go,” said Silas, abruptly.
4393: “It’s come to me—I’ve a right to keep it.”
4394: 
4395: The proposition to take the child from him had come to Silas quite
4396: unexpectedly, and his speech, uttered under a strong sudden impulse,
4397: was almost like a revelation to himself: a minute before, he had no
4398: distinct intention about the child.
4399: 
4400: “Did you ever hear the like?” said Mrs. Kimble, in mild surprise, to
4401: her neighbour.
4402: 
4403: “Now, ladies, I must trouble you to stand aside,” said Mr. Kimble,
4404: coming from the card-room, in some bitterness at the interruption, but
4405: drilled by the long habit of his profession into obedience to
4406: unpleasant calls, even when he was hardly sober.
4407: 
4408: “It’s a nasty business turning out now, eh, Kimble?” said the Squire.
4409: “He might ha’ gone for your young fellow—the ’prentice, there—what’s
4410: his name?”
4411: 
4412: “Might? aye—what’s the use of talking about might?” growled uncle
4413: Kimble, hastening out with Marner, and followed by Mr. Crackenthorp and
4414: Godfrey. “Get me a pair of thick boots, Godfrey, will you? And stay,
4415: let somebody run to Winthrop’s and fetch Dolly—she’s the best woman to
4416: get. Ben was here himself before supper; is he gone?”
4417: 
4418: “Yes, sir, I met him,” said Marner; “but I couldn’t stop to tell him
4419: anything, only I said I was going for the doctor, and he said the
4420: doctor was at the Squire’s. And I made haste and ran, and there was
4421: nobody to be seen at the back o’ the house, and so I went in to where
4422: the company was.”
4423: 
4424: The child, no longer distracted by the bright light and the smiling
4425: women’s faces, began to cry and call for “mammy”, though always
4426: clinging to Marner, who had apparently won her thorough confidence.
4427: Godfrey had come back with the boots, and felt the cry as if some fibre
4428: were drawn tight within him.
4429: 
4430: “I’ll go,” he said, hastily, eager for some movement; “I’ll go and
4431: fetch the woman—Mrs. Winthrop.”
4432: 
4433: “Oh, pooh—send somebody else,” said uncle Kimble, hurrying away with
4434: Marner.
4435: 
4436: “You’ll let me know if I can be of any use, Kimble,” said Mr.
4437: Crackenthorp. But the doctor was out of hearing.
4438: 
4439: Godfrey, too, had disappeared: he was gone to snatch his hat and coat,
4440: having just reflection enough to remember that he must not look like a
4441: madman; but he rushed out of the house into the snow without heeding
4442: his thin shoes.
4443: 
4444: In a few minutes he was on his rapid way to the Stone-pits by the side
4445: of Dolly, who, though feeling that she was entirely in her place in
4446: encountering cold and snow on an errand of mercy, was much concerned at
4447: a young gentleman’s getting his feet wet under a like impulse.
4448: 
4449: “You’d a deal better go back, sir,” said Dolly, with respectful
4450: compassion. “You’ve no call to catch cold; and I’d ask you if you’d be
4451: so good as tell my husband to come, on your way back—he’s at the
4452: Rainbow, I doubt—if you found him anyway sober enough to be o’ use. Or
4453: else, there’s Mrs. Snell ’ud happen send the boy up to fetch and carry,
4454: for there may be things wanted from the doctor’s.”
4455: 
4456: “No, I’ll stay, now I’m once out—I’ll stay outside here,” said Godfrey,
4457: when they came opposite Marner’s cottage. “You can come and tell me if
4458: I can do anything.”
4459: 
4460: “Well, sir, you’re very good: you’ve a tender heart,” said Dolly, going
4461: to the door.
4462: 
4463: Godfrey was too painfully preoccupied to feel a twinge of self-reproach
4464: at this undeserved praise. He walked up and down, unconscious that he
4465: was plunging ankle-deep in snow, unconscious of everything but
4466: trembling suspense about what was going on in the cottage, and the
4467: effect of each alternative on his future lot. No, not quite unconscious
4468: of everything else. Deeper down, and half-smothered by passionate
4469: desire and dread, there was the sense that he ought not to be waiting
4470: on these alternatives; that he ought to accept the consequences of his
4471: deeds, own the miserable wife, and fulfil the claims of the helpless
4472: child. But he had not moral courage enough to contemplate that active
4473: renunciation of Nancy as possible for him: he had only conscience and
4474: heart enough to make him for ever uneasy under the weakness that
4475: forbade the renunciation. And at this moment his mind leaped away from
4476: all restraint toward the sudden prospect of deliverance from his long
4477: bondage.
4478: 
4479: “Is she dead?” said the voice that predominated over every other within
4480: him. “If she is, I may marry Nancy; and then I shall be a good fellow
4481: in future, and have no secrets, and the child—shall be taken care of
4482: somehow.” But across that vision came the other possibility—“She may
4483: live, and then it’s all up with me.”
4484: 
4485: Godfrey never knew how long it was before the door of the cottage
4486: opened and Mr. Kimble came out. He went forward to meet his uncle,
4487: prepared to suppress the agitation he must feel, whatever news he was
4488: to hear.
4489: 
4490: “I waited for you, as I’d come so far,” he said, speaking first.
4491: 
4492: “Pooh, it was nonsense for you to come out: why didn’t you send one of
4493: the men? There’s nothing to be done. She’s dead—has been dead for
4494: hours, I should say.”
4495: 
4496: “What sort of woman is she?” said Godfrey, feeling the blood rush to
4497: his face.
4498: 
4499: “A young woman, but emaciated, with long black hair. Some vagrant—quite
4500: in rags. She’s got a wedding-ring on, however. They must fetch her away
4501: to the workhouse to-morrow. Come, come along.”
4502: 
4503: “I want to look at her,” said Godfrey. “I think I saw such a woman
4504: yesterday. I’ll overtake you in a minute or two.”
4505: 
4506: Mr. Kimble went on, and Godfrey turned back to the cottage. He cast
4507: only one glance at the dead face on the pillow, which Dolly had
4508: smoothed with decent care; but he remembered that last look at his
4509: unhappy hated wife so well, that at the end of sixteen years every line
4510: in the worn face was present to him when he told the full story of this
4511: night.
4512: 
4513: He turned immediately towards the hearth, where Silas Marner sat
4514: lulling the child. She was perfectly quiet now, but not asleep—only
4515: soothed by sweet porridge and warmth into that wide-gazing calm which
4516: makes us older human beings, with our inward turmoil, feel a certain
4517: awe in the presence of a little child, such as we feel before some
4518: quiet majesty or beauty in the earth or sky—before a steady glowing
4519: planet, or a full-flowered eglantine, or the bending trees over a
4520: silent pathway. The wide-open blue eyes looked up at Godfrey’s without
4521: any uneasiness or sign of recognition: the child could make no visible
4522: audible claim on its father; and the father felt a strange mixture of
4523: feelings, a conflict of regret and joy, that the pulse of that little
4524: heart had no response for the half-jealous yearning in his own, when
4525: the blue eyes turned away from him slowly, and fixed themselves on the
4526: weaver’s queer face, which was bent low down to look at them, while the
4527: small hand began to pull Marner’s withered cheek with loving
4528: disfiguration.
4529: 
4530: “You’ll take the child to the parish to-morrow?” asked Godfrey,
4531: speaking as indifferently as he could.
4532: 
4533: “Who says so?” said Marner, sharply. “Will they make me take her?”
4534: 
4535: “Why, you wouldn’t like to keep her, should you—an old bachelor like
4536: you?”
4537: 
4538: “Till anybody shows they’ve a right to take her away from me,” said
4539: Marner. “The mother’s dead, and I reckon it’s got no father: it’s a
4540: lone thing—and I’m a lone thing. My money’s gone, I don’t know
4541: where—and this is come from I don’t know where. I know nothing—I’m
4542: partly mazed.”
4543: 
4544: “Poor little thing!” said Godfrey. “Let me give something towards
4545: finding it clothes.”
4546: 
4547: He had put his hand in his pocket and found half-a-guinea, and,
4548: thrusting it into Silas’s hand, he hurried out of the cottage to
4549: overtake Mr. Kimble.
4550: 
4551: “Ah, I see it’s not the same woman I saw,” he said, as he came up.
4552: “It’s a pretty little child: the old fellow seems to want to keep it;
4553: that’s strange for a miser like him. But I gave him a trifle to help
4554: him out: the parish isn’t likely to quarrel with him for the right to
4555: keep the child.”
4556: 
4557: “No; but I’ve seen the time when I might have quarrelled with him for
4558: it myself. It’s too late now, though. If the child ran into the fire,
4559: your aunt’s too fat to overtake it: she could only sit and grunt like
4560: an alarmed sow. But what a fool you are, Godfrey, to come out in your
4561: dancing shoes and stockings in this way—and you one of the beaux of the
4562: evening, and at your own house! What do you mean by such freaks, young
4563: fellow? Has Miss Nancy been cruel, and do you want to spite her by
4564: spoiling your pumps?”
4565: 
4566: “Oh, everything has been disagreeable to-night. I was tired to death of
4567: jigging and gallanting, and that bother about the hornpipes. And I’d
4568: got to dance with the other Miss Gunn,” said Godfrey, glad of the
4569: subterfuge his uncle had suggested to him.
4570: 
4571: The prevarication and white lies which a mind that keeps itself
4572: ambitiously pure is as uneasy under as a great artist under the false
4573: touches that no eye detects but his own, are worn as lightly as mere
4574: trimmings when once the actions have become a lie.
4575: 
4576: Godfrey reappeared in the White Parlour with dry feet, and, since the
4577: truth must be told, with a sense of relief and gladness that was too
4578: strong for painful thoughts to struggle with. For could he not venture
4579: now, whenever opportunity offered, to say the tenderest things to Nancy
4580: Lammeter—to promise her and himself that he would always be just what
4581: she would desire to see him? There was no danger that his dead wife
4582: would be recognized: those were not days of active inquiry and wide
4583: report; and as for the registry of their marriage, that was a long way
4584: off, buried in unturned pages, away from every one’s interest but his
4585: own. Dunsey might betray him if he came back; but Dunsey might be won
4586: to silence.
4587: 
4588: And when events turn out so much better for a man than he has had
4589: reason to dread, is it not a proof that his conduct has been less
4590: foolish and blameworthy than it might otherwise have appeared? When we
4591: are treated well, we naturally begin to think that we are not
4592: altogether unmeritorious, and that it is only just we should treat
4593: ourselves well, and not mar our own good fortune. Where, after all,
4594: would be the use of his confessing the past to Nancy Lammeter, and
4595: throwing away his happiness?—nay, hers? for he felt some confidence
4596: that she loved him. As for the child, he would see that it was cared
4597: for: he would never forsake it; he would do everything but own it.
4598: Perhaps it would be just as happy in life without being owned by its
4599: father, seeing that nobody could tell how things would turn out, and
4600: that—is there any other reason wanted?—well, then, that the father
4601: would be much happier without owning the child.
4602: 
4603: 
4604: 
4605: CHAPTER XIV.
4606: 
4607: There was a pauper’s burial that week in Raveloe, and up Kench Yard at
4608: Batherley it was known that the dark-haired woman with the fair child,
4609: who had lately come to lodge there, was gone away again. That was all
4610: the express note taken that Molly had disappeared from the eyes of men.
4611: But the unwept death which, to the general lot, seemed as trivial as
4612: the summer-shed leaf, was charged with the force of destiny to certain
4613: human lives that we know of, shaping their joys and sorrows even to the
4614: end.
4615: 
4616: Silas Marner’s determination to keep the “tramp’s child” was matter of
4617: hardly less surprise and iterated talk in the village than the robbery
4618: of his money. That softening of feeling towards him which dated from
4619: his misfortune, that merging of suspicion and dislike in a rather
4620: contemptuous pity for him as lone and crazy, was now accompanied with a
4621: more active sympathy, especially amongst the women. Notable mothers,
4622: who knew what it was to keep children “whole and sweet”; lazy mothers,
4623: who knew what it was to be interrupted in folding their arms and
4624: scratching their elbows by the mischievous propensities of children
4625: just firm on their legs, were equally interested in conjecturing how a
4626: lone man would manage with a two-year-old child on his hands, and were
4627: equally ready with their suggestions: the notable chiefly telling him
4628: what he had better do, and the lazy ones being emphatic in telling him
4629: what he would never be able to do.
4630: 
4631: Among the notable mothers, Dolly Winthrop was the one whose neighbourly
4632: offices were the most acceptable to Marner, for they were rendered
4633: without any show of bustling instruction. Silas had shown her the
4634: half-guinea given to him by Godfrey, and had asked her what he should
4635: do about getting some clothes for the child.
4636: 
4637: “Eh, Master Marner,” said Dolly, “there’s no call to buy, no more nor a
4638: pair o’ shoes; for I’ve got the little petticoats as Aaron wore five
4639: years ago, and it’s ill spending the money on them baby-clothes, for
4640: the child ’ull grow like grass i’ May, bless it—that it will.”
4641: 
4642: And the same day Dolly brought her bundle, and displayed to Marner, one
4643: by one, the tiny garments in their due order of succession, most of
4644: them patched and darned, but clean and neat as fresh-sprung herbs. This
4645: was the introduction to a great ceremony with soap and water, from
4646: which Baby came out in new beauty, and sat on Dolly’s knee, handling
4647: her toes and chuckling and patting her palms together with an air of
4648: having made several discoveries about herself, which she communicated
4649: by alternate sounds of “gug-gug-gug”, and “mammy”. The “mammy” was not
4650: a cry of need or uneasiness: Baby had been used to utter it without
4651: expecting either tender sound or touch to follow.
4652: 
4653: “Anybody ’ud think the angils in heaven couldn’t be prettier,” said
4654: Dolly, rubbing the golden curls and kissing them. “And to think of its
4655: being covered wi’ them dirty rags—and the poor mother—froze to death;
4656: but there’s Them as took care of it, and brought it to your door,
4657: Master Marner. The door was open, and it walked in over the snow, like
4658: as if it had been a little starved robin. Didn’t you say the door was
4659: open?”
4660: 
4661: “Yes,” said Silas, meditatively. “Yes—the door was open. The money’s
4662: gone I don’t know where, and this is come from I don’t know where.”
4663: 
4664: He had not mentioned to any one his unconsciousness of the child’s
4665: entrance, shrinking from questions which might lead to the fact he
4666: himself suspected—namely, that he had been in one of his trances.
4667: 
4668: “Ah,” said Dolly, with soothing gravity, “it’s like the night and the
4669: morning, and the sleeping and the waking, and the rain and the
4670: harvest—one goes and the other comes, and we know nothing how nor
4671: where. We may strive and scrat and fend, but it’s little we can do
4672: arter all—the big things come and go wi’ no striving o’ our’n—they do,
4673: that they do; and I think you’re in the right on it to keep the little
4674: un, Master Marner, seeing as it’s been sent to you, though there’s
4675: folks as thinks different. You’ll happen be a bit moithered with it
4676: while it’s so little; but I’ll come, and welcome, and see to it for
4677: you: I’ve a bit o’ time to spare most days, for when one gets up
4678: betimes i’ the morning, the clock seems to stan’ still tow’rt ten,
4679: afore it’s time to go about the victual. So, as I say, I’ll come and
4680: see to the child for you, and welcome.”
4681: 
4682: “Thank you... kindly,” said Silas, hesitating a little. “I’ll be glad
4683: if you’ll tell me things. But,” he added, uneasily, leaning forward to
4684: look at Baby with some jealousy, as she was resting her head backward
4685: against Dolly’s arm, and eyeing him contentedly from a distance—“But I
4686: want to do things for it myself, else it may get fond o’ somebody else,
4687: and not fond o’ me. I’ve been used to fending for myself in the house—I
4688: can learn, I can learn.”
4689: 
4690: “Eh, to be sure,” said Dolly, gently. “I’ve seen men as are wonderful
4691: handy wi’ children. The men are awk’ard and contrairy mostly, God help
4692: ’em—but when the drink’s out of ’em, they aren’t unsensible, though
4693: they’re bad for leeching and bandaging—so fiery and unpatient. You see
4694: this goes first, next the skin,” proceeded Dolly, taking up the little
4695: shirt, and putting it on.
4696: 
4697: “Yes,” said Marner, docilely, bringing his eyes very close, that they
4698: might be initiated in the mysteries; whereupon Baby seized his head
4699: with both her small arms, and put her lips against his face with
4700: purring noises.
4701: 
4702: “See there,” said Dolly, with a woman’s tender tact, “she’s fondest o’
4703: you. She wants to go o’ your lap, I’ll be bound. Go, then: take her,
4704: Master Marner; you can put the things on, and then you can say as
4705: you’ve done for her from the first of her coming to you.”
4706: 
4707: Marner took her on his lap, trembling with an emotion mysterious to
4708: himself, at something unknown dawning on his life. Thought and feeling
4709: were so confused within him, that if he had tried to give them
4710: utterance, he could only have said that the child was come instead of
4711: the gold—that the gold had turned into the child. He took the garments
4712: from Dolly, and put them on under her teaching; interrupted, of course,
4713: by Baby’s gymnastics.
4714: 
4715: “There, then! why, you take to it quite easy, Master Marner,” said
4716: Dolly; “but what shall you do when you’re forced to sit in your loom?
4717: For she’ll get busier and mischievouser every day—she will, bless her.
4718: It’s lucky as you’ve got that high hearth i’stead of a grate, for that
4719: keeps the fire more out of her reach: but if you’ve got anything as can
4720: be spilt or broke, or as is fit to cut her fingers off, she’ll be at
4721: it—and it is but right you should know.”
4722: 
4723: Silas meditated a little while in some perplexity. “I’ll tie her to the
4724: leg o’ the loom,” he said at last—“tie her with a good long strip o’
4725: something.”
4726: 
4727: “Well, mayhap that’ll do, as it’s a little gell, for they’re easier
4728: persuaded to sit i’ one place nor the lads. I know what the lads are;
4729: for I’ve had four—four I’ve had, God knows—and if you was to take and
4730: tie ’em up, they’d make a fighting and a crying as if you was ringing
4731: the pigs. But I’ll bring you my little chair, and some bits o’ red rag
4732: and things for her to play wi’; an’ she’ll sit and chatter to ’em as if
4733: they was alive. Eh, if it wasn’t a sin to the lads to wish ’em made
4734: different, bless ’em, I should ha’ been glad for one of ’em to be a
4735: little gell; and to think as I could ha’ taught her to scour, and mend,
4736: and the knitting, and everything. But I can teach ’em this little un,
4737: Master Marner, when she gets old enough.”
4738: 
4739: “But she’ll be _my_ little un,” said Marner, rather hastily. “She’ll be
4740: nobody else’s.”
4741: 
4742: “No, to be sure; you’ll have a right to her, if you’re a father to her,
4743: and bring her up according. But,” added Dolly, coming to a point which
4744: she had determined beforehand to touch upon, “you must bring her up
4745: like christened folks’s children, and take her to church, and let her
4746: learn her catechise, as my little Aaron can say off—the “I believe”,
4747: and everything, and “hurt nobody by word or deed”,—as well as if he was
4748: the clerk. That’s what you must do, Master Marner, if you’d do the
4749: right thing by the orphin child.”
4750: 
4751: Marner’s pale face flushed suddenly under a new anxiety. His mind was
4752: too busy trying to give some definite bearing to Dolly’s words for him
4753: to think of answering her.
4754: 
4755: “And it’s my belief,” she went on, “as the poor little creatur has
4756: never been christened, and it’s nothing but right as the parson should
4757: be spoke to; and if you was noways unwilling, I’d talk to Mr. Macey
4758: about it this very day. For if the child ever went anyways wrong, and
4759: you hadn’t done your part by it, Master Marner—’noculation, and
4760: everything to save it from harm—it ’ud be a thorn i’ your bed for ever
4761: o’ this side the grave; and I can’t think as it ’ud be easy lying down
4762: for anybody when they’d got to another world, if they hadn’t done their
4763: part by the helpless children as come wi’out their own asking.”
4764: 
4765: Dolly herself was disposed to be silent for some time now, for she had
4766: spoken from the depths of her own simple belief, and was much concerned
4767: to know whether her words would produce the desired effect on Silas. He
4768: was puzzled and anxious, for Dolly’s word “christened” conveyed no
4769: distinct meaning to him. He had only heard of baptism, and had only
4770: seen the baptism of grown-up men and women.
4771: 
4772: “What is it as you mean by “christened”?” he said at last, timidly.
4773: “Won’t folks be good to her without it?”
4774: 
4775: “Dear, dear! Master Marner,” said Dolly, with gentle distress and
4776: compassion. “Had you never no father nor mother as taught you to say
4777: your prayers, and as there’s good words and good things to keep us from
4778: harm?”
4779: 
4780: “Yes,” said Silas, in a low voice; “I know a deal about that—used to,
4781: used to. But your ways are different: my country was a good way off.”
4782: He paused a few moments, and then added, more decidedly, “But I want to
4783: do everything as can be done for the child. And whatever’s right for it
4784: i’ this country, and you think ’ull do it good, I’ll act according, if
4785: you’ll tell me.”
4786: 
4787: “Well, then, Master Marner,” said Dolly, inwardly rejoiced, “I’ll ask
4788: Mr. Macey to speak to the parson about it; and you must fix on a name
4789: for it, because it must have a name giv’ it when it’s christened.”
4790: 
4791: “My mother’s name was Hephzibah,” said Silas, “and my little sister was
4792: named after her.”
4793: 
4794: “Eh, that’s a hard name,” said Dolly. “I partly think it isn’t a
4795: christened name.”
4796: 
4797: “It’s a Bible name,” said Silas, old ideas recurring.
4798: 
4799: “Then I’ve no call to speak again’ it,” said Dolly, rather startled by
4800: Silas’s knowledge on this head; “but you see I’m no scholard, and I’m
4801: slow at catching the words. My husband says I’m allays like as if I was
4802: putting the haft for the handle—that’s what he says—for he’s very
4803: sharp, God help him. But it was awk’ard calling your little sister by
4804: such a hard name, when you’d got nothing big to say, like—wasn’t it,
4805: Master Marner?”
4806: 
4807: “We called her Eppie,” said Silas.
4808: 
4809: “Well, if it was noways wrong to shorten the name, it ’ud be a deal
4810: handier. And so I’ll go now, Master Marner, and I’ll speak about the
4811: christening afore dark; and I wish you the best o’ luck, and it’s my
4812: belief as it’ll come to you, if you do what’s right by the orphin
4813: child;—and there’s the ’noculation to be seen to; and as to washing its
4814: bits o’ things, you need look to nobody but me, for I can do ’em wi’
4815: one hand when I’ve got my suds about. Eh, the blessed angil! You’ll let
4816: me bring my Aaron one o’ these days, and he’ll show her his little cart
4817: as his father’s made for him, and the black-and-white pup as he’s got
4818: a-rearing.”
4819: 
4820: Baby _was_ christened, the rector deciding that a double baptism was
4821: the lesser risk to incur; and on this occasion Silas, making himself as
4822: clean and tidy as he could, appeared for the first time within the
4823: church, and shared in the observances held sacred by his neighbours. He
4824: was quite unable, by means of anything he heard or saw, to identify the
4825: Raveloe religion with his old faith; if he could at any time in his
4826: previous life have done so, it must have been by the aid of a strong
4827: feeling ready to vibrate with sympathy, rather than by a comparison of
4828: phrases and ideas: and now for long years that feeling had been
4829: dormant. He had no distinct idea about the baptism and the
4830: church-going, except that Dolly had said it was for the good of the
4831: child; and in this way, as the weeks grew to months, the child created
4832: fresh and fresh links between his life and the lives from which he had
4833: hitherto shrunk continually into narrower isolation. Unlike the gold
4834: which needed nothing, and must be worshipped in close-locked
4835: solitude—which was hidden away from the daylight, was deaf to the song
4836: of birds, and started to no human tones—Eppie was a creature of endless
4837: claims and ever-growing desires, seeking and loving sunshine, and
4838: living sounds, and living movements; making trial of everything, with
4839: trust in new joy, and stirring the human kindness in all eyes that
4840: looked on her. The gold had kept his thoughts in an ever-repeated
4841: circle, leading to nothing beyond itself; but Eppie was an object
4842: compacted of changes and hopes that forced his thoughts onward, and
4843: carried them far away from their old eager pacing towards the same
4844: blank limit—carried them away to the new things that would come with
4845: the coming years, when Eppie would have learned to understand how her
4846: father Silas cared for her; and made him look for images of that time
4847: in the ties and charities that bound together the families of his
4848: neighbours. The gold had asked that he should sit weaving longer and
4849: longer, deafened and blinded more and more to all things except the
4850: monotony of his loom and the repetition of his web; but Eppie called
4851: him away from his weaving, and made him think all its pauses a holiday,
4852: reawakening his senses with her fresh life, even to the old
4853: winter-flies that came crawling forth in the early spring sunshine, and
4854: warming him into joy because _she_ had joy.
4855: 
4856: And when the sunshine grew strong and lasting, so that the buttercups
4857: were thick in the meadows, Silas might be seen in the sunny midday, or
4858: in the late afternoon when the shadows were lengthening under the
4859: hedgerows, strolling out with uncovered head to carry Eppie beyond the
4860: Stone-pits to where the flowers grew, till they reached some favourite
4861: bank where he could sit down, while Eppie toddled to pluck the flowers,
4862: and make remarks to the winged things that murmured happily above the
4863: bright petals, calling “Dad-dad’s” attention continually by bringing
4864: him the flowers. Then she would turn her ear to some sudden bird-note,
4865: and Silas learned to please her by making signs of hushed stillness,
4866: that they might listen for the note to come again: so that when it
4867: came, she set up her small back and laughed with gurgling triumph.
4868: Sitting on the banks in this way, Silas began to look for the once
4869: familiar herbs again; and as the leaves, with their unchanged outline
4870: and markings, lay on his palm, there was a sense of crowding
4871: remembrances from which he turned away timidly, taking refuge in
4872: Eppie’s little world, that lay lightly on his enfeebled spirit.
4873: 
4874: As the child’s mind was growing into knowledge, his mind was growing
4875: into memory: as her life unfolded, his soul, long stupefied in a cold
4876: narrow prison, was unfolding too, and trembling gradually into full
4877: consciousness.
4878: 
4879: It was an influence which must gather force with every new year: the
4880: tones that stirred Silas’s heart grew articulate, and called for more
4881: distinct answers; shapes and sounds grew clearer for Eppie’s eyes and
4882: ears, and there was more that “Dad-dad” was imperatively required to
4883: notice and account for. Also, by the time Eppie was three years old,
4884: she developed a fine capacity for mischief, and for devising ingenious
4885: ways of being troublesome, which found much exercise, not only for
4886: Silas’s patience, but for his watchfulness and penetration. Sorely was
4887: poor Silas puzzled on such occasions by the incompatible demands of
4888: love. Dolly Winthrop told him that punishment was good for Eppie, and
4889: that, as for rearing a child without making it tingle a little in soft
4890: and safe places now and then, it was not to be done.
4891: 
4892: “To be sure, there’s another thing you might do, Master Marner,” added
4893: Dolly, meditatively: “you might shut her up once i’ the coal-hole. That
4894: was what I did wi’ Aaron; for I was that silly wi’ the youngest lad, as
4895: I could never bear to smack him. Not as I could find i’ my heart to let
4896: him stay i’ the coal-hole more nor a minute, but it was enough to colly
4897: him all over, so as he must be new washed and dressed, and it was as
4898: good as a rod to him—that was. But I put it upo’ your conscience,
4899: Master Marner, as there’s one of ’em you must choose—ayther smacking or
4900: the coal-hole—else she’ll get so masterful, there’ll be no holding
4901: her.”
4902: 
4903: Silas was impressed with the melancholy truth of this last remark; but
4904: his force of mind failed before the only two penal methods open to him,
4905: not only because it was painful to him to hurt Eppie, but because he
4906: trembled at a moment’s contention with her, lest she should love him
4907: the less for it. Let even an affectionate Goliath get himself tied to a
4908: small tender thing, dreading to hurt it by pulling, and dreading still
4909: more to snap the cord, and which of the two, pray, will be master? It
4910: was clear that Eppie, with her short toddling steps, must lead father
4911: Silas a pretty dance on any fine morning when circumstances favoured
4912: mischief.
4913: 
4914: For example. He had wisely chosen a broad strip of linen as a means of
4915: fastening her to his loom when he was busy: it made a broad belt round
4916: her waist, and was long enough to allow of her reaching the truckle-bed
4917: and sitting down on it, but not long enough for her to attempt any
4918: dangerous climbing. One bright summer’s morning Silas had been more
4919: engrossed than usual in “setting up” a new piece of work, an occasion
4920: on which his scissors were in requisition. These scissors, owing to an
4921: especial warning of Dolly’s, had been kept carefully out of Eppie’s
4922: reach; but the click of them had had a peculiar attraction for her ear,
4923: and watching the results of that click, she had derived the philosophic
4924: lesson that the same cause would produce the same effect. Silas had
4925: seated himself in his loom, and the noise of weaving had begun; but he
4926: had left his scissors on a ledge which Eppie’s arm was long enough to
4927: reach; and now, like a small mouse, watching her opportunity, she stole
4928: quietly from her corner, secured the scissors, and toddled to the bed
4929: again, setting up her back as a mode of concealing the fact. She had a
4930: distinct intention as to the use of the scissors; and having cut the
4931: linen strip in a jagged but effectual manner, in two moments she had
4932: run out at the open door where the sunshine was inviting her, while
4933: poor Silas believed her to be a better child than usual. It was not
4934: until he happened to need his scissors that the terrible fact burst
4935: upon him: Eppie had run out by herself—had perhaps fallen into the
4936: Stone-pit. Silas, shaken by the worst fear that could have befallen
4937: him, rushed out, calling “Eppie!” and ran eagerly about the unenclosed
4938: space, exploring the dry cavities into which she might have fallen, and
4939: then gazing with questioning dread at the smooth red surface of the
4940: water. The cold drops stood on his brow. How long had she been out?
4941: There was one hope—that she had crept through the stile and got into
4942: the fields, where he habitually took her to stroll. But the grass was
4943: high in the meadow, and there was no descrying her, if she were there,
4944: except by a close search that would be a trespass on Mr. Osgood’s crop.
4945: Still, that misdemeanour must be committed; and poor Silas, after
4946: peering all round the hedgerows, traversed the grass, beginning with
4947: perturbed vision to see Eppie behind every group of red sorrel, and to
4948: see her moving always farther off as he approached. The meadow was
4949: searched in vain; and he got over the stile into the next field,
4950: looking with dying hope towards a small pond which was now reduced to
4951: its summer shallowness, so as to leave a wide margin of good adhesive
4952: mud. Here, however, sat Eppie, discoursing cheerfully to her own small
4953: boot, which she was using as a bucket to convey the water into a deep
4954: hoof-mark, while her little naked foot was planted comfortably on a
4955: cushion of olive-green mud. A red-headed calf was observing her with
4956: alarmed doubt through the opposite hedge.
4957: 
4958: Here was clearly a case of aberration in a christened child which
4959: demanded severe treatment; but Silas, overcome with convulsive joy at
4960: finding his treasure again, could do nothing but snatch her up, and
4961: cover her with half-sobbing kisses. It was not until he had carried her
4962: home, and had begun to think of the necessary washing, that he
4963: recollected the need that he should punish Eppie, and “make her
4964: remember”. The idea that she might run away again and come to harm,
4965: gave him unusual resolution, and for the first time he determined to
4966: try the coal-hole—a small closet near the hearth.
4967: 
4968: “Naughty, naughty Eppie,” he suddenly began, holding her on his knee,
4969: and pointing to her muddy feet and clothes—“naughty to cut with the
4970: scissors and run away. Eppie must go into the coal-hole for being
4971: naughty. Daddy must put her in the coal-hole.”
4972: 
4973: He half-expected that this would be shock enough, and that Eppie would
4974: begin to cry. But instead of that, she began to shake herself on his
4975: knee, as if the proposition opened a pleasing novelty. Seeing that he
4976: must proceed to extremities, he put her into the coal-hole, and held
4977: the door closed, with a trembling sense that he was using a strong
4978: measure. For a moment there was silence, but then came a little cry,
4979: “Opy, opy!” and Silas let her out again, saying, “Now Eppie ’ull never
4980: be naughty again, else she must go in the coal-hole—a black naughty
4981: place.”
4982: 
4983: The weaving must stand still a long while this morning, for now Eppie
4984: must be washed, and have clean clothes on; but it was to be hoped that
4985: this punishment would have a lasting effect, and save time in
4986: future—though, perhaps, it would have been better if Eppie had cried
4987: more.
4988: 
4989: In half an hour she was clean again, and Silas having turned his back
4990: to see what he could do with the linen band, threw it down again, with
4991: the reflection that Eppie would be good without fastening for the rest
4992: of the morning. He turned round again, and was going to place her in
4993: her little chair near the loom, when she peeped out at him with black
4994: face and hands again, and said, “Eppie in de toal-hole!”
4995: 
4996: This total failure of the coal-hole discipline shook Silas’s belief in
4997: the efficacy of punishment. “She’d take it all for fun,” he observed to
4998: Dolly, “if I didn’t hurt her, and that I can’t do, Mrs. Winthrop. If
4999: she makes me a bit o’ trouble, I can bear it. And she’s got no tricks
5000: but what she’ll grow out of.”
5001: 
5002: “Well, that’s partly true, Master Marner,” said Dolly, sympathetically;
5003: “and if you can’t bring your mind to frighten her off touching things,
5004: you must do what you can to keep ’em out of her way. That’s what I do
5005: wi’ the pups as the lads are allays a-rearing. They _will_ worry and
5006: gnaw—worry and gnaw they will, if it was one’s Sunday cap as hung
5007: anywhere so as they could drag it. They know no difference, God help
5008: ’em: it’s the pushing o’ the teeth as sets ’em on, that’s what it is.”
5009: 
5010: So Eppie was reared without punishment, the burden of her misdeeds
5011: being borne vicariously by father Silas. The stone hut was made a soft
5012: nest for her, lined with downy patience: and also in the world that lay
5013: beyond the stone hut she knew nothing of frowns and denials.
5014: 
5015: Notwithstanding the difficulty of carrying her and his yarn or linen at
5016: the same time, Silas took her with him in most of his journeys to the
5017: farmhouses, unwilling to leave her behind at Dolly Winthrop’s, who was
5018: always ready to take care of her; and little curly-headed Eppie, the
5019: weaver’s child, became an object of interest at several outlying
5020: homesteads, as well as in the village. Hitherto he had been treated
5021: very much as if he had been a useful gnome or brownie—a queer and
5022: unaccountable creature, who must necessarily be looked at with
5023: wondering curiosity and repulsion, and with whom one would be glad to
5024: make all greetings and bargains as brief as possible, but who must be
5025: dealt with in a propitiatory way, and occasionally have a present of
5026: pork or garden stuff to carry home with him, seeing that without him
5027: there was no getting the yarn woven. But now Silas met with open
5028: smiling faces and cheerful questioning, as a person whose satisfactions
5029: and difficulties could be understood. Everywhere he must sit a little
5030: and talk about the child, and words of interest were always ready for
5031: him: “Ah, Master Marner, you’ll be lucky if she takes the measles soon
5032: and easy!”—or, “Why, there isn’t many lone men ’ud ha’ been wishing to
5033: take up with a little un like that: but I reckon the weaving makes you
5034: handier than men as do out-door work—you’re partly as handy as a woman,
5035: for weaving comes next to spinning.” Elderly masters and mistresses,
5036: seated observantly in large kitchen arm-chairs, shook their heads over
5037: the difficulties attendant on rearing children, felt Eppie’s round arms
5038: and legs, and pronounced them remarkably firm, and told Silas that, if
5039: she turned out well (which, however, there was no telling), it would be
5040: a fine thing for him to have a steady lass to do for him when he got
5041: helpless. Servant maidens were fond of carrying her out to look at the
5042: hens and chickens, or to see if any cherries could be shaken down in
5043: the orchard; and the small boys and girls approached her slowly, with
5044: cautious movement and steady gaze, like little dogs face to face with
5045: one of their own kind, till attraction had reached the point at which
5046: the soft lips were put out for a kiss. No child was afraid of
5047: approaching Silas when Eppie was near him: there was no repulsion
5048: around him now, either for young or old; for the little child had come
5049: to link him once more with the whole world. There was love between him
5050: and the child that blent them into one, and there was love between the
5051: child and the world—from men and women with parental looks and tones,
5052: to the red lady-birds and the round pebbles.
5053: 
5054: Silas began now to think of Raveloe life entirely in relation to Eppie:
5055: she must have everything that was a good in Raveloe; and he listened
5056: docilely, that he might come to understand better what this life was,
5057: from which, for fifteen years, he had stood aloof as from a strange
5058: thing, with which he could have no communion: as some man who has a
5059: precious plant to which he would give a nurturing home in a new soil,
5060: thinks of the rain, and the sunshine, and all influences, in relation
5061: to his nursling, and asks industriously for all knowledge that will
5062: help him to satisfy the wants of the searching roots, or to guard leaf
5063: and bud from invading harm. The disposition to hoard had been utterly
5064: crushed at the very first by the loss of his long-stored gold: the
5065: coins he earned afterwards seemed as irrelevant as stones brought to
5066: complete a house suddenly buried by an earthquake; the sense of
5067: bereavement was too heavy upon him for the old thrill of satisfaction
5068: to arise again at the touch of the newly-earned coin. And now something
5069: had come to replace his hoard which gave a growing purpose to the
5070: earnings, drawing his hope and joy continually onward beyond the money.
5071: 
5072: In old days there were angels who came and took men by the hand and led
5073: them away from the city of destruction. We see no white-winged angels
5074: now. But yet men are led away from threatening destruction: a hand is
5075: put into theirs, which leads them forth gently towards a calm and
5076: bright land, so that they look no more backward; and the hand may be a
5077: little child’s.
5078: 
5079: 
5080: 
5081: CHAPTER XV.
5082: 
5083: There was one person, as you will believe, who watched with keener
