5084: though more hidden interest than any other, the prosperous growth of
5085: Eppie under the weaver’s care. He dared not do anything that would
5086: imply a stronger interest in a poor man’s adopted child than could be
5087: expected from the kindliness of the young Squire, when a chance meeting
5088: suggested a little present to a simple old fellow whom others noticed
5089: with goodwill; but he told himself that the time would come when he
5090: might do something towards furthering the welfare of his daughter
5091: without incurring suspicion. Was he very uneasy in the meantime at his
5092: inability to give his daughter her birthright? I cannot say that he
5093: was. The child was being taken care of, and would very likely be happy,
5094: as people in humble stations often were—happier, perhaps, than those
5095: brought up in luxury.
5096: 
5097: That famous ring that pricked its owner when he forgot duty and
5098: followed desire—I wonder if it pricked very hard when he set out on the
5099: chase, or whether it pricked but lightly then, and only pierced to the
5100: quick when the chase had long been ended, and hope, folding her wings,
5101: looked backward and became regret?
5102: 
5103: Godfrey Cass’s cheek and eye were brighter than ever now. He was so
5104: undivided in his aims, that he seemed like a man of firmness. No Dunsey
5105: had come back: people had made up their minds that he was gone for a
5106: soldier, or gone “out of the country”, and no one cared to be specific
5107: in their inquiries on a subject delicate to a respectable family.
5108: Godfrey had ceased to see the shadow of Dunsey across his path; and the
5109: path now lay straight forward to the accomplishment of his best,
5110: longest-cherished wishes. Everybody said Mr. Godfrey had taken the
5111: right turn; and it was pretty clear what would be the end of things,
5112: for there were not many days in the week that he was not seen riding to
5113: the Warrens. Godfrey himself, when he was asked jocosely if the day had
5114: been fixed, smiled with the pleasant consciousness of a lover who could
5115: say “yes”, if he liked. He felt a reformed man, delivered from
5116: temptation; and the vision of his future life seemed to him as a
5117: promised land for which he had no cause to fight. He saw himself with
5118: all his happiness centred on his own hearth, while Nancy would smile on
5119: him as he played with the children.
5120: 
5121: And that other child—not on the hearth—he would not forget it; he would
5122: see that it was well provided for. That was a father’s duty.
5123: 
5124: 
5125: 
5126: PART II.
5127: 
5128: 
5129: 
5130: CHAPTER XVI.
5131: 
5132: It was a bright autumn Sunday, sixteen years after Silas Marner had
5133: found his new treasure on the hearth. The bells of the old Raveloe
5134: church were ringing the cheerful peal which told that the morning
5135: service was ended; and out of the arched doorway in the tower came
5136: slowly, retarded by friendly greetings and questions, the richer
5137: parishioners who had chosen this bright Sunday morning as eligible for
5138: church-going. It was the rural fashion of that time for the more
5139: important members of the congregation to depart first, while their
5140: humbler neighbours waited and looked on, stroking their bent heads or
5141: dropping their curtsies to any large ratepayer who turned to notice
5142: them.
5143: 
5144: Foremost among these advancing groups of well-clad people, there are
5145: some whom we shall recognize, in spite of Time, who has laid his hand
5146: on them all. The tall blond man of forty is not much changed in feature
5147: from the Godfrey Cass of six-and-twenty: he is only fuller in flesh,
5148: and has only lost the indefinable look of youth—a loss which is marked
5149: even when the eye is undulled and the wrinkles are not yet come.
5150: Perhaps the pretty woman, not much younger than he, who is leaning on
5151: his arm, is more changed than her husband: the lovely bloom that used
5152: to be always on her cheek now comes but fitfully, with the fresh
5153: morning air or with some strong surprise; yet to all who love human
5154: faces best for what they tell of human experience, Nancy’s beauty has a
5155: heightened interest. Often the soul is ripened into fuller goodness
5156: while age has spread an ugly film, so that mere glances can never
5157: divine the preciousness of the fruit. But the years have not been so
5158: cruel to Nancy. The firm yet placid mouth, the clear veracious glance
5159: of the brown eyes, speak now of a nature that has been tested and has
5160: kept its highest qualities; and even the costume, with its dainty
5161: neatness and purity, has more significance now the coquetries of youth
5162: can have nothing to do with it.
5163: 
5164: Mr. and Mrs. Godfrey Cass (any higher title has died away from Raveloe
5165: lips since the old Squire was gathered to his fathers and his
5166: inheritance was divided) have turned round to look for the tall aged
5167: man and the plainly dressed woman who are a little behind—Nancy having
5168: observed that they must wait for “father and Priscilla”—and now they
5169: all turn into a narrower path leading across the churchyard to a small
5170: gate opposite the Red House. We will not follow them now; for may there
5171: not be some others in this departing congregation whom we should like
5172: to see again—some of those who are not likely to be handsomely clad,
5173: and whom we may not recognize so easily as the master and mistress of
5174: the Red House?
5175: 
5176: But it is impossible to mistake Silas Marner. His large brown eyes seem
5177: to have gathered a longer vision, as is the way with eyes that have
5178: been short-sighted in early life, and they have a less vague, a more
5179: answering gaze; but in everything else one sees signs of a frame much
5180: enfeebled by the lapse of the sixteen years. The weaver’s bent
5181: shoulders and white hair give him almost the look of advanced age,
5182: though he is not more than five-and-fifty; but there is the freshest
5183: blossom of youth close by his side—a blonde dimpled girl of eighteen,
5184: who has vainly tried to chastise her curly auburn hair into smoothness
5185: under her brown bonnet: the hair ripples as obstinately as a brooklet
5186: under the March breeze, and the little ringlets burst away from the
5187: restraining comb behind and show themselves below the bonnet-crown.
5188: Eppie cannot help being rather vexed about her hair, for there is no
5189: other girl in Raveloe who has hair at all like it, and she thinks hair
5190: ought to be smooth. She does not like to be blameworthy even in small
5191: things: you see how neatly her prayer-book is folded in her spotted
5192: handkerchief.
5193: 
5194: That good-looking young fellow, in a new fustian suit, who walks behind
5195: her, is not quite sure upon the question of hair in the abstract, when
5196: Eppie puts it to him, and thinks that perhaps straight hair is the best
5197: in general, but he doesn’t want Eppie’s hair to be different. She
5198: surely divines that there is some one behind her who is thinking about
5199: her very particularly, and mustering courage to come to her side as
5200: soon as they are out in the lane, else why should she look rather shy,
5201: and take care not to turn away her head from her father Silas, to whom
5202: she keeps murmuring little sentences as to who was at church and who
5203: was not at church, and how pretty the red mountain-ash is over the
5204: Rectory wall?
5205: 
5206: “I wish _we_ had a little garden, father, with double daisies in, like
5207: Mrs. Winthrop’s,” said Eppie, when they were out in the lane; “only
5208: they say it ’ud take a deal of digging and bringing fresh soil—and you
5209: couldn’t do that, could you, father? Anyhow, I shouldn’t like you to do
5210: it, for it ’ud be too hard work for you.”
5211: 
5212: “Yes, I could do it, child, if you want a bit o’ garden: these long
5213: evenings, I could work at taking in a little bit o’ the waste, just
5214: enough for a root or two o’ flowers for you; and again, i’ the morning,
5215: I could have a turn wi’ the spade before I sat down to the loom. Why
5216: didn’t you tell me before as you wanted a bit o’ garden?”
5217: 
5218: “_I_ can dig it for you, Master Marner,” said the young man in fustian,
5219: who was now by Eppie’s side, entering into the conversation without the
5220: trouble of formalities. “It’ll be play to me after I’ve done my day’s
5221: work, or any odd bits o’ time when the work’s slack. And I’ll bring you
5222: some soil from Mr. Cass’s garden—he’ll let me, and willing.”
5223: 
5224: “Eh, Aaron, my lad, are you there?” said Silas; “I wasn’t aware of you;
5225: for when Eppie’s talking o’ things, I see nothing but what she’s
5226: a-saying. Well, if you could help me with the digging, we might get her
5227: a bit o’ garden all the sooner.”
5228: 
5229: “Then, if you think well and good,” said Aaron, “I’ll come to the
5230: Stone-pits this afternoon, and we’ll settle what land’s to be taken in,
5231: and I’ll get up an hour earlier i’ the morning, and begin on it.”
5232: 
5233: “But not if you don’t promise me not to work at the hard digging,
5234: father,” said Eppie. “For I shouldn’t ha’ said anything about it,” she
5235: added, half-bashfully, half-roguishly, “only Mrs. Winthrop said as
5236: Aaron ’ud be so good, and—”
5237: 
5238: “And you might ha’ known it without mother telling you,” said Aaron.
5239: “And Master Marner knows too, I hope, as I’m able and willing to do a
5240: turn o’ work for him, and he won’t do me the unkindness to anyways take
5241: it out o’ my hands.”
5242: 
5243: “There, now, father, you won’t work in it till it’s all easy,” said
5244: Eppie, “and you and me can mark out the beds, and make holes and plant
5245: the roots. It’ll be a deal livelier at the Stone-pits when we’ve got
5246: some flowers, for I always think the flowers can see us and know what
5247: we’re talking about. And I’ll have a bit o’ rosemary, and bergamot, and
5248: thyme, because they’re so sweet-smelling; but there’s no lavender only
5249: in the gentlefolks’ gardens, I think.”
5250: 
5251: “That’s no reason why you shouldn’t have some,” said Aaron, “for I can
5252: bring you slips of anything; I’m forced to cut no end of ’em when I’m
5253: gardening, and throw ’em away mostly. There’s a big bed o’ lavender at
5254: the Red House: the missis is very fond of it.”
5255: 
5256: “Well,” said Silas, gravely, “so as you don’t make free for us, or ask
5257: for anything as is worth much at the Red House: for Mr. Cass’s been so
5258: good to us, and built us up the new end o’ the cottage, and given us
5259: beds and things, as I couldn’t abide to be imposin’ for garden-stuff or
5260: anything else.”
5261: 
5262: “No, no, there’s no imposin’,” said Aaron; “there’s never a garden in
5263: all the parish but what there’s endless waste in it for want o’
5264: somebody as could use everything up. It’s what I think to myself
5265: sometimes, as there need nobody run short o’ victuals if the land was
5266: made the most on, and there was never a morsel but what could find its
5267: way to a mouth. It sets one thinking o’ that—gardening does. But I must
5268: go back now, else mother ’ull be in trouble as I aren’t there.”
5269: 
5270: “Bring her with you this afternoon, Aaron,” said Eppie; “I shouldn’t
5271: like to fix about the garden, and her not know everything from the
5272: first—should _you_, father?”
5273: 
5274: “Aye, bring her if you can, Aaron,” said Silas; “she’s sure to have a
5275: word to say as’ll help us to set things on their right end.”
5276: 
5277: Aaron turned back up the village, while Silas and Eppie went on up the
5278: lonely sheltered lane.
5279: 
5280: “O daddy!” she began, when they were in privacy, clasping and squeezing
5281: Silas’s arm, and skipping round to give him an energetic kiss. “My
5282: little old daddy! I’m so glad. I don’t think I shall want anything else
5283: when we’ve got a little garden; and I knew Aaron would dig it for us,”
5284: she went on with roguish triumph—“I knew that very well.”
5285: 
5286: “You’re a deep little puss, you are,” said Silas, with the mild passive
5287: happiness of love-crowned age in his face; “but you’ll make yourself
5288: fine and beholden to Aaron.”
5289: 
5290: “Oh, no, I shan’t,” said Eppie, laughing and frisking; “he likes it.”
5291: 
5292: “Come, come, let me carry your prayer-book, else you’ll be dropping it,
5293: jumping i’ that way.”
5294: 
5295: Eppie was now aware that her behaviour was under observation, but it
5296: was only the observation of a friendly donkey, browsing with a log
5297: fastened to his foot—a meek donkey, not scornfully critical of human
5298: trivialities, but thankful to share in them, if possible, by getting
5299: his nose scratched; and Eppie did not fail to gratify him with her
5300: usual notice, though it was attended with the inconvenience of his
5301: following them, painfully, up to the very door of their home.
5302: 
5303: But the sound of a sharp bark inside, as Eppie put the key in the door,
5304: modified the donkey’s views, and he limped away again without bidding.
5305: The sharp bark was the sign of an excited welcome that was awaiting
5306: them from a knowing brown terrier, who, after dancing at their legs in
5307: a hysterical manner, rushed with a worrying noise at a tortoise-shell
5308: kitten under the loom, and then rushed back with a sharp bark again, as
5309: much as to say, “I have done my duty by this feeble creature, you
5310: perceive”; while the lady-mother of the kitten sat sunning her white
5311: bosom in the window, and looked round with a sleepy air of expecting
5312: caresses, though she was not going to take any trouble for them.
5313: 
5314: The presence of this happy animal life was not the only change which
5315: had come over the interior of the stone cottage. There was no bed now
5316: in the living-room, and the small space was well filled with decent
5317: furniture, all bright and clean enough to satisfy Dolly Winthrop’s eye.
5318: The oaken table and three-cornered oaken chair were hardly what was
5319: likely to be seen in so poor a cottage: they had come, with the beds
5320: and other things, from the Red House; for Mr. Godfrey Cass, as every
5321: one said in the village, did very kindly by the weaver; and it was
5322: nothing but right a man should be looked on and helped by those who
5323: could afford it, when he had brought up an orphan child, and been
5324: father and mother to her—and had lost his money too, so as he had
5325: nothing but what he worked for week by week, and when the weaving was
5326: going down too—for there was less and less flax spun—and Master Marner
5327: was none so young. Nobody was jealous of the weaver, for he was
5328: regarded as an exceptional person, whose claims on neighbourly help
5329: were not to be matched in Raveloe. Any superstition that remained
5330: concerning him had taken an entirely new colour; and Mr. Macey, now a
5331: very feeble old man of fourscore and six, never seen except in his
5332: chimney-corner or sitting in the sunshine at his door-sill, was of
5333: opinion that when a man had done what Silas had done by an orphan
5334: child, it was a sign that his money would come to light again, or
5335: leastwise that the robber would be made to answer for it—for, as Mr.
5336: Macey observed of himself, his faculties were as strong as ever.
5337: 
5338: Silas sat down now and watched Eppie with a satisfied gaze as she
5339: spread the clean cloth, and set on it the potato-pie, warmed up slowly
5340: in a safe Sunday fashion, by being put into a dry pot over a
5341: slowly-dying fire, as the best substitute for an oven. For Silas would
5342: not consent to have a grate and oven added to his conveniences: he
5343: loved the old brick hearth as he had loved his brown pot—and was it not
5344: there when he had found Eppie? The gods of the hearth exist for us
5345: still; and let all new faith be tolerant of that fetishism, lest it
5346: bruise its own roots.
5347: 
5348: Silas ate his dinner more silently than usual, soon laying down his
5349: knife and fork, and watching half-abstractedly Eppie’s play with Snap
5350: and the cat, by which her own dining was made rather a lengthy
5351: business. Yet it was a sight that might well arrest wandering thoughts:
5352: Eppie, with the rippling radiance of her hair and the whiteness of her
5353: rounded chin and throat set off by the dark-blue cotton gown, laughing
5354: merrily as the kitten held on with her four claws to one shoulder, like
5355: a design for a jug-handle, while Snap on the right hand and Puss on the
5356: other put up their paws towards a morsel which she held out of the
5357: reach of both—Snap occasionally desisting in order to remonstrate with
5358: the cat by a cogent worrying growl on the greediness and futility of
5359: her conduct; till Eppie relented, caressed them both, and divided the
5360: morsel between them.
5361: 
5362: But at last Eppie, glancing at the clock, checked the play, and said,
5363: “O daddy, you’re wanting to go into the sunshine to smoke your pipe.
5364: But I must clear away first, so as the house may be tidy when godmother
5365: comes. I’ll make haste—I won’t be long.”
5366: 
5367: Silas had taken to smoking a pipe daily during the last two years,
5368: having been strongly urged to it by the sages of Raveloe, as a practice
5369: “good for the fits”; and this advice was sanctioned by Dr. Kimble, on
5370: the ground that it was as well to try what could do no harm—a principle
5371: which was made to answer for a great deal of work in that gentleman’s
5372: medical practice. Silas did not highly enjoy smoking, and often
5373: wondered how his neighbours could be so fond of it; but a humble sort
5374: of acquiescence in what was held to be good, had become a strong habit
5375: of that new self which had been developed in him since he had found
5376: Eppie on his hearth: it had been the only clew his bewildered mind
5377: could hold by in cherishing this young life that had been sent to him
5378: out of the darkness into which his gold had departed. By seeking what
5379: was needful for Eppie, by sharing the effect that everything produced
5380: on her, he had himself come to appropriate the forms of custom and
5381: belief which were the mould of Raveloe life; and as, with reawakening
5382: sensibilities, memory also reawakened, he had begun to ponder over the
5383: elements of his old faith, and blend them with his new impressions,
5384: till he recovered a consciousness of unity between his past and
5385: present. The sense of presiding goodness and the human trust which come
5386: with all pure peace and joy, had given him a dim impression that there
5387: had been some error, some mistake, which had thrown that dark shadow
5388: over the days of his best years; and as it grew more and more easy to
5389: him to open his mind to Dolly Winthrop, he gradually communicated to
5390: her all he could describe of his early life. The communication was
5391: necessarily a slow and difficult process, for Silas’s meagre power of
5392: explanation was not aided by any readiness of interpretation in Dolly,
5393: whose narrow outward experience gave her no key to strange customs, and
5394: made every novelty a source of wonder that arrested them at every step
5395: of the narrative. It was only by fragments, and at intervals which left
5396: Dolly time to revolve what she had heard till it acquired some
5397: familiarity for her, that Silas at last arrived at the climax of the
5398: sad story—the drawing of lots, and its false testimony concerning him;
5399: and this had to be repeated in several interviews, under new questions
5400: on her part as to the nature of this plan for detecting the guilty and
5401: clearing the innocent.
5402: 
5403: “And yourn’s the same Bible, you’re sure o’ that, Master Marner—the
5404: Bible as you brought wi’ you from that country—it’s the same as what
5405: they’ve got at church, and what Eppie’s a-learning to read in?”
5406: 
5407: “Yes,” said Silas, “every bit the same; and there’s drawing o’ lots in
5408: the Bible, mind you,” he added in a lower tone.
5409: 
5410: “Oh, dear, dear,” said Dolly in a grieved voice, as if she were hearing
5411: an unfavourable report of a sick man’s case. She was silent for some
5412: minutes; at last she said—
5413: 
5414: “There’s wise folks, happen, as know how it all is; the parson knows,
5415: I’ll be bound; but it takes big words to tell them things, and such as
5416: poor folks can’t make much out on. I can never rightly know the meaning
5417: o’ what I hear at church, only a bit here and there, but I know it’s
5418: good words—I do. But what lies upo’ your mind—it’s this, Master Marner:
5419: as, if Them above had done the right thing by you, They’d never ha’ let
5420: you be turned out for a wicked thief when you was innicent.”
5421: 
5422: “Ah!” said Silas, who had now come to understand Dolly’s phraseology,
5423: “that was what fell on me like as if it had been red-hot iron; because,
5424: you see, there was nobody as cared for me or clave to me above nor
5425: below. And him as I’d gone out and in wi’ for ten year and more, since
5426: when we was lads and went halves—mine own familiar friend in whom I
5427: trusted, had lifted up his heel again’ me, and worked to ruin me.”
5428: 
5429: “Eh, but he was a bad un—I can’t think as there’s another such,” said
5430: Dolly. “But I’m o’ercome, Master Marner; I’m like as if I’d waked and
5431: didn’t know whether it was night or morning. I feel somehow as sure as
5432: I do when I’ve laid something up though I can’t justly put my hand on
5433: it, as there was a rights in what happened to you, if one could but
5434: make it out; and you’d no call to lose heart as you did. But we’ll talk
5435: on it again; for sometimes things come into my head when I’m leeching
5436: or poulticing, or such, as I could never think on when I was sitting
5437: still.”
5438: 
5439: Dolly was too useful a woman not to have many opportunities of
5440: illumination of the kind she alluded to, and she was not long before
5441: she recurred to the subject.
5442: 
5443: “Master Marner,” she said, one day that she came to bring home Eppie’s
5444: washing, “I’ve been sore puzzled for a good bit wi’ that trouble o’
5445: yourn and the drawing o’ lots; and it got twisted back’ards and
5446: for’ards, as I didn’t know which end to lay hold on. But it come to me
5447: all clear like, that night when I was sitting up wi’ poor Bessy Fawkes,
5448: as is dead and left her children behind, God help ’em—it come to me as
5449: clear as daylight; but whether I’ve got hold on it now, or can anyways
5450: bring it to my tongue’s end, that I don’t know. For I’ve often a deal
5451: inside me as’ll never come out; and for what you talk o’ your folks in
5452: your old country niver saying prayers by heart nor saying ’em out of a
5453: book, they must be wonderful cliver; for if I didn’t know “Our Father”,
5454: and little bits o’ good words as I can carry out o’ church wi’ me, I
5455: might down o’ my knees every night, but nothing could I say.”
5456: 
5457: “But you can mostly say something as I can make sense on, Mrs.
5458: Winthrop,” said Silas.
5459: 
5460: “Well, then, Master Marner, it come to me summat like this: I can make
5461: nothing o’ the drawing o’ lots and the answer coming wrong; it ’ud
5462: mayhap take the parson to tell that, and he could only tell us i’ big
5463: words. But what come to me as clear as the daylight, it was when I was
5464: troubling over poor Bessy Fawkes, and it allays comes into my head when
5465: I’m sorry for folks, and feel as I can’t do a power to help ’em, not if
5466: I was to get up i’ the middle o’ the night—it comes into my head as
5467: Them above has got a deal tenderer heart nor what I’ve got—for I can’t
5468: be anyways better nor Them as made me; and if anything looks hard to
5469: me, it’s because there’s things I don’t know on; and for the matter o’
5470: that, there may be plenty o’ things I don’t know on, for it’s little as
5471: I know—that it is. And so, while I was thinking o’ that, you come into
5472: my mind, Master Marner, and it all come pouring in:—if _I_ felt i’ my
5473: inside what was the right and just thing by you, and them as prayed and
5474: drawed the lots, all but that wicked un, if _they_’d ha’ done the right
5475: thing by you if they could, isn’t there Them as was at the making on
5476: us, and knows better and has a better will? And that’s all as ever I
5477: can be sure on, and everything else is a big puzzle to me when I think
5478: on it. For there was the fever come and took off them as were
5479: full-growed, and left the helpless children; and there’s the breaking
5480: o’ limbs; and them as ’ud do right and be sober have to suffer by them
5481: as are contrairy—eh, there’s trouble i’ this world, and there’s things
5482: as we can niver make out the rights on. And all as we’ve got to do is
5483: to trusten, Master Marner—to do the right thing as fur as we know, and
5484: to trusten. For if us as knows so little can see a bit o’ good and
5485: rights, we may be sure as there’s a good and a rights bigger nor what
5486: we can know—I feel it i’ my own inside as it must be so. And if you
5487: could but ha’ gone on trustening, Master Marner, you wouldn’t ha’ run
5488: away from your fellow-creaturs and been so lone.”
5489: 
5490: “Ah, but that ’ud ha’ been hard,” said Silas, in an under-tone; “it ’ud
5491: ha’ been hard to trusten then.”
5492: 
5493: “And so it would,” said Dolly, almost with compunction; “them things
5494: are easier said nor done; and I’m partly ashamed o’ talking.”
5495: 
5496: “Nay, nay,” said Silas, “you’re i’ the right, Mrs. Winthrop—you’re i’
5497: the right. There’s good i’ this world—I’ve a feeling o’ that now; and
5498: it makes a man feel as there’s a good more nor he can see, i’ spite o’
5499: the trouble and the wickedness. That drawing o’ the lots is dark; but
5500: the child was sent to me: there’s dealings with us—there’s dealings.”
5501: 
5502: This dialogue took place in Eppie’s earlier years, when Silas had to
5503: part with her for two hours every day, that she might learn to read at
5504: the dame school, after he had vainly tried himself to guide her in that
5505: first step to learning. Now that she was grown up, Silas had often been
5506: led, in those moments of quiet outpouring which come to people who live
5507: together in perfect love, to talk with _her_ too of the past, and how
5508: and why he had lived a lonely man until she had been sent to him. For
5509: it would have been impossible for him to hide from Eppie that she was
5510: not his own child: even if the most delicate reticence on the point
5511: could have been expected from Raveloe gossips in her presence, her own
5512: questions about her mother could not have been parried, as she grew up,
5513: without that complete shrouding of the past which would have made a
5514: painful barrier between their minds. So Eppie had long known how her
5515: mother had died on the snowy ground, and how she herself had been found
5516: on the hearth by father Silas, who had taken her golden curls for his
5517: lost guineas brought back to him. The tender and peculiar love with
5518: which Silas had reared her in almost inseparable companionship with
5519: himself, aided by the seclusion of their dwelling, had preserved her
5520: from the lowering influences of the village talk and habits, and had
5521: kept her mind in that freshness which is sometimes falsely supposed to
5522: be an invariable attribute of rusticity. Perfect love has a breath of
5523: poetry which can exalt the relations of the least-instructed human
5524: beings; and this breath of poetry had surrounded Eppie from the time
5525: when she had followed the bright gleam that beckoned her to Silas’s
5526: hearth; so that it is not surprising if, in other things besides her
5527: delicate prettiness, she was not quite a common village maiden, but had
5528: a touch of refinement and fervour which came from no other teaching
5529: than that of tenderly-nurtured unvitiated feeling. She was too childish
5530: and simple for her imagination to rove into questions about her unknown
5531: father; for a long while it did not even occur to her that she must
5532: have had a father; and the first time that the idea of her mother
5533: having had a husband presented itself to her, was when Silas showed her
5534: the wedding-ring which had been taken from the wasted finger, and had
5535: been carefully preserved by him in a little lackered box shaped like a
5536: shoe. He delivered this box into Eppie’s charge when she had grown up,
5537: and she often opened it to look at the ring: but still she thought
5538: hardly at all about the father of whom it was the symbol. Had she not a
5539: father very close to her, who loved her better than any real fathers in
5540: the village seemed to love their daughters? On the contrary, who her
5541: mother was, and how she came to die in that forlornness, were questions
5542: that often pressed on Eppie’s mind. Her knowledge of Mrs. Winthrop, who
5543: was her nearest friend next to Silas, made her feel that a mother must
5544: be very precious; and she had again and again asked Silas to tell her
5545: how her mother looked, whom she was like, and how he had found her
5546: against the furze bush, led towards it by the little footsteps and the
5547: outstretched arms. The furze bush was there still; and this afternoon,
5548: when Eppie came out with Silas into the sunshine, it was the first
5549: object that arrested her eyes and thoughts.
5550: 
5551: “Father,” she said, in a tone of gentle gravity, which sometimes came
5552: like a sadder, slower cadence across her playfulness, “we shall take
5553: the furze bush into the garden; it’ll come into the corner, and just
5554: against it I’ll put snowdrops and crocuses, ’cause Aaron says they
5555: won’t die out, but’ll always get more and more.”
5556: 
5557: “Ah, child,” said Silas, always ready to talk when he had his pipe in
5558: his hand, apparently enjoying the pauses more than the puffs, “it
5559: wouldn’t do to leave out the furze bush; and there’s nothing prettier,
5560: to my thinking, when it’s yallow with flowers. But it’s just come into
5561: my head what we’re to do for a fence—mayhap Aaron can help us to a
5562: thought; but a fence we must have, else the donkeys and things ’ull
5563: come and trample everything down. And fencing’s hard to be got at, by
5564: what I can make out.”
5565: 
5566: “Oh, I’ll tell you, daddy,” said Eppie, clasping her hands suddenly,
5567: after a minute’s thought. “There’s lots o’ loose stones about, some of
5568: ’em not big, and we might lay ’em atop of one another, and make a wall.
5569: You and me could carry the smallest, and Aaron ’ud carry the rest—I
5570: know he would.”
5571: 
5572: “Eh, my precious un,” said Silas, “there isn’t enough stones to go all
5573: round; and as for you carrying, why, wi’ your little arms you couldn’t
5574: carry a stone no bigger than a turnip. You’re dillicate made, my dear,”
5575: he added, with a tender intonation—“that’s what Mrs. Winthrop says.”
5576: 
5577: “Oh, I’m stronger than you think, daddy,” said Eppie; “and if there
5578: wasn’t stones enough to go all round, why they’ll go part o’ the way,
5579: and then it’ll be easier to get sticks and things for the rest. See
5580: here, round the big pit, what a many stones!”
5581: 
5582: She skipped forward to the pit, meaning to lift one of the stones and
5583: exhibit her strength, but she started back in surprise.
5584: 
5585: “Oh, father, just come and look here,” she exclaimed—“come and see how
5586: the water’s gone down since yesterday. Why, yesterday the pit was ever
5587: so full!”
5588: 
5589: “Well, to be sure,” said Silas, coming to her side. “Why, that’s the
5590: draining they’ve begun on, since harvest, i’ Mr. Osgood’s fields, I
5591: reckon. The foreman said to me the other day, when I passed by ’em,
5592: “Master Marner,” he said, “I shouldn’t wonder if we lay your bit o’
5593: waste as dry as a bone.” It was Mr. Godfrey Cass, he said, had gone
5594: into the draining: he’d been taking these fields o’ Mr. Osgood.”
5595: 
5596: “How odd it’ll seem to have the old pit dried up!” said Eppie, turning
5597: away, and stooping to lift rather a large stone. “See, daddy, I can
5598: carry this quite well,” she said, going along with much energy for a
5599: few steps, but presently letting it fall.
5600: 
5601: “Ah, you’re fine and strong, aren’t you?” said Silas, while Eppie shook
5602: her aching arms and laughed. “Come, come, let us go and sit down on the
5603: bank against the stile there, and have no more lifting. You might hurt
5604: yourself, child. You’d need have somebody to work for you—and my arm
5605: isn’t over strong.”
5606: 
5607: Silas uttered the last sentence slowly, as if it implied more than met
5608: the ear; and Eppie, when they sat down on the bank, nestled close to
5609: his side, and, taking hold caressingly of the arm that was not over
5610: strong, held it on her lap, while Silas puffed again dutifully at the
5611: pipe, which occupied his other arm. An ash in the hedgerow behind made
5612: a fretted screen from the sun, and threw happy playful shadows all
5613: about them.
5614: 
5615: “Father,” said Eppie, very gently, after they had been sitting in
5616: silence a little while, “if I was to be married, ought I to be married
5617: with my mother’s ring?”
5618: 
5619: Silas gave an almost imperceptible start, though the question fell in
5620: with the under-current of thought in his own mind, and then said, in a
5621: subdued tone, “Why, Eppie, have you been a-thinking on it?”
5622: 
5623: “Only this last week, father,” said Eppie, ingenuously, “since Aaron
5624: talked to me about it.”
5625: 
5626: “And what did he say?” said Silas, still in the same subdued way, as if
5627: he were anxious lest he should fall into the slightest tone that was
5628: not for Eppie’s good.
5629: 
5630: “He said he should like to be married, because he was a-going in
5631: four-and-twenty, and had got a deal of gardening work, now Mr. Mott’s
5632: given up; and he goes twice a-week regular to Mr. Cass’s, and once to
5633: Mr. Osgood’s, and they’re going to take him on at the Rectory.”
5634: 
5635: “And who is it as he’s wanting to marry?” said Silas, with rather a sad
5636: smile.
5637: 
5638: “Why, me, to be sure, daddy,” said Eppie, with dimpling laughter,
5639: kissing her father’s cheek; “as if he’d want to marry anybody else!”
5640: 
5641: “And you mean to have him, do you?” said Silas.
5642: 
5643: “Yes, some time,” said Eppie, “I don’t know when. Everybody’s married
5644: some time, Aaron says. But I told him that wasn’t true: for, I said,
5645: look at father—he’s never been married.”
5646: 
5647: “No, child,” said Silas, “your father was a lone man till you was sent
5648: to him.”
5649: 
5650: “But you’ll never be lone again, father,” said Eppie, tenderly. “That
5651: was what Aaron said—“I could never think o’ taking you away from Master
5652: Marner, Eppie.” And I said, “It ’ud be no use if you did, Aaron.” And
5653: he wants us all to live together, so as you needn’t work a bit, father,
5654: only what’s for your own pleasure; and he’d be as good as a son to
5655: you—that was what he said.”
5656: 
5657: “And should you like that, Eppie?” said Silas, looking at her.
5658: 
5659: “I shouldn’t mind it, father,” said Eppie, quite simply. “And I should
5660: like things to be so as you needn’t work much. But if it wasn’t for
5661: that, I’d sooner things didn’t change. I’m very happy: I like Aaron to
5662: be fond of me, and come and see us often, and behave pretty to you—he
5663: always _does_ behave pretty to you, doesn’t he, father?”
5664: 
5665: “Yes, child, nobody could behave better,” said Silas, emphatically.
5666: “He’s his mother’s lad.”
5667: 
5668: “But I don’t want any change,” said Eppie. “I should like to go on a
5669: long, long while, just as we are. Only Aaron does want a change; and he
5670: made me cry a bit—only a bit—because he said I didn’t care for him, for
5671: if I cared for him I should want us to be married, as he did.”
5672: 
5673: “Eh, my blessed child,” said Silas, laying down his pipe as if it were
5674: useless to pretend to smoke any longer, “you’re o’er young to be
5675: married. We’ll ask Mrs. Winthrop—we’ll ask Aaron’s mother what _she_
5676: thinks: if there’s a right thing to do, she’ll come at it. But there’s
5677: this to be thought on, Eppie: things _will_ change, whether we like it
5678: or no; things won’t go on for a long while just as they are and no
5679: difference. I shall get older and helplesser, and be a burden on you,
5680: belike, if I don’t go away from you altogether. Not as I mean you’d
5681: think me a burden—I know you wouldn’t—but it ’ud be hard upon you; and
5682: when I look for’ard to that, I like to think as you’d have somebody
5683: else besides me—somebody young and strong, as’ll outlast your own life,
5684: and take care on you to the end.” Silas paused, and, resting his wrists
5685: on his knees, lifted his hands up and down meditatively as he looked on
5686: the ground.
5687: 
5688: “Then, would you like me to be married, father?” said Eppie, with a
5689: little trembling in her voice.
5690: 
5691: “I’ll not be the man to say no, Eppie,” said Silas, emphatically; “but
5692: we’ll ask your godmother. She’ll wish the right thing by you and her
5693: son too.”
5694: 
5695: “There they come, then,” said Eppie. “Let us go and meet ’em. Oh, the
5696: pipe! won’t you have it lit again, father?” said Eppie, lifting that
5697: medicinal appliance from the ground.
5698: 
5699: “Nay, child,” said Silas, “I’ve done enough for to-day. I think,
5700: mayhap, a little of it does me more good than so much at once.”
5701: 
5702: 
5703: 
5704: CHAPTER XVII.
5705: 
5706: While Silas and Eppie were seated on the bank discoursing in the
5707: fleckered shade of the ash tree, Miss Priscilla Lammeter was resisting
5708: her sister’s arguments, that it would be better to take tea at the Red
5709: House, and let her father have a long nap, than drive home to the
5710: Warrens so soon after dinner. The family party (of four only) were
5711: seated round the table in the dark wainscoted parlour, with the Sunday
5712: dessert before them, of fresh filberts, apples, and pears, duly
5713: ornamented with leaves by Nancy’s own hand before the bells had rung
5714: for church.
5715: 
5716: A great change has come over the dark wainscoted parlour since we saw
5717: it in Godfrey’s bachelor days, and under the wifeless reign of the old
5718: Squire. Now all is polish, on which no yesterday’s dust is ever allowed
5719: to rest, from the yard’s width of oaken boards round the carpet, to the
5720: old Squire’s gun and whips and walking-sticks, ranged on the stag’s
5721: antlers above the mantelpiece. All other signs of sporting and outdoor
5722: occupation Nancy has removed to another room; but she has brought into
5723: the Red House the habit of filial reverence, and preserves sacredly in
5724: a place of honour these relics of her husband’s departed father. The
5725: tankards are on the side-table still, but the bossed silver is undimmed
5726: by handling, and there are no dregs to send forth unpleasant
5727: suggestions: the only prevailing scent is of the lavender and
5728: rose-leaves that fill the vases of Derbyshire spar. All is purity and
5729: order in this once dreary room, for, fifteen years ago, it was entered
5730: by a new presiding spirit.
5731: 
5732: “Now, father,” said Nancy, “_is_ there any call for you to go home to
5733: tea? Mayn’t you just as well stay with us?—such a beautiful evening as
5734: it’s likely to be.”
5735: 
5736: The old gentleman had been talking with Godfrey about the increasing
5737: poor-rate and the ruinous times, and had not heard the dialogue between
5738: his daughters.
5739: 
5740: “My dear, you must ask Priscilla,” he said, in the once firm voice, now
5741: become rather broken. “She manages me and the farm too.”
5742: 
5743: “And reason good as I should manage you, father,” said Priscilla, “else
5744: you’d be giving yourself your death with rheumatism. And as for the
5745: farm, if anything turns out wrong, as it can’t but do in these times,
5746: there’s nothing kills a man so soon as having nobody to find fault with
5747: but himself. It’s a deal the best way o’ being master, to let somebody
5748: else do the ordering, and keep the blaming in your own hands. It ’ud
5749: save many a man a stroke, _I_ believe.”
5750: 
5751: “Well, well, my dear,” said her father, with a quiet laugh, “I didn’t
5752: say you don’t manage for everybody’s good.”
5753: 
5754: “Then manage so as you may stay tea, Priscilla,” said Nancy, putting
5755: her hand on her sister’s arm affectionately. “Come now; and we’ll go
5756: round the garden while father has his nap.”
5757: 
5758: “My dear child, he’ll have a beautiful nap in the gig, for I shall
5759: drive. And as for staying tea, I can’t hear of it; for there’s this
5760: dairymaid, now she knows she’s to be married, turned Michaelmas, she’d
5761: as lief pour the new milk into the pig-trough as into the pans. That’s
5762: the way with ’em all: it’s as if they thought the world ’ud be new-made
5763: because they’re to be married. So come and let me put my bonnet on, and
5764: there’ll be time for us to walk round the garden while the horse is
5765: being put in.”
5766: 
5767: When the sisters were treading the neatly-swept garden-walks, between
5768: the bright turf that contrasted pleasantly with the dark cones and
5769: arches and wall-like hedges of yew, Priscilla said—
5770: 
5771: “I’m as glad as anything at your husband’s making that exchange o’ land
5772: with cousin Osgood, and beginning the dairying. It’s a thousand pities
5773: you didn’t do it before; for it’ll give you something to fill your
5774: mind. There’s nothing like a dairy if folks want a bit o’ worrit to
5775: make the days pass. For as for rubbing furniture, when you can once see
5776: your face in a table there’s nothing else to look for; but there’s
5777: always something fresh with the dairy; for even in the depths o’ winter
5778: there’s some pleasure in conquering the butter, and making it come
5779: whether or no. My dear,” added Priscilla, pressing her sister’s hand
5780: affectionately as they walked side by side, “you’ll never be low when
5781: you’ve got a dairy.”
5782: 
5783: “Ah, Priscilla,” said Nancy, returning the pressure with a grateful
5784: glance of her clear eyes, “but it won’t make up to Godfrey: a dairy’s
5785: not so much to a man. And it’s only what he cares for that ever makes
5786: me low. I’m contented with the blessings we have, if he could be
5787: contented.”
5788: 
5789: “It drives me past patience,” said Priscilla, impetuously, “that way o’
5790: the men—always wanting and wanting, and never easy with what they’ve
5791: got: they can’t sit comfortable in their chairs when they’ve neither
5792: ache nor pain, but either they must stick a pipe in their mouths, to
5793: make ’em better than well, or else they must be swallowing something
5794: strong, though they’re forced to make haste before the next meal comes
5795: in. But joyful be it spoken, our father was never that sort o’ man. And
5796: if it had pleased God to make you ugly, like me, so as the men wouldn’t
5797: ha’ run after you, we might have kept to our own family, and had
5798: nothing to do with folks as have got uneasy blood in their veins.”
5799: 
5800: “Oh, don’t say so, Priscilla,” said Nancy, repenting that she had
5801: called forth this outburst; “nobody has any occasion to find fault with
5802: Godfrey. It’s natural he should be disappointed at not having any
5803: children: every man likes to have somebody to work for and lay by for,
5804: and he always counted so on making a fuss with ’em when they were
5805: little. There’s many another man ’ud hanker more than he does. He’s the
5806: best of husbands.”
5807: 
5808: “Oh, I know,” said Priscilla, smiling sarcastically, “I know the way o’
5809: wives; they set one on to abuse their husbands, and then they turn
5810: round on one and praise ’em as if they wanted to sell ’em. But
5811: father’ll be waiting for me; we must turn now.”
5812: 
5813: The large gig with the steady old grey was at the front door, and Mr.
5814: Lammeter was already on the stone steps, passing the time in recalling
5815: to Godfrey what very fine points Speckle had when his master used to
5816: ride him.
5817: 
5818: “I always _would_ have a good horse, you know,” said the old gentleman,
5819: not liking that spirited time to be quite effaced from the memory of
5820: his juniors.
5821: 
5822: “Mind you bring Nancy to the Warrens before the week’s out, Mr. Cass,”
5823: was Priscilla’s parting injunction, as she took the reins, and shook
5824: them gently, by way of friendly incitement to Speckle.
5825: 
5826: “I shall just take a turn to the fields against the Stone-pits, Nancy,
5827: and look at the draining,” said Godfrey.
5828: 
5829: “You’ll be in again by tea-time, dear?”
5830: 
5831: “Oh, yes, I shall be back in an hour.”
5832: 
5833: It was Godfrey’s custom on a Sunday afternoon to do a little
5834: contemplative farming in a leisurely walk. Nancy seldom accompanied
5835: him; for the women of her generation—unless, like Priscilla, they took
5836: to outdoor management—were not given to much walking beyond their own
5837: house and garden, finding sufficient exercise in domestic duties. So,
5838: when Priscilla was not with her, she usually sat with Mant’s Bible
5839: before her, and after following the text with her eyes for a little
5840: while, she would gradually permit them to wander as her thoughts had
5841: already insisted on wandering.
5842: 
5843: But Nancy’s Sunday thoughts were rarely quite out of keeping with the
5844: devout and reverential intention implied by the book spread open before
5845: her. She was not theologically instructed enough to discern very
5846: clearly the relation between the sacred documents of the past which she
5847: opened without method, and her own obscure, simple life; but the spirit
5848: of rectitude, and the sense of responsibility for the effect of her
5849: conduct on others, which were strong elements in Nancy’s character, had
5850: made it a habit with her to scrutinize her past feelings and actions
5851: with self-questioning solicitude. Her mind not being courted by a great
5852: variety of subjects, she filled the vacant moments by living inwardly,
5853: again and again, through all her remembered experience, especially
5854: through the fifteen years of her married time, in which her life and
5855: its significance had been doubled. She recalled the small details, the
5856: words, tones, and looks, in the critical scenes which had opened a new
5857: epoch for her by giving her a deeper insight into the relations and
5858: trials of life, or which had called on her for some little effort of
5859: forbearance, or of painful adherence to an imagined or real duty—asking
5860: herself continually whether she had been in any respect blamable. This
5861: excessive rumination and self-questioning is perhaps a morbid habit
5862: inevitable to a mind of much moral sensibility when shut out from its
5863: due share of outward activity and of practical claims on its
5864: affections—inevitable to a noble-hearted, childless woman, when her lot
5865: is narrow. “I can do so little—have I done it all well?” is the
5866: perpetually recurring thought; and there are no voices calling her away
5867: from that soliloquy, no peremptory demands to divert energy from vain
5868: regret or superfluous scruple.
5869: 
5870: There was one main thread of painful experience in Nancy’s married
5871: life, and on it hung certain deeply-felt scenes, which were the
5872: oftenest revived in retrospect. The short dialogue with Priscilla in
5873: the garden had determined the current of retrospect in that frequent
5874: direction this particular Sunday afternoon. The first wandering of her
5875: thought from the text, which she still attempted dutifully to follow
5876: with her eyes and silent lips, was into an imaginary enlargement of the
5877: defence she had set up for her husband against Priscilla’s implied
5878: blame. The vindication of the loved object is the best balm affection
5879: can find for its wounds:—“A man must have so much on his mind,” is the
5880: belief by which a wife often supports a cheerful face under rough
5881: answers and unfeeling words. And Nancy’s deepest wounds had all come
5882: from the perception that the absence of children from their hearth was
5883: dwelt on in her husband’s mind as a privation to which he could not
5884: reconcile himself.
5885: 
5886: Yet sweet Nancy might have been expected to feel still more keenly the
5887: denial of a blessing to which she had looked forward with all the
5888: varied expectations and preparations, solemn and prettily trivial,
5889: which fill the mind of a loving woman when she expects to become a
5890: mother. Was there not a drawer filled with the neat work of her hands,
5891: all unworn and untouched, just as she had arranged it there fourteen
5892: years ago—just, but for one little dress, which had been made the
5893: burial-dress? But under this immediate personal trial Nancy was so
5894: firmly unmurmuring, that years ago she had suddenly renounced the habit
5895: of visiting this drawer, lest she should in this way be cherishing a
5896: longing for what was not given.
5897: 
5898: Perhaps it was this very severity towards any indulgence of what she
5899: held to be sinful regret in herself, that made her shrink from applying
5900: her own standard to her husband. “It is very different—it is much worse
5901: for a man to be disappointed in that way: a woman can always be
5902: satisfied with devoting herself to her husband, but a man wants
5903: something that will make him look forward more—and sitting by the fire
5904: is so much duller to him than to a woman.” And always, when Nancy
5905: reached this point in her meditations—trying, with predetermined
5906: sympathy, to see everything as Godfrey saw it—there came a renewal of
5907: self-questioning. _Had_ she done everything in her power to lighten
5908: Godfrey’s privation? Had she really been right in the resistance which
5909: had cost her so much pain six years ago, and again four years ago—the
5910: resistance to her husband’s wish that they should adopt a child?
5911: Adoption was more remote from the ideas and habits of that time than of
5912: our own; still Nancy had her opinion on it. It was as necessary to her
5913: mind to have an opinion on all topics, not exclusively masculine, that
5914: had come under her notice, as for her to have a precisely marked place
5915: for every article of her personal property: and her opinions were
5916: always principles to be unwaveringly acted on. They were firm, not
5917: because of their basis, but because she held them with a tenacity
5918: inseparable from her mental action. On all the duties and proprieties
5919: of life, from filial behaviour to the arrangements of the evening
5920: toilette, pretty Nancy Lammeter, by the time she was three-and-twenty,
5921: had her unalterable little code, and had formed every one of her habits
5922: in strict accordance with that code. She carried these decided
5923: judgments within her in the most unobtrusive way: they rooted
5924: themselves in her mind, and grew there as quietly as grass. Years ago,
5925: we know, she insisted on dressing like Priscilla, because “it was right
5926: for sisters to dress alike”, and because “she would do what was right
5927: if she wore a gown dyed with cheese-colouring”. That was a trivial but
5928: typical instance of the mode in which Nancy’s life was regulated.
5929: 
5930: It was one of those rigid principles, and no petty egoistic feeling,
5931: which had been the ground of Nancy’s difficult resistance to her
5932: husband’s wish. To adopt a child, because children of your own had been
5933: denied you, was to try and choose your lot in spite of Providence: the
5934: adopted child, she was convinced, would never turn out well, and would
5935: be a curse to those who had wilfully and rebelliously sought what it
5936: was clear that, for some high reason, they were better without. When
5937: you saw a thing was not meant to be, said Nancy, it was a bounden duty
5938: to leave off so much as wishing for it. And so far, perhaps, the wisest
5939: of men could scarcely make more than a verbal improvement in her
5940: principle. But the conditions under which she held it apparent that a
5941: thing was not meant to be, depended on a more peculiar mode of
5942: thinking. She would have given up making a purchase at a particular
5943: place if, on three successive times, rain, or some other cause of
5944: Heaven’s sending, had formed an obstacle; and she would have
5945: anticipated a broken limb or other heavy misfortune to any one who
5946: persisted in spite of such indications.
5947: 
5948: “But why should you think the child would turn out ill?” said Godfrey,
5949: in his remonstrances. “She has thriven as well as child can do with the
5950: weaver; and _he_ adopted her. There isn’t such a pretty little girl
5951: anywhere else in the parish, or one fitter for the station we could
5952: give her. Where can be the likelihood of her being a curse to anybody?”
5953: 
5954: “Yes, my dear Godfrey,” said Nancy, who was sitting with her hands
5955: tightly clasped together, and with yearning, regretful affection in her
5956: eyes. “The child may not turn out ill with the weaver. But, then, he
5957: didn’t go to seek her, as we should be doing. It will be wrong: I feel
5958: sure it will. Don’t you remember what that lady we met at the Royston
5959: Baths told us about the child her sister adopted? That was the only
5960: adopting I ever heard of: and the child was transported when it was
5961: twenty-three. Dear Godfrey, don’t ask me to do what I know is wrong: I
5962: should never be happy again. I know it’s very hard for _you_—it’s
5963: easier for me—but it’s the will of Providence.”
5964: 
5965: It might seem singular that Nancy—with her religious theory pieced
5966: together out of narrow social traditions, fragments of church doctrine
5967: imperfectly understood, and girlish reasonings on her small
5968: experience—should have arrived by herself at a way of thinking so
5969: nearly akin to that of many devout people, whose beliefs are held in
5970: the shape of a system quite remote from her knowledge—singular, if we
5971: did not know that human beliefs, like all other natural growths, elude
5972: the barriers of system.
5973: 
5974: Godfrey had from the first specified Eppie, then about twelve years
5975: old, as a child suitable for them to adopt. It had never occurred to
5976: him that Silas would rather part with his life than with Eppie. Surely
5977: the weaver would wish the best to the child he had taken so much
5978: trouble with, and would be glad that such good fortune should happen to
5979: her: she would always be very grateful to him, and he would be well
5980: provided for to the end of his life—provided for as the excellent part
5981: he had done by the child deserved. Was it not an appropriate thing for
5982: people in a higher station to take a charge off the hands of a man in a
5983: lower? It seemed an eminently appropriate thing to Godfrey, for reasons
5984: that were known only to himself; and by a common fallacy, he imagined
5985: the measure would be easy because he had private motives for desiring
5986: it. This was rather a coarse mode of estimating Silas’s relation to
5987: Eppie; but we must remember that many of the impressions which Godfrey
5988: was likely to gather concerning the labouring people around him would
5989: favour the idea that deep affections can hardly go along with callous
5990: palms and scant means; and he had not had the opportunity, even if he
5991: had had the power, of entering intimately into all that was exceptional
5992: in the weaver’s experience. It was only the want of adequate knowledge
5993: that could have made it possible for Godfrey deliberately to entertain
5994: an unfeeling project: his natural kindness had outlived that blighting
5995: time of cruel wishes, and Nancy’s praise of him as a husband was not
5996: founded entirely on a wilful illusion.
5997: 
5998: “I was right,” she said to herself, when she had recalled all their
5999: scenes of discussion—“I feel I was right to say him nay, though it hurt
6000: me more than anything; but how good Godfrey has been about it! Many men
6001: would have been very angry with me for standing out against their
6002: wishes; and they might have thrown out that they’d had ill-luck in
6003: marrying me; but Godfrey has never been the man to say me an unkind
6004: word. It’s only what he can’t hide: everything seems so blank to him, I
6005: know; and the land—what a difference it ’ud make to him, when he goes
6006: to see after things, if he’d children growing up that he was doing it
6007: all for! But I won’t murmur; and perhaps if he’d married a woman who’d
6008: have had children, she’d have vexed him in other ways.”
6009: 
6010: This possibility was Nancy’s chief comfort; and to give it greater
6011: strength, she laboured to make it impossible that any other wife should
6012: have had more perfect tenderness. She had been _forced_ to vex him by
6013: that one denial. Godfrey was not insensible to her loving effort, and
6014: did Nancy no injustice as to the motives of her obstinacy. It was
6015: impossible to have lived with her fifteen years and not be aware that
6016: an unselfish clinging to the right, and a sincerity clear as the
6017: flower-born dew, were her main characteristics; indeed, Godfrey felt
6018: this so strongly, that his own more wavering nature, too averse to
6019: facing difficulty to be unvaryingly simple and truthful, was kept in a
6020: certain awe of this gentle wife who watched his looks with a yearning
6021: to obey them. It seemed to him impossible that he should ever confess
6022: to her the truth about Eppie: she would never recover from the
6023: repulsion the story of his earlier marriage would create, told to her
6024: now, after that long concealment. And the child, too, he thought, must
6025: become an object of repulsion: the very sight of her would be painful.
6026: The shock to Nancy’s mingled pride and ignorance of the world’s evil
6027: might even be too much for her delicate frame. Since he had married her
6028: with that secret on his heart, he must keep it there to the last.
6029: Whatever else he did, he could not make an irreparable breach between
6030: himself and this long-loved wife.
6031: 
6032: Meanwhile, why could he not make up his mind to the absence of children
6033: from a hearth brightened by such a wife? Why did his mind fly uneasily
6034: to that void, as if it were the sole reason why life was not thoroughly
6035: joyous to him? I suppose it is the way with all men and women who reach
6036: middle age without the clear perception that life never _can_ be
6037: thoroughly joyous: under the vague dullness of the grey hours,
6038: dissatisfaction seeks a definite object, and finds it in the privation
6039: of an untried good. Dissatisfaction seated musingly on a childless
6040: hearth, thinks with envy of the father whose return is greeted by young
6041: voices—seated at the meal where the little heads rise one above another
6042: like nursery plants, it sees a black care hovering behind every one of
6043: them, and thinks the impulses by which men abandon freedom, and seek
6044: for ties, are surely nothing but a brief madness. In Godfrey’s case
6045: there were further reasons why his thoughts should be continually
6046: solicited by this one point in his lot: his conscience, never
6047: thoroughly easy about Eppie, now gave his childless home the aspect of
6048: a retribution; and as the time passed on, under Nancy’s refusal to
6049: adopt her, any retrieval of his error became more and more difficult.
6050: 
6051: On this Sunday afternoon it was already four years since there had been
6052: any allusion to the subject between them, and Nancy supposed that it
6053: was for ever buried.
6054: 
6055: “I wonder if he’ll mind it less or more as he gets older,” she thought;
6056: “I’m afraid more. Aged people feel the miss of children: what would
6057: father do without Priscilla? And if I die, Godfrey will be very
6058: lonely—not holding together with his brothers much. But I won’t be
6059: over-anxious, and trying to make things out beforehand: I must do my
6060: best for the present.”
6061: 
6062: With that last thought Nancy roused herself from her reverie, and
6063: turned her eyes again towards the forsaken page. It had been forsaken
6064: longer than she imagined, for she was presently surprised by the
6065: appearance of the servant with the tea-things. It was, in fact, a
6066: little before the usual time for tea; but Jane had her reasons.
6067: 
6068: “Is your master come into the yard, Jane?”
6069: 
6070: “No ’m, he isn’t,” said Jane, with a slight emphasis, of which,
6071: however, her mistress took no notice.
6072: 
6073: “I don’t know whether you’ve seen ’em, ’m,” continued Jane, after a
6074: pause, “but there’s folks making haste all one way, afore the front
6075: window. I doubt something’s happened. There’s niver a man to be seen i’
6076: the yard, else I’d send and see. I’ve been up into the top attic, but
6077: there’s no seeing anything for trees. I hope nobody’s hurt, that’s
6078: all.”
6079: 
6080: “Oh, no, I daresay there’s nothing much the matter,” said Nancy. “It’s
6081: perhaps Mr. Snell’s bull got out again, as he did before.”
6082: 
6083: “I wish he mayn’t gore anybody then, that’s all,” said Jane, not
6084: altogether despising a hypothesis which covered a few imaginary
6085: calamities.
6086: 
6087: “That girl is always terrifying me,” thought Nancy; “I wish Godfrey
6088: would come in.”
6089: 
6090: She went to the front window and looked as far as she could see along
6091: the road, with an uneasiness which she felt to be childish, for there
6092: were now no such signs of excitement as Jane had spoken of, and Godfrey
6093: would not be likely to return by the village road, but by the fields.
6094: She continued to stand, however, looking at the placid churchyard with
6095: the long shadows of the gravestones across the bright green hillocks,
6096: and at the glowing autumn colours of the Rectory trees beyond. Before
6097: such calm external beauty the presence of a vague fear is more
6098: distinctly felt—like a raven flapping its slow wing across the sunny
6099: air. Nancy wished more and more that Godfrey would come in.
6100: 
6101: 
6102: 
6103: CHAPTER XVIII.
6104: 
6105: Some one opened the door at the other end of the room, and Nancy felt
6106: that it was her husband. She turned from the window with gladness in
6107: her eyes, for the wife’s chief dread was stilled.
6108: 
6109: “Dear, I’m so thankful you’re come,” she said, going towards him. “I
6110: began to get—”
6111: 
6112: She paused abruptly, for Godfrey was laying down his hat with trembling
6113: hands, and turned towards her with a pale face and a strange
6114: unanswering glance, as if he saw her indeed, but saw her as part of a
6115: scene invisible to herself. She laid her hand on his arm, not daring to
6116: speak again; but he left the touch unnoticed, and threw himself into
6117: his chair.
6118: 
6119: Jane was already at the door with the hissing urn. “Tell her to keep
6120: away, will you?” said Godfrey; and when the door was closed again he
6121: exerted himself to speak more distinctly.
6122: 
6123: “Sit down, Nancy—there,” he said, pointing to a chair opposite him. “I
6124: came back as soon as I could, to hinder anybody’s telling you but me.
6125: I’ve had a great shock—but I care most about the shock it’ll be to
6126: you.”
6127: 
6128: “It isn’t father and Priscilla?” said Nancy, with quivering lips,
6129: clasping her hands together tightly on her lap.
6130: 
6131: “No, it’s nobody living,” said Godfrey, unequal to the considerate
6132: skill with which he would have wished to make his revelation. “It’s
6133: Dunstan—my brother Dunstan, that we lost sight of sixteen years ago.
6134: We’ve found him—found his body—his skeleton.”
6135: 
6136: The deep dread Godfrey’s look had created in Nancy made her feel these
6137: words a relief. She sat in comparative calmness to hear what else he
6138: had to tell. He went on:
6139: 
6140: “The Stone-pit has gone dry suddenly—from the draining, I suppose; and
6141: there he lies—has lain for sixteen years, wedged between two great
6142: stones. There’s his watch and seals, and there’s my gold-handled
6143: hunting-whip, with my name on: he took it away, without my knowing, the
6144: day he went hunting on Wildfire, the last time he was seen.”
6145: 
6146: Godfrey paused: it was not so easy to say what came next. “Do you think
6147: he drowned himself?” said Nancy, almost wondering that her husband
6148: should be so deeply shaken by what had happened all those years ago to
6149: an unloved brother, of whom worse things had been augured.
6150: 
6151: “No, he fell in,” said Godfrey, in a low but distinct voice, as if he
6152: felt some deep meaning in the fact. Presently he added: “Dunstan was
6153: the man that robbed Silas Marner.”
6154: 
6155: The blood rushed to Nancy’s face and neck at this surprise and shame,
6156: for she had been bred up to regard even a distant kinship with crime as
6157: a dishonour.
6158: 
6159: “O Godfrey!” she said, with compassion in her tone, for she had
6160: immediately reflected that the dishonour must be felt still more keenly
6161: by her husband.
6162: 
6163: “There was the money in the pit,” he continued—“all the weaver’s money.
6164: Everything’s been gathered up, and they’re taking the skeleton to the
6165: Rainbow. But I came back to tell you: there was no hindering it; you
6166: must know.”
6167: 
6168: He was silent, looking on the ground for two long minutes. Nancy would
6169: have said some words of comfort under this disgrace, but she refrained,
6170: from an instinctive sense that there was something behind—that Godfrey
6171: had something else to tell her. Presently he lifted his eyes to her
6172: face, and kept them fixed on her, as he said—
6173: 
6174: “Everything comes to light, Nancy, sooner or later. When God Almighty
6175: wills it, our secrets are found out. I’ve lived with a secret on my
6176: mind, but I’ll keep it from you no longer. I wouldn’t have you know it
6177: by somebody else, and not by me—I wouldn’t have you find it out after
6178: I’m dead. I’ll tell you now. It’s been “I will” and “I won’t” with me
6179: all my life—I’ll make sure of myself now.”
6180: 
6181: Nancy’s utmost dread had returned. The eyes of the husband and wife met
6182: with awe in them, as at a crisis which suspended affection.
6183: 
6184: “Nancy,” said Godfrey, slowly, “when I married you, I hid something
6185: from you—something I ought to have told you. That woman Marner found
6186: dead in the snow—Eppie’s mother—that wretched woman—was my wife: Eppie
6187: is my child.”
6188: 
6189: He paused, dreading the effect of his confession. But Nancy sat quite
6190: still, only that her eyes dropped and ceased to meet his. She was pale
6191: and quiet as a meditative statue, clasping her hands on her lap.
6192: 
6193: “You’ll never think the same of me again,” said Godfrey, after a little
6194: while, with some tremor in his voice.
6195: 
6196: She was silent.
6197: 
6198: “I oughtn’t to have left the child unowned: I oughtn’t to have kept it
6199: from you. But I couldn’t bear to give you up, Nancy. I was led away
6200: into marrying her—I suffered for it.”
6201: 
6202: Still Nancy was silent, looking down; and he almost expected that she
6203: would presently get up and say she would go to her father’s. How could
6204: she have any mercy for faults that must seem so black to her, with her
6205: simple, severe notions?
6206: 
6207: But at last she lifted up her eyes to his again and spoke. There was no
6208: indignation in her voice—only deep regret.
6209: 
6210: “Godfrey, if you had but told me this six years ago, we could have done
6211: some of our duty by the child. Do you think I’d have refused to take
6212: her in, if I’d known she was yours?”
6213: 
6214: At that moment Godfrey felt all the bitterness of an error that was not
6215: simply futile, but had defeated its own end. He had not measured this
6216: wife with whom he had lived so long. But she spoke again, with more
6217: agitation.
6218: 
6219: “And—Oh, Godfrey—if we’d had her from the first, if you’d taken to her
6220: as you ought, she’d have loved me for her mother—and you’d have been
6221: happier with me: I could better have bore my little baby dying, and our
6222: life might have been more like what we used to think it ’ud be.”
6223: 
6224: The tears fell, and Nancy ceased to speak.
6225: 
6226: “But you wouldn’t have married me then, Nancy, if I’d told you,” said
6227: Godfrey, urged, in the bitterness of his self-reproach, to prove to
6228: himself that his conduct had not been utter folly. “You may think you
6229: would now, but you wouldn’t then. With your pride and your father’s,
6230: you’d have hated having anything to do with me after the talk there’d
6231: have been.”
6232: 
6233: “I can’t say what I should have done about that, Godfrey. I should
6234: never have married anybody else. But I wasn’t worth doing wrong
6235: for—nothing is in this world. Nothing is so good as it seems
6236: beforehand—not even our marrying wasn’t, you see.” There was a faint
6237: sad smile on Nancy’s face as she said the last words.
6238: 
6239: “I’m a worse man than you thought I was, Nancy,” said Godfrey, rather
6240: tremulously. “Can you forgive me ever?”
6241: 
6242: “The wrong to me is but little, Godfrey: you’ve made it up to me—you’ve
6243: been good to me for fifteen years. It’s another you did the wrong to;
6244: and I doubt it can never be all made up for.”
6245: 
6246: “But we can take Eppie now,” said Godfrey. “I won’t mind the world
6247: knowing at last. I’ll be plain and open for the rest o’ my life.”
6248: 
6249: “It’ll be different coming to us, now she’s grown up,” said Nancy,
6250: shaking her head sadly. “But it’s your duty to acknowledge her and
6251: provide for her; and I’ll do my part by her, and pray to God Almighty
6252: to make her love me.”
6253: 
6254: “Then we’ll go together to Silas Marner’s this very night, as soon as
6255: everything’s quiet at the Stone-pits.”
6256: 
6257: 
6258: 
6259: CHAPTER XIX.
6260: 
6261: Between eight and nine o’clock that evening, Eppie and Silas were
6262: seated alone in the cottage. After the great excitement the weaver had
6263: undergone from the events of the afternoon, he had felt a longing for
6264: this quietude, and had even begged Mrs. Winthrop and Aaron, who had
6265: naturally lingered behind every one else, to leave him alone with his
6266: child. The excitement had not passed away: it had only reached that
6267: stage when the keenness of the susceptibility makes external stimulus
6268: intolerable—when there is no sense of weariness, but rather an
6269: intensity of inward life, under which sleep is an impossibility. Any
6270: one who has watched such moments in other men remembers the brightness
6271: of the eyes and the strange definiteness that comes over coarse
6272: features from that transient influence. It is as if a new fineness of
6273: ear for all spiritual voices had sent wonder-working vibrations through
6274: the heavy mortal frame—as if “beauty born of murmuring sound” had
6275: passed into the face of the listener.
6276: 
6277: Silas’s face showed that sort of transfiguration, as he sat in his
6278: arm-chair and looked at Eppie. She had drawn her own chair towards his
6279: knees, and leaned forward, holding both his hands, while she looked up
6280: at him. On the table near them, lit by a candle, lay the recovered
6281: gold—the old long-loved gold, ranged in orderly heaps, as Silas used to
6282: range it in the days when it was his only joy. He had been telling her
6283: how he used to count it every night, and how his soul was utterly
6284: desolate till she was sent to him.
6285: 
6286: “At first, I’d a sort o’ feeling come across me now and then,” he was
6287: saying in a subdued tone, “as if you might be changed into the gold
6288: again; for sometimes, turn my head which way I would, I seemed to see
6289: the gold; and I thought I should be glad if I could feel it, and find
6290: it was come back. But that didn’t last long. After a bit, I should have
6291: thought it was a curse come again, if it had drove you from me, for I’d
6292: got to feel the need o’ your looks and your voice and the touch o’ your
6293: little fingers. You didn’t know then, Eppie, when you were such a
6294: little un—you didn’t know what your old father Silas felt for you.”
6295: 
6296: “But I know now, father,” said Eppie. “If it hadn’t been for you,
6297: they’d have taken me to the workhouse, and there’d have been nobody to
6298: love me.”
6299: 
6300: “Eh, my precious child, the blessing was mine. If you hadn’t been sent
6301: to save me, I should ha’ gone to the grave in my misery. The money was
6302: taken away from me in time; and you see it’s been kept—kept till it was
6303: wanted for you. It’s wonderful—our life is wonderful.”
6304: 
6305: Silas sat in silence a few minutes, looking at the money. “It takes no
6306: hold of me now,” he said, ponderingly—“the money doesn’t. I wonder if
6307: it ever could again—I doubt it might, if I lost you, Eppie. I might
6308: come to think I was forsaken again, and lose the feeling that God was
6309: good to me.”
6310: 
6311: At that moment there was a knocking at the door; and Eppie was obliged
6312: to rise without answering Silas. Beautiful she looked, with the
6313: tenderness of gathering tears in her eyes and a slight flush on her
6314: cheeks, as she stepped to open the door. The flush deepened when she
6315: saw Mr. and Mrs. Godfrey Cass. She made her little rustic curtsy, and
6316: held the door wide for them to enter.
6317: 
6318: “We’re disturbing you very late, my dear,” said Mrs. Cass, taking
6319: Eppie’s hand, and looking in her face with an expression of anxious
6320: interest and admiration. Nancy herself was pale and tremulous.
6321: 
6322: Eppie, after placing chairs for Mr. and Mrs. Cass, went to stand
6323: against Silas, opposite to them.
6324: 
6325: “Well, Marner,” said Godfrey, trying to speak with perfect firmness,
6326: “it’s a great comfort to me to see you with your money again, that
6327: you’ve been deprived of so many years. It was one of my family did you
6328: the wrong—the more grief to me—and I feel bound to make up to you for
6329: it in every way. Whatever I can do for you will be nothing but paying a
6330: debt, even if I looked no further than the robbery. But there are other
6331: things I’m beholden—shall be beholden to you for, Marner.”
6332: 
6333: Godfrey checked himself. It had been agreed between him and his wife
6334: that the subject of his fatherhood should be approached very carefully,
6335: and that, if possible, the disclosure should be reserved for the
6336: future, so that it might be made to Eppie gradually. Nancy had urged
6337: this, because she felt strongly the painful light in which Eppie must
6338: inevitably see the relation between her father and mother.
6339: 
6340: Silas, always ill at ease when he was being spoken to by “betters”,
6341: such as Mr. Cass—tall, powerful, florid men, seen chiefly on
6342: horseback—answered with some constraint—
6343: 
6344: “Sir, I’ve a deal to thank you for a’ready. As for the robbery, I count
6345: it no loss to me. And if I did, you couldn’t help it: you aren’t
6346: answerable for it.”
6347: 
6348: “You may look at it in that way, Marner, but I never can; and I hope
6349: you’ll let me act according to my own feeling of what’s just. I know
6350: you’re easily contented: you’ve been a hard-working man all your life.”
6351: 
6352: “Yes, sir, yes,” said Marner, meditatively. “I should ha’ been bad off
6353: without my work: it was what I held by when everything else was gone
6354: from me.”
6355: 
6356: “Ah,” said Godfrey, applying Marner’s words simply to his bodily wants,
6357: “it was a good trade for you in this country, because there’s been a
6358: great deal of linen-weaving to be done. But you’re getting rather past
6359: such close work, Marner: it’s time you laid by and had some rest. You
6360: look a good deal pulled down, though you’re not an old man, _are_ you?”
6361: 
6362: “Fifty-five, as near as I can say, sir,” said Silas.
6363: 
6364: “Oh, why, you may live thirty years longer—look at old Macey! And that
6365: money on the table, after all, is but little. It won’t go far either
6366: way—whether it’s put out to interest, or you were to live on it as long
6367: as it would last: it wouldn’t go far if you’d nobody to keep but
6368: yourself, and you’ve had two to keep for a good many years now.”
6369: 
6370: “Eh, sir,” said Silas, unaffected by anything Godfrey was saying, “I’m
6371: in no fear o’ want. We shall do very well—Eppie and me ’ull do well
6372: enough. There’s few working-folks have got so much laid by as that. I
6373: don’t know what it is to gentlefolks, but I look upon it as a
6374: deal—almost too much. And as for us, it’s little we want.”
6375: 
6376: “Only the garden, father,” said Eppie, blushing up to the ears the
6377: moment after.
6378: 
6379: “You love a garden, do you, my dear?” said Nancy, thinking that this
6380: turn in the point of view might help her husband. “We should agree in
6381: that: I give a deal of time to the garden.”
6382: 
6383: “Ah, there’s plenty of gardening at the Red House,” said Godfrey,
6384: surprised at the difficulty he found in approaching a proposition which
6385: had seemed so easy to him in the distance. “You’ve done a good part by
6386: Eppie, Marner, for sixteen years. It ’ud be a great comfort to you to
6387: see her well provided for, wouldn’t it? She looks blooming and healthy,
6388: but not fit for any hardships: she doesn’t look like a strapping girl
6389: come of working parents. You’d like to see her taken care of by those
6390: who can leave her well off, and make a lady of her; she’s more fit for
6391: it than for a rough life, such as she might come to have in a few
6392: years’ time.”
6393: 
6394: A slight flush came over Marner’s face, and disappeared, like a passing
6395: gleam. Eppie was simply wondering Mr. Cass should talk so about things
6396: that seemed to have nothing to do with reality; but Silas was hurt and
6397: uneasy.
6398: 
6399: “I don’t take your meaning, sir,” he answered, not having words at
6400: command to express the mingled feelings with which he had heard Mr.
6401: Cass’s words.
6402: 
6403: “Well, my meaning is this, Marner,” said Godfrey, determined to come to
