6404: the point. “Mrs. Cass and I, you know, have no children—nobody to
6405: benefit by our good home and everything else we have—more than enough
6406: for ourselves. And we should like to have somebody in the place of a
6407: daughter to us—we should like to have Eppie, and treat her in every way
6408: as our own child. It ’ud be a great comfort to you in your old age, I
6409: hope, to see her fortune made in that way, after you’ve been at the
6410: trouble of bringing her up so well. And it’s right you should have
6411: every reward for that. And Eppie, I’m sure, will always love you and be
6412: grateful to you: she’d come and see you very often, and we should all
6413: be on the look-out to do everything we could towards making you
6414: comfortable.”
6415: 
6416: A plain man like Godfrey Cass, speaking under some embarrassment,
6417: necessarily blunders on words that are coarser than his intentions, and
6418: that are likely to fall gratingly on susceptible feelings. While he had
6419: been speaking, Eppie had quietly passed her arm behind Silas’s head,
6420: and let her hand rest against it caressingly: she felt him trembling
6421: violently. He was silent for some moments when Mr. Cass had
6422: ended—powerless under the conflict of emotions, all alike painful.
6423: Eppie’s heart was swelling at the sense that her father was in
6424: distress; and she was just going to lean down and speak to him, when
6425: one struggling dread at last gained the mastery over every other in
6426: Silas, and he said, faintly—
6427: 
6428: “Eppie, my child, speak. I won’t stand in your way. Thank Mr. and Mrs.
6429: Cass.”
6430: 
6431: Eppie took her hand from her father’s head, and came forward a step.
6432: Her cheeks were flushed, but not with shyness this time: the sense that
6433: her father was in doubt and suffering banished that sort of
6434: self-consciousness. She dropped a low curtsy, first to Mrs. Cass and
6435: then to Mr. Cass, and said—
6436: 
6437: “Thank you, ma’am—thank you, sir. But I can’t leave my father, nor own
6438: anybody nearer than him. And I don’t want to be a lady—thank you all
6439: the same” (here Eppie dropped another curtsy). “I couldn’t give up the
6440: folks I’ve been used to.”
6441: 
6442: Eppie’s lips began to tremble a little at the last words. She retreated
6443: to her father’s chair again, and held him round the neck: while Silas,
6444: with a subdued sob, put up his hand to grasp hers.
6445: 
6446: The tears were in Nancy’s eyes, but her sympathy with Eppie was,
6447: naturally, divided with distress on her husband’s account. She dared
6448: not speak, wondering what was going on in her husband’s mind.
6449: 
6450: Godfrey felt an irritation inevitable to almost all of us when we
6451: encounter an unexpected obstacle. He had been full of his own penitence
6452: and resolution to retrieve his error as far as the time was left to
6453: him; he was possessed with all-important feelings, that were to lead to
6454: a predetermined course of action which he had fixed on as the right,
6455: and he was not prepared to enter with lively appreciation into other
6456: people’s feelings counteracting his virtuous resolves. The agitation
6457: with which he spoke again was not quite unmixed with anger.
6458: 
6459: “But I’ve a claim on you, Eppie—the strongest of all claims. It’s my
6460: duty, Marner, to own Eppie as my child, and provide for her. She is my
6461: own child—her mother was my wife. I’ve a natural claim on her that must
6462: stand before every other.”
6463: 
6464: Eppie had given a violent start, and turned quite pale. Silas, on the
6465: contrary, who had been relieved, by Eppie’s answer, from the dread lest
6466: his mind should be in opposition to hers, felt the spirit of resistance
6467: in him set free, not without a touch of parental fierceness. “Then,
6468: sir,” he answered, with an accent of bitterness that had been silent in
6469: him since the memorable day when his youthful hope had perished—“then,
6470: sir, why didn’t you say so sixteen year ago, and claim her before I’d
6471: come to love her, i’stead o’ coming to take her from me now, when you
6472: might as well take the heart out o’ my body? God gave her to me because
6473: you turned your back upon her, and He looks upon her as mine: you’ve no
6474: right to her! When a man turns a blessing from his door, it falls to
6475: them as take it in.”
6476: 
6477: “I know that, Marner. I was wrong. I’ve repented of my conduct in that
6478: matter,” said Godfrey, who could not help feeling the edge of Silas’s
6479: words.
6480: 
6481: “I’m glad to hear it, sir,” said Marner, with gathering excitement;
6482: “but repentance doesn’t alter what’s been going on for sixteen year.
6483: Your coming now and saying “I’m her father” doesn’t alter the feelings
6484: inside us. It’s me she’s been calling her father ever since she could
6485: say the word.”
6486: 
6487: “But I think you might look at the thing more reasonably, Marner,” said
6488: Godfrey, unexpectedly awed by the weaver’s direct truth-speaking. “It
6489: isn’t as if she was to be taken quite away from you, so that you’d
6490: never see her again. She’ll be very near you, and come to see you very
6491: often. She’ll feel just the same towards you.”
6492: 
6493: “Just the same?” said Marner, more bitterly than ever. “How’ll she feel
6494: just the same for me as she does now, when we eat o’ the same bit, and
6495: drink o’ the same cup, and think o’ the same things from one day’s end
6496: to another? Just the same? that’s idle talk. You’d cut us i’ two.”
6497: 
6498: Godfrey, unqualified by experience to discern the pregnancy of Marner’s
6499: simple words, felt rather angry again. It seemed to him that the weaver
6500: was very selfish (a judgment readily passed by those who have never
6501: tested their own power of sacrifice) to oppose what was undoubtedly for
6502: Eppie’s welfare; and he felt himself called upon, for her sake, to
6503: assert his authority.
6504: 
6505: “I should have thought, Marner,” he said, severely—“I should have
6506: thought your affection for Eppie would make you rejoice in what was for
6507: her good, even if it did call upon you to give up something. You ought
6508: to remember your own life’s uncertain, and she’s at an age now when her
6509: lot may soon be fixed in a way very different from what it would be in
6510: her father’s home: she may marry some low working-man, and then,
6511: whatever I might do for her, I couldn’t make her well-off. You’re
6512: putting yourself in the way of her welfare; and though I’m sorry to
6513: hurt you after what you’ve done, and what I’ve left undone, I feel now
6514: it’s my duty to insist on taking care of my own daughter. I want to do
6515: my duty.”
6516: 
6517: It would be difficult to say whether it were Silas or Eppie that was
6518: more deeply stirred by this last speech of Godfrey’s. Thought had been
6519: very busy in Eppie as she listened to the contest between her old
6520: long-loved father and this new unfamiliar father who had suddenly come
6521: to fill the place of that black featureless shadow which had held the
6522: ring and placed it on her mother’s finger. Her imagination had darted
6523: backward in conjectures, and forward in previsions, of what this
6524: revealed fatherhood implied; and there were words in Godfrey’s last
6525: speech which helped to make the previsions especially definite. Not
6526: that these thoughts, either of past or future, determined her
6527: resolution—_that_ was determined by the feelings which vibrated to
6528: every word Silas had uttered; but they raised, even apart from these
6529: feelings, a repulsion towards the offered lot and the newly-revealed
6530: father.
6531: 
6532: Silas, on the other hand, was again stricken in conscience, and alarmed
6533: lest Godfrey’s accusation should be true—lest he should be raising his
6534: own will as an obstacle to Eppie’s good. For many moments he was mute,
6535: struggling for the self-conquest necessary to the uttering of the
6536: difficult words. They came out tremulously.
6537: 
6538: “I’ll say no more. Let it be as you will. Speak to the child. I’ll
6539: hinder nothing.”
6540: 
6541: Even Nancy, with all the acute sensibility of her own affections,
6542: shared her husband’s view, that Marner was not justifiable in his wish
6543: to retain Eppie, after her real father had avowed himself. She felt
6544: that it was a very hard trial for the poor weaver, but her code allowed
6545: no question that a father by blood must have a claim above that of any
6546: foster-father. Besides, Nancy, used all her life to plenteous
6547: circumstances and the privileges of “respectability”, could not enter
6548: into the pleasures which early nurture and habit connect with all the
6549: little aims and efforts of the poor who are born poor: to her mind,
6550: Eppie, in being restored to her birthright, was entering on a too long
6551: withheld but unquestionable good. Hence she heard Silas’s last words
6552: with relief, and thought, as Godfrey did, that their wish was achieved.
6553: 
6554: “Eppie, my dear,” said Godfrey, looking at his daughter, not without
6555: some embarrassment, under the sense that she was old enough to judge
6556: him, “it’ll always be our wish that you should show your love and
6557: gratitude to one who’s been a father to you so many years, and we shall
6558: want to help you to make him comfortable in every way. But we hope
6559: you’ll come to love us as well; and though I haven’t been what a father
6560: should ha’ been to you all these years, I wish to do the utmost in my
6561: power for you for the rest of my life, and provide for you as my only
6562: child. And you’ll have the best of mothers in my wife—that’ll be a
6563: blessing you haven’t known since you were old enough to know it.”
6564: 
6565: “My dear, you’ll be a treasure to me,” said Nancy, in her gentle voice.
6566: “We shall want for nothing when we have our daughter.”
6567: 
6568: Eppie did not come forward and curtsy, as she had done before. She held
6569: Silas’s hand in hers, and grasped it firmly—it was a weaver’s hand,
6570: with a palm and finger-tips that were sensitive to such pressure—while
6571: she spoke with colder decision than before.
6572: 
6573: “Thank you, ma’am—thank you, sir, for your offers—they’re very great,
6574: and far above my wish. For I should have no delight i’ life any more if
6575: I was forced to go away from my father, and knew he was sitting at
6576: home, a-thinking of me and feeling lone. We’ve been used to be happy
6577: together every day, and I can’t think o’ no happiness without him. And
6578: he says he’d nobody i’ the world till I was sent to him, and he’d have
6579: nothing when I was gone. And he’s took care of me and loved me from the
6580: first, and I’ll cleave to him as long as he lives, and nobody shall
6581: ever come between him and me.”
6582: 
6583: “But you must make sure, Eppie,” said Silas, in a low voice—“you must
6584: make sure as you won’t ever be sorry, because you’ve made your choice
6585: to stay among poor folks, and with poor clothes and things, when you
6586: might ha’ had everything o’ the best.”
6587: 
6588: His sensitiveness on this point had increased as he listened to Eppie’s
6589: words of faithful affection.
6590: 
6591: “I can never be sorry, father,” said Eppie. “I shouldn’t know what to
6592: think on or to wish for with fine things about me, as I haven’t been
6593: used to. And it ’ud be poor work for me to put on things, and ride in a
6594: gig, and sit in a place at church, as ’ud make them as I’m fond of
6595: think me unfitting company for ’em. What could _I_ care for then?”
6596: 
6597: Nancy looked at Godfrey with a pained questioning glance. But his eyes
6598: were fixed on the floor, where he was moving the end of his stick, as
6599: if he were pondering on something absently. She thought there was a
6600: word which might perhaps come better from her lips than from his.
6601: 
6602: “What you say is natural, my dear child—it’s natural you should cling
6603: to those who’ve brought you up,” she said, mildly; “but there’s a duty
6604: you owe to your lawful father. There’s perhaps something to be given up
6605: on more sides than one. When your father opens his home to you, I think
6606: it’s right you shouldn’t turn your back on it.”
6607: 
6608: “I can’t feel as I’ve got any father but one,” said Eppie, impetuously,
6609: while the tears gathered. “I’ve always thought of a little home where
6610: he’d sit i’ the corner, and I should fend and do everything for him: I
6611: can’t think o’ no other home. I wasn’t brought up to be a lady, and I
6612: can’t turn my mind to it. I like the working-folks, and their victuals,
6613: and their ways. And,” she ended passionately, while the tears fell,
6614: “I’m promised to marry a working-man, as’ll live with father, and help
6615: me to take care of him.”
6616: 
6617: Godfrey looked up at Nancy with a flushed face and smarting dilated
6618: eyes. This frustration of a purpose towards which he had set out under
6619: the exalted consciousness that he was about to compensate in some
6620: degree for the greatest demerit of his life, made him feel the air of
6621: the room stifling.
6622: 
6623: “Let us go,” he said, in an under-tone.
6624: 
6625: “We won’t talk of this any longer now,” said Nancy, rising. “We’re your
6626: well-wishers, my dear—and yours too, Marner. We shall come and see you
6627: again. It’s getting late now.”
6628: 
6629: In this way she covered her husband’s abrupt departure, for Godfrey had
6630: gone straight to the door, unable to say more.
6631: 
6632: 
6633: 
6634: CHAPTER XX.
6635: 
6636: Nancy and Godfrey walked home under the starlight in silence. When they
6637: entered the oaken parlour, Godfrey threw himself into his chair, while
6638: Nancy laid down her bonnet and shawl, and stood on the hearth near her
6639: husband, unwilling to leave him even for a few minutes, and yet fearing
6640: to utter any word lest it might jar on his feeling. At last Godfrey
6641: turned his head towards her, and their eyes met, dwelling in that
6642: meeting without any movement on either side. That quiet mutual gaze of
6643: a trusting husband and wife is like the first moment of rest or refuge
6644: from a great weariness or a great danger—not to be interfered with by
6645: speech or action which would distract the sensations from the fresh
6646: enjoyment of repose.
6647: 
6648: But presently he put out his hand, and as Nancy placed hers within it,
6649: he drew her towards him, and said—
6650: 
6651: “That’s ended!”
6652: 
6653: She bent to kiss him, and then said, as she stood by his side, “Yes,
6654: I’m afraid we must give up the hope of having her for a daughter. It
6655: wouldn’t be right to want to force her to come to us against her will.
6656: We can’t alter her bringing up and what’s come of it.”
6657: 
6658: “No,” said Godfrey, with a keen decisiveness of tone, in contrast with
6659: his usually careless and unemphatic speech—“there’s debts we can’t pay
6660: like money debts, by paying extra for the years that have slipped by.
6661: While I’ve been putting off and putting off, the trees have been
6662: growing—it’s too late now. Marner was in the right in what he said
6663: about a man’s turning away a blessing from his door: it falls to
6664: somebody else. I wanted to pass for childless once, Nancy—I shall pass
6665: for childless now against my wish.”
6666: 
6667: Nancy did not speak immediately, but after a little while she
6668: asked—“You won’t make it known, then, about Eppie’s being your
6669: daughter?”
6670: 
6671: “No: where would be the good to anybody?—only harm. I must do what I
6672: can for her in the state of life she chooses. I must see who it is
6673: she’s thinking of marrying.”
6674: 
6675: “If it won’t do any good to make the thing known,” said Nancy, who
6676: thought she might now allow herself the relief of entertaining a
6677: feeling which she had tried to silence before, “I should be very
6678: thankful for father and Priscilla never to be troubled with knowing
6679: what was done in the past, more than about Dunsey: it can’t be helped,
6680: their knowing that.”
6681: 
6682: “I shall put it in my will—I think I shall put it in my will. I
6683: shouldn’t like to leave anything to be found out, like this of Dunsey,”
6684: said Godfrey, meditatively. “But I can’t see anything but difficulties
6685: that ’ud come from telling it now. I must do what I can to make her
6686: happy in her own way. I’ve a notion,” he added, after a moment’s pause,
6687: “it’s Aaron Winthrop she meant she was engaged to. I remember seeing
6688: him with her and Marner going away from church.”
6689: 
6690: “Well, he’s very sober and industrious,” said Nancy, trying to view the
6691: matter as cheerfully as possible.
6692: 
6693: Godfrey fell into thoughtfulness again. Presently he looked up at Nancy
6694: sorrowfully, and said—
6695: 
6696: “She’s a very pretty, nice girl, isn’t she, Nancy?”
6697: 
6698: “Yes, dear; and with just your hair and eyes: I wondered it had never
6699: struck me before.”
6700: 
6701: “I think she took a dislike to me at the thought of my being her
6702: father: I could see a change in her manner after that.”
6703: 
6704: “She couldn’t bear to think of not looking on Marner as her father,”
6705: said Nancy, not wishing to confirm her husband’s painful impression.
6706: 
6707: “She thinks I did wrong by her mother as well as by her. She thinks me
6708: worse than I am. But she _must_ think it: she can never know all. It’s
6709: part of my punishment, Nancy, for my daughter to dislike me. I should
6710: never have got into that trouble if I’d been true to you—if I hadn’t
6711: been a fool. I’d no right to expect anything but evil could come of
6712: that marriage—and when I shirked doing a father’s part too.”
6713: 
6714: Nancy was silent: her spirit of rectitude would not let her try to
6715: soften the edge of what she felt to be a just compunction. He spoke
6716: again after a little while, but the tone was rather changed: there was
6717: tenderness mingled with the previous self-reproach.
6718: 
6719: “And I got _you_, Nancy, in spite of all; and yet I’ve been grumbling
6720: and uneasy because I hadn’t something else—as if I deserved it.”
6721: 
6722: “You’ve never been wanting to me, Godfrey,” said Nancy, with quiet
6723: sincerity. “My only trouble would be gone if you resigned yourself to
6724: the lot that’s been given us.”
6725: 
6726: “Well, perhaps it isn’t too late to mend a bit there. Though it _is_
6727: too late to mend some things, say what they will.”
6728: 
6729: 
6730: 
6731: CHAPTER XXI.
6732: 
6733: The next morning, when Silas and Eppie were seated at their breakfast,
6734: he said to her—
6735: 
6736: “Eppie, there’s a thing I’ve had on my mind to do this two year, and
6737: now the money’s been brought back to us, we can do it. I’ve been
6738: turning it over and over in the night, and I think we’ll set out
6739: to-morrow, while the fine days last. We’ll leave the house and
6740: everything for your godmother to take care on, and we’ll make a little
6741: bundle o’ things and set out.”
6742: 
6743: “Where to go, daddy?” said Eppie, in much surprise.
6744: 
6745: “To my old country—to the town where I was born—up Lantern Yard. I want
6746: to see Mr. Paston, the minister: something may ha’ come out to make ’em
6747: know I was innicent o’ the robbery. And Mr. Paston was a man with a
6748: deal o’ light—I want to speak to him about the drawing o’ the lots. And
6749: I should like to talk to him about the religion o’ this country-side,
6750: for I partly think he doesn’t know on it.”
6751: 
6752: Eppie was very joyful, for there was the prospect not only of wonder
6753: and delight at seeing a strange country, but also of coming back to
6754: tell Aaron all about it. Aaron was so much wiser than she was about
6755: most things—it would be rather pleasant to have this little advantage
6756: over him. Mrs. Winthrop, though possessed with a dim fear of dangers
6757: attendant on so long a journey, and requiring many assurances that it
6758: would not take them out of the region of carriers’ carts and slow
6759: waggons, was nevertheless well pleased that Silas should revisit his
6760: own country, and find out if he had been cleared from that false
6761: accusation.
6762: 
6763: “You’d be easier in your mind for the rest o’ your life, Master
6764: Marner,” said Dolly—“that you would. And if there’s any light to be got
6765: up the yard as you talk on, we’ve need of it i’ this world, and I’d be
6766: glad on it myself, if you could bring it back.”
6767: 
6768: So on the fourth day from that time, Silas and Eppie, in their Sunday
6769: clothes, with a small bundle tied in a blue linen handkerchief, were
6770: making their way through the streets of a great manufacturing town.
6771: Silas, bewildered by the changes thirty years had brought over his
6772: native place, had stopped several persons in succession to ask them the
6773: name of this town, that he might be sure he was not under a mistake
6774: about it.
6775: 
6776: “Ask for Lantern Yard, father—ask this gentleman with the tassels on
6777: his shoulders a-standing at the shop door; he isn’t in a hurry like the
6778: rest,” said Eppie, in some distress at her father’s bewilderment, and
6779: ill at ease, besides, amidst the noise, the movement, and the multitude
6780: of strange indifferent faces.
6781: 
6782: “Eh, my child, he won’t know anything about it,” said Silas;
6783: “gentlefolks didn’t ever go up the Yard. But happen somebody can tell
6784: me which is the way to Prison Street, where the jail is. I know the way
6785: out o’ that as if I’d seen it yesterday.”
6786: 
6787: With some difficulty, after many turnings and new inquiries, they
6788: reached Prison Street; and the grim walls of the jail, the first object
6789: that answered to any image in Silas’s memory, cheered him with the
6790: certitude, which no assurance of the town’s name had hitherto given
6791: him, that he was in his native place.
6792: 
6793: “Ah,” he said, drawing a long breath, “there’s the jail, Eppie; that’s
6794: just the same: I aren’t afraid now. It’s the third turning on the left
6795: hand from the jail doors—that’s the way we must go.”
6796: 
6797: “Oh, what a dark ugly place!” said Eppie. “How it hides the sky! It’s
6798: worse than the Workhouse. I’m glad you don’t live in this town now,
6799: father. Is Lantern Yard like this street?”
6800: 
6801: “My precious child,” said Silas, smiling, “it isn’t a big street like
6802: this. I never was easy i’ this street myself, but I was fond o’ Lantern
6803: Yard. The shops here are all altered, I think—I can’t make ’em out; but
6804: I shall know the turning, because it’s the third.”
6805: 
6806: “Here it is,” he said, in a tone of satisfaction, as they came to a
6807: narrow alley. “And then we must go to the left again, and then straight
6808: for’ard for a bit, up Shoe Lane: and then we shall be at the entry next
6809: to the o’erhanging window, where there’s the nick in the road for the
6810: water to run. Eh, I can see it all.”
6811: 
6812: “O father, I’m like as if I was stifled,” said Eppie. “I couldn’t ha’
6813: thought as any folks lived i’ this way, so close together. How pretty
6814: the Stone-pits ’ull look when we get back!”
6815: 
6816: “It looks comical to _me_, child, now—and smells bad. I can’t think as
6817: it usened to smell so.”
6818: 
6819: Here and there a sallow, begrimed face looked out from a gloomy doorway
6820: at the strangers, and increased Eppie’s uneasiness, so that it was a
6821: longed-for relief when they issued from the alleys into Shoe Lane,
6822: where there was a broader strip of sky.
6823: 
6824: “Dear heart!” said Silas, “why, there’s people coming out o’ the Yard
6825: as if they’d been to chapel at this time o’ day—a weekday noon!”
6826: 
6827: Suddenly he started and stood still with a look of distressed
6828: amazement, that alarmed Eppie. They were before an opening in front of
6829: a large factory, from which men and women were streaming for their
6830: midday meal.
6831: 
6832: “Father,” said Eppie, clasping his arm, “what’s the matter?”
6833: 
6834: But she had to speak again and again before Silas could answer her.
6835: 
6836: “It’s gone, child,” he said, at last, in strong agitation—“Lantern
6837: Yard’s gone. It must ha’ been here, because here’s the house with the
6838: o’erhanging window—I know that—it’s just the same; but they’ve made
6839: this new opening; and see that big factory! It’s all gone—chapel and
6840: all.”
6841: 
6842: “Come into that little brush-shop and sit down, father—they’ll let you
6843: sit down,” said Eppie, always on the watch lest one of her father’s
6844: strange attacks should come on. “Perhaps the people can tell you all
6845: about it.”
6846: 
6847: But neither from the brush-maker, who had come to Shoe Lane only ten
6848: years ago, when the factory was already built, nor from any other
6849: source within his reach, could Silas learn anything of the old Lantern
6850: Yard friends, or of Mr. Paston the minister.
6851: 
6852: “The old place is all swep’ away,” Silas said to Dolly Winthrop on the
6853: night of his return—“the little graveyard and everything. The old
6854: home’s gone; I’ve no home but this now. I shall never know whether they
6855: got at the truth o’ the robbery, nor whether Mr. Paston could ha’ given
6856: me any light about the drawing o’ the lots. It’s dark to me, Mrs.
6857: Winthrop, that is; I doubt it’ll be dark to the last.”
6858: 
6859: “Well, yes, Master Marner,” said Dolly, who sat with a placid listening
6860: face, now bordered by grey hairs; “I doubt it may. It’s the will o’
6861: Them above as a many things should be dark to us; but there’s some
6862: things as I’ve never felt i’ the dark about, and they’re mostly what
6863: comes i’ the day’s work. You were hard done by that once, Master
6864: Marner, and it seems as you’ll never know the rights of it; but that
6865: doesn’t hinder there _being_ a rights, Master Marner, for all it’s dark
6866: to you and me.”
6867: 
6868: “No,” said Silas, “no; that doesn’t hinder. Since the time the child
6869: was sent to me and I’ve come to love her as myself, I’ve had light
6870: enough to trusten by; and now she says she’ll never leave me, I think I
6871: shall trusten till I die.”
6872: 
6873: 
6874: 
6875: CONCLUSION
6876: 
6877: There was one time of the year which was held in Raveloe to be
6878: especially suitable for a wedding. It was when the great lilacs and
6879: laburnums in the old-fashioned gardens showed their golden and purple
6880: wealth above the lichen-tinted walls, and when there were calves still
6881: young enough to want bucketfuls of fragrant milk. People were not so
6882: busy then as they must become when the full cheese-making and the
6883: mowing had set in; and besides, it was a time when a light bridal dress
6884: could be worn with comfort and seen to advantage.
6885: 
6886: Happily the sunshine fell more warmly than usual on the lilac tufts the
6887: morning that Eppie was married, for her dress was a very light one. She
6888: had often thought, though with a feeling of renunciation, that the
6889: perfection of a wedding-dress would be a white cotton, with the tiniest
6890: pink sprig at wide intervals; so that when Mrs. Godfrey Cass begged to
6891: provide one, and asked Eppie to choose what it should be, previous
6892: meditation had enabled her to give a decided answer at once.
6893: 
6894: Seen at a little distance as she walked across the churchyard and down
6895: the village, she seemed to be attired in pure white, and her hair
6896: looked like the dash of gold on a lily. One hand was on her husband’s
6897: arm, and with the other she clasped the hand of her father Silas.
6898: 
6899: “You won’t be giving me away, father,” she had said before they went to
6900: church; “you’ll only be taking Aaron to be a son to you.”
6901: 
6902: Dolly Winthrop walked behind with her husband; and there ended the
6903: little bridal procession.
6904: 
6905: There were many eyes to look at it, and Miss Priscilla Lammeter was
6906: glad that she and her father had happened to drive up to the door of
6907: the Red House just in time to see this pretty sight. They had come to
6908: keep Nancy company to-day, because Mr. Cass had had to go away to
6909: Lytherley, for special reasons. That seemed to be a pity, for otherwise
6910: he might have gone, as Mr. Crackenthorp and Mr. Osgood certainly would,
6911: to look on at the wedding-feast which he had ordered at the Rainbow,
6912: naturally feeling a great interest in the weaver who had been wronged
6913: by one of his own family.
6914: 
6915: “I could ha’ wished Nancy had had the luck to find a child like that
6916: and bring her up,” said Priscilla to her father, as they sat in the
6917: gig; “I should ha’ had something young to think of then, besides the
6918: lambs and the calves.”
6919: 
6920: “Yes, my dear, yes,” said Mr. Lammeter; “one feels that as one gets
6921: older. Things look dim to old folks: they’d need have some young eyes
6922: about ’em, to let ’em know the world’s the same as it used to be.”
6923: 
6924: Nancy came out now to welcome her father and sister; and the wedding
6925: group had passed on beyond the Red House to the humbler part of the
6926: village.
6927: 
6928: Dolly Winthrop was the first to divine that old Mr. Macey, who had been
6929: set in his arm-chair outside his own door, would expect some special
6930: notice as they passed, since he was too old to be at the wedding-feast.
6931: 
6932: “Mr. Macey’s looking for a word from us,” said Dolly; “he’ll be hurt if
6933: we pass him and say nothing—and him so racked with rheumatiz.”
6934: 
6935: So they turned aside to shake hands with the old man. He had looked
6936: forward to the occasion, and had his premeditated speech.
6937: 
6938: “Well, Master Marner,” he said, in a voice that quavered a good deal,
6939: “I’ve lived to see my words come true. I was the first to say there was
6940: no harm in you, though your looks might be again’ you; and I was the
6941: first to say you’d get your money back. And it’s nothing but rightful
6942: as you should. And I’d ha’ said the “Amens”, and willing, at the holy
6943: matrimony; but Tookey’s done it a good while now, and I hope you’ll
6944: have none the worse luck.”
6945: 
6946: In the open yard before the Rainbow the party of guests were already
6947: assembled, though it was still nearly an hour before the appointed
6948: feast time. But by this means they could not only enjoy the slow advent
6949: of their pleasure; they had also ample leisure to talk of Silas
6950: Marner’s strange history, and arrive by due degrees at the conclusion
6951: that he had brought a blessing on himself by acting like a father to a
6952: lone motherless child. Even the farrier did not negative this
6953: sentiment: on the contrary, he took it up as peculiarly his own, and
6954: invited any hardy person present to contradict him. But he met with no
6955: contradiction; and all differences among the company were merged in a
6956: general agreement with Mr. Snell’s sentiment, that when a man had
6957: deserved his good luck, it was the part of his neighbours to wish him
6958: joy.
6959: 
6960: As the bridal group approached, a hearty cheer was raised in the
6961: Rainbow yard; and Ben Winthrop, whose jokes had retained their
6962: acceptable flavour, found it agreeable to turn in there and receive
6963: congratulations; not requiring the proposed interval of quiet at the
6964: Stone-pits before joining the company.
6965: 
6966: Eppie had a larger garden than she had ever expected there now; and in
6967: other ways there had been alterations at the expense of Mr. Cass, the
6968: landlord, to suit Silas’s larger family. For he and Eppie had declared
6969: that they would rather stay at the Stone-pits than go to any new home.
6970: The garden was fenced with stones on two sides, but in front there was
6971: an open fence, through which the flowers shone with answering gladness,
6972: as the four united people came within sight of them.
6973: 
6974: “O father,” said Eppie, “what a pretty home ours is! I think nobody
6975: could be happier than we are.”
